The Bulletin

[Saturday, November 17, 1894.

“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal
to the price stated, hooks in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address ;
American Authors.
One does not forget Cooper and Hawthorne, Bret Harte and Howells, in
wondering when America is going to produce a first-rank novelist. Poets she has—
essayists, scientists, orators, historians—who may compare with all but the highest
names in old-world literature; but her Thackeray, her Balzac, her Tolstoi, are still
to seek. There seems to be something lacking in the American brain or the Ameri-
can environment, though with such a mingling of rich blood, such a variety of
climate, and such a teeming panorama of effort and incident, their chances would
appear rather in favor of a fuller, stronger intellectual life than is found else-
where. So far, American culture tends to width rather than depth.
Interest in books and writers is more widely diffused in America than in
England, for example; and the humblest American newspapers exhibit a literary
agility and originality which, however ridiculous in extremes, are yet preferable to
the comatose parochialism of most English journals outside the centres of population.
But the power of specialisation and concentration seems wanting to American
writers of fiction. They have no back to their heads. They soar high for short
flights, but lack the strength of pinion for sustained struggle. Conceive what memor-
able fruit might be borne were the method of Zola applied in a study of American
life and character—its miseries, its splendours, its industrial strifes, its ferocious
whirl of politics, its stern subjugation of natural forces ! But where is the American-
Zola —gifted not only with the mind to plan such a task, but also with the tenacity
and industry to complete it ? West, east, centre, north and south, one calls vainly
for an answer. Yet American fictionists have their field of special merit, though it
be not on the highest plane of genius. They are beautiful bellettrists, masters of the
short story, which they have lifted to a pitch of dainty delightfulness only reached in
France beside. Their critical novelists of the neo-realistic school, like Howell and J ames,
if refined to the point of weakness, are invariably graceful and witty. Most of the
authors collected below are artists in words whose style has the delicate savour of a
pothouse flower, and who treat their themes with the sympathy and imagination of
a poet.
This series (David Douglas’ series of American authors) is charmingly printed;
and the books are small and compact, suitable for the pocket. Posted at Is. 3d.
each volume, paper covers ; or cloth extra, 2s. 3d.
By W. D. HOWELLS.
“ The Shadow of a Dream.”
“ A Foregone Conclusion.”
“A Chance Acquaintance.”
“ Their Wedding Journey.”
“ A Counterfeit Presentment.”
“ The Lady of the Aroostook.” 2 vols.
“ Out of the Question.”
“ The Undiscovered Country.” 2 vols.
“ A Fearful Responsibility.”
“ Venetian Life.” 2 vols.
“ Italian Journeys.” 2 vols.
“ The Rise of Silas Lapham.” 2 vols.
“Indian Summer.” 2 vols.
“ An Imperative Duty.”
Perhaps the characteristic modern American novels.
By OLIVER WENDELL HOLMES.
“ The Autocrat of the Breakfast Table.”
2 vols.
“ The Poet at the Breakfast Table.”
2 vols.
“ The Professor at the Breakfast Table.” 2 vols.
By JOHN BURROUGHS.
“ Winter Sunshine.”
“ Locusts and Wild Honey.”
“ Wake-Robin.”
“ Fresh Fields.”
“ Birds and Poets.”
“ Pepacton.”
Pleasant essays in the style of Thoreau, giving the unaffected thoughts of a
literary naturalist.
By F. R. STOCKTON.
« Rudder Grange.”
“ The Lady or the Tiger.*
“ A Borrowed Month,”
“ Rudder Grange ” is a marvel of fun, and in “ The Lady or the Tiger ” and
« A Borrowed Month” are some of the most tantalisingly humorous stories in English.
By GEO. W. CABLE.
“ Madame Delphine.”
Charming stories.
“ Old Creole Days.”
By T. B. ALDRICH.
“ Prudence Palfrey.”
“ The Stillwater Tragedy,” 2 vols.
“ The Queen of Sheba.”
“ Marjorie Daw.”
“ Wyndham Towers.”
Mr. Aldrich is one of the ablest of the cultured coterie of American journalist-
litterateurs, and his books are all clever and attractive.
By M. E. WILKINS.
“ A Humble Romance..” | “ A Far-Away Melody.”

Mining* Books.
A PRACTICAL GUIDE TO PROSPECTORS, EXPLORERS, AND MIXERS
by C. W. Moore. Cloth, 15s. 9d.
This book is especially full and valuable in the matter of blowpipe analysis, and
many colored illustrations are added.
“ ELEMENTARY COAL-MIXING,” by R. Peel. Cloth, 2s. 9d.
An overseer’s handbook.
“THE PROSPECTOR’S FIELD-BOOK,” by F. S. Osborne. Cloth, Bs. 9d.
Endeavors to solve the prospector’s many riddles in simple language.
“THE HYDRAULIC GOLD-MIXER’S MANUAL,” by T. S. G. Kirkpatrick.
Cloth, 6s. 3d.
Not at all bad, with many illustrations; and specially successful because it
confines itself definitely to one branch of the subject.
“ A MANUAL OF MINING,” by M. C. Helsung. Cloth, 21s.
This is a comprehensive American treatise on engines and gear, mine-develop-
ment and working, suitable for managers who want to study up the question
thoroughly.
“THE MINER’S POCKET-BOOK,” by C. G. Lock. Leather, 13s.
“ THE MINER’S HANDBOOK,” by John Milne. Cloth, 7s. 9d.
Two very useful books for mine managers and students, with a wealth of
practical and tabular information. Lock’s is the fuller: but Milne is not to be
despised.
Pretty Prosers.
« YIRGINIBUS PUERISQUE,” by R. L. Stevenson. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
Essays now well-known, but as fresh as Spring, and perennially charming.
«MY LADY NICOTINE,” by J. M. Barrie. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
A book chief among ten thousand and altogether lovely. The smoker who has
not read it has not smoked.
« NOUGHTS AND CROSSES,” by A. T. Quillbr Couch. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
Surely the quaintest, sweetest stories ever penned, with art reaching its acme
in deliciously subtle simplicity.
“IN A CANADIAN CANOE,” by Barry Pain. Cloth, 4s.
Sketches, verses and stories varying in merit, but with keen thought and a
clever style at the back of all.
“ PROSE FANCIES,” by Richard Le Gallienne. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
A recent book of idyllic essays, throwing the glamour of imagination over the
desert of commonplace, and transmuting lead to gold by the alchemy of exquisite
prose. “ A Borrowed Sovereign ” should be read at least six times.
« POST-PRANDIAL PHILOSOPHY,” by Grant Allen. Cloth, 4s.
In these articles Grant Allen brings his scientific knowledge and scientific train*
ing to bear effectively on subjects of the day. He is invariably clear and suggestive,
and his tendencies make always for progress.

A Western coaching pioneer has jus* 5
died—Frank Whitney, of Cobb and Co., a
founder of the firm, who started life as a “ whip.’’
Jim Rutherford, Walter Hall, Bradley, and Frank
Whitney were for years the “Cobb and Co.”
When fortune smiled on the plucky Yankees,
Whitney purchased the beautiful old homestead
of tho Iceleys, at Coornbing Park, Carcoar, N.S. W.
Mr. Reeves, the M.L. Labor-Minister, has introduced an
Undesirable Immigrants Exclusion Bill, containing extra-
ordinary provisions, and proscribing all persons insane,
leprous, tuberculous, possessed of less than a specified
sum in cash, or worthy of 12 months’ gaol.
Too, too exclusive Reeves,
Each candid soul believes
The courage of conviction bears thee up
In thy rough coast’s defence
From those, no matter whence,
Who, sick or poor, evade the bitter cup.
Thy anti-Asian zeal
May work for common weal,
Tho’ all take service from the patient Chow :
’Gainst mania, crime and taint
Provide, as best, restraint;
But poverty—must that be branded now ?
Twere easy, with the rest,
To treat thy Bill as jest,
Its sorry schedules, ill-adapted means;
The critics waste their wit
On form and terms of it;
Its object none distrusts or contravenes.
This Bill, good Reeves, had stayed.
As wanting found when weighed,
Dibbs, Homer, Jesus, Epictetus, Popej
And grief that land awaits,
Which carves o’er wide sea-gates—
“ Ours and our children's: entrance never hope .”
A.E.W.
“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper,
** Tlx © 33 nlletln,”
Premier Australian 'Literary Journal ,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy,
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians. —The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System- one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as'one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless hews paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent soraf
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the olahns of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
oblonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.-

per Annum.
Business communications to be addressed to

all remittances are payable. Editorial communi-
aliens to be addressed to J. F. Archibald.

Tanko Creek Incident.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The parson came to Gungamine, which i* on
Yanko Creek,
With tidings of damnation for the sinners
gathered there,
The rouseabouts and shearers —and his game was,
so to speak,
To put the fear of Hell in them with preaching
and with prayer.
Maconochie, of Gungamine, a pious man was he,
He rounded up his station hands to hear the
parson spout.
Amongst them there were men who ne’er in prayer
had bended knee ;
But “ down upon your marrow-bones ” it was,
or else —“ get out!”
The parson preached, the parson prayed, his
prayers were deep and loud.
Maconochie at fitting times responsive groaned
“Amen!”
But silence like a gravestone lay upon the kneeling
crowd :
Some softly chewed tobacco and some spat
around —and then —
Maconochie, of Gungamine, rose swiftly from his
knees;
His face was red with righteous rage as he those
sinners scanned.
He shouted “You may go to H 1 your own
way if you please—
But, d n your eyes, you might have giv n
the blanky man a hand !”
Vic.

In Austral Light for Nov., Rev. J. J.
Malone reminds us in “ The Australian Poet:
Henry Kendall,” that neither Gordon nor Stephens
is Australian by birth. His keynote of criticism
is that “ Kendall is a poet who owes to Australia
everything except his poetry” :
No poet can claim to be a true interpreter of the natural
features of this primeval land who does not succeed in
transfusing the oppressive stillness and brooding melan-
choly of the lonely bush into his poetry. Kendall has
succeeded in doing that with more fidelity, with greater
vividness and power, than either Gordon or Brunton
Btephe;:3.
A Maoriland correspondent to The Bul-
letin
The stagnation here is a reactionary result of late com-
parative prosperity. Enterprise is dead, and shopkeepers
are inordinately querulous about credit. There are
many unemployed, but, as they are not centralised, the
outcry is not extreme. The depression is keen, all the
same. Large firms are discharging their hands by scores,
and men who have been securely placed for years are
eyeing their situations anxiously. The Ministry reap all
the blame, whether culpable or not. Premier Seddon
devoutly wishes for a Maoriland Coolgardie, and inclines
to a boom policy. He has lost much in popular esteem,
and, compared with Ballance, is an utter failure. Were
it not for Treas. Ward, the Ministry would be consistently
wild cat. Ward is the sheet-anchor, and is the only level-
head of the crowd.

A Maoriland (male) correspondent sends
the following soul-wail re Miss Nannie Harding,
the charming little actress, late of Sydney
Lyceum:—
You sav : “ Miss Harding is a Welshwoman, of which
she is peculiarly proud.” Why 1 Why should a woman
be proud of something she can t help? She might as
well be proud of big feet, corns, freckles, or a pug nose,
lust because they were hers. The Welsh peoples vanity
is so phenomenal that they think the obscure corner that
accidentally saw their first blind, helpless struggles and
re-echoed to their mewlings is honored above all other
■not* on earth. The man who wrote the par. is as great
a'fool as the woman. I could understand that “ Cow Fat
is a native of China, of which he is intensely proud, for
one don’t expect much manners of a Chinaman, who,
from a national point of view, thinks all foreigners dirt.
The tars on H.M.S. Pinafore were also proud of being
Englishmen,-but they knew no better, and their ignor-
ance is held up for the laughter of the world ; but tor the
all-wise Bulletin to chronicle such a thing is too much.
And to be proud of being Welsh, above all things, shows
the lady to be easily proud, for, to sum it up in a few
words, Wales is the smallest country with the smallest
people, both mentally and physically in the world ; where
ignorance is national, and which your friend, even
Brigand Stanley, was ashamed of as a birthplace.
A good deal of this is sound logic, and only goes
to show what a fool of a thing logic is. By the
same process of reasoning the above infuriated
correspondent has no right to be proud that he is
a man and not a horse, because he didn t attain
the dignity of a human being by any effort of his
own; therefore, if anybody calls him a man he
lias no reason to feel proud, and if anyone calls
him a horse he has no cause to feel offended, xne
fact that he doubtless would feel offended only
shows that he is the same kind of an ass as any-
body else. Logically, no doubt, nobody has any
right to feel proud concerning his country, or his
lineage, or his name, or his general personal
appearance, because he didn’t make any of them ;
nevertheless if he didn’t feel proud no country
would amount to much, and almost everybody
would' be named Smith or Hogg or something
unpleasant, and the human race would be even
more blotched and freckled, and more troubled
with ingrowing nails and untidy hair than it is.
Logic is a great institution, no doubt, but a
strictly logical world wouldn’t be worth living in,
all the same. , •

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
tn the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
'Correspondence" column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding tivo columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire these sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission aj such MSS.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 17, 1894.
A State Grog-Department.
The fact that Australians, in common with
the rest of humanity, drink too much is so
universally admitted that further argument
on that branch of the drink question is un-
necessary. The additional fact that they
drink many curious and horrible compounds,
and are inwardly corroded by all manner of
gruesome and muddy adulterants, is also a
matter which is too well-known to require
demonstration. The strange acids and
alkalis which find their way into the alcohol
of this country, and into the inner economy
of its inhabitants, the weird drugs and
chemicals, the dirt and coloring matters, the
tobacco-juice, vitriol, white spirit, burnt
sugar, salt, essences and perfumes, the things
that are used to make one gallon—or some-
times no gallon at all--of genuine liquor do
the duty of four, the things that are em-
ployed to give it an artificial “bead,” or a
sparkle, or a “ head,” to stir it tip when it
is flat or to tone it down when the taste of
some particular horror is too conspicuous, all
read like the story of some nightmare
brewed in a cauldron by a witch of the
olden time. What the effect of these
adulterants is upon the aggregate constitu-
tion can only be vaguely estimated, for there
are no comparative statistics available ; there
was no period in history when the Australian
drank pure liquor, so how differently the
mortality and insanity and other returns
would read if he did drink pure liquor is
largely a matter for conjecture. The one
thing that is reasonably certain is that they
would read differently. Meanwhile, the
law makes feeble efforts to purify the national
drinks by sending round an officer at long
intervals to buy samples from the retailer
and have them analysed, and if they won't
stand analysis a fine is imposed on the
miserable publican, and thereafter business
proceeds much as usual.
* * •
The precautions taken against the sale of
poisonous liquor are inefficient for many
reasons. They apply mainly, in an absurdly
spasmodic and ineffectual way, in the cities
and provincial towns ; the remote coun-
try shanties, where the liquor horror as-
sumes its worst form, are little visited.
The publican, in many instances, knows
or suspects his visitor, and only sells
him samples which can bear inspection.
The law deals only with cases of actual
adulteration, and all manner of bad and
unwholesome liquors get through simply
because they are not actually adulterated.
And, above all, the law is ineffective because
—in New South Wales at all events —it deals
only with the retail drink-seller. In that
land of anomalies, any kind of drink, no
matter hew nefarious, may, in 999 cases out
of 1000, be imported with absolute impunity;
and the wholesale merchant may sell it to
the retailer without any questions being
asked. Then when it is found in the re-
tailer’s possession the law assumes that the
adulteration must of necessity have been
effected by him, and acts accordingly. The
law is perfectly aware that, in many cases,
this a sumption is wholly erroneous. It
is a matter of ' notoriety that large
quantities of spirits which are refused
admission at other ports, on the ground that
they are unfit for human consumption, are
sent on to Sydney and landed there without
the slightest hindrance. The Government,
not long since, formally admitted this fact
by proposing to introduce a bill according to
which no imported liquor should be issued
from bond unless it was certified, after
official examination, to be fit for human con-
sumption. And the Collector of Customs
further admitted it by protesting against the
proposed measure, on the ground that it
would be “ a harsh interference with trade.”
Therefore, the State officers are aware, by
their own acknowledgment, that New South
Wales receives from abroad quantities of
liquor unfit for human use, and yet
it allows this liquor to be imported and
sold to the publican with impunity, and
contents itself with fining that miserable
scapegoat, and endorsing his licence, when
he unconsciously or carelessly buys the com-
pound and passes it on to the consumer.
* * #
The attitude of the Collector of Customs
in this matter indicates a curious mental
condition on the part of that official. The
Government proposal would only have the
effect of interfering with the trade in bad
liquor; the trade in good liquor would
be materially helped by it, partly because
the Government certificate would be a guar-
antee as to quality, and partly because the
honest trader would be protected from the
unfair competition of nefarious alcohol im-
ported at almost nominal prices. Therefore,
it is the trade in liquor that is unfit to drink
which would be harshly interfered with, and
the officer who considers that such a trade
shouldn’t be harshly interfered with is in a
remarkable state of mind. Moreover, the
law already interferes much more harshly
with the same trade so far as concerns the
retail branch of it, and why the original
offender should go free while the secondary
one is heavily punished passes comprehen-
sion. The Bulletin presumes that the
Collector of Customs is a sound Freetrader.
• * «
Even, however, if the Government pro-
posal is carried out, which seems decidedly
improbable, it will only touch the outermost
fringe of the evil. It will leave the supervision
of the retail adulterator as inefficient as ever.
It will do nothing whatever to check the
local manufacture of illicit drink, mostly of
the worst quality, or to improve the local
article, which is not illicit but simply un-
wholesome. Also, it will leave untouched
all the other ill features of the trade—the
vast number of superfluous “ hotelsthe
brutal ex-pugilists and broken-down “sports”
who take to hotel-keeping in their old age
for want of any other refuge ; the dingy,
dirty, unkempt establishments which are
partly brothels and partly dens of thieves ;
and the necessity, arising from excessive
competition, to do everything possible to
create an artificial thirst in a community
which is already much too thirsty by nature,
and to establish an artificial demand where
there is already far more demand than
enough. The most careful and efficient
supervision of the quality of the liquor sold
will leave all these matters untouched,
and there is no visible remedy for them
save by making the drink traffic in
all its branches importing and manu-
facturing, wholesale and retail a State
monopoly. Other measures, no matter how
stringent, can only palliate one evil at a time
and can only do that feebly and indecisively.
The nationalisation of the drink traffic is the
one device which will deal effectively with
all the aspects of the problem at once.
• * 9
The public accepts new ideas slowly and
reluctantly, and to make it begin to consider
them seriously, or even to make it begin to
realise that there are such ideas in existence,
it is necessary to present them over and over
again, in all manner of forms, and with per-
sistent reiteration. Therefore, The Bul-
letin makes no apology for defining once
more the case for a State Drink Department,
and will make no apology for defining it
several hundred more times in the future.
Under State management only sufficient
bars would be maintained to meet the
public demand, whereas, according to the
present system, they are as many as
can contrive to make a living for their
owners, said living being often eked out
by all manner of immoral devices. Under
these circumstances the number of bars
would probably be reduced by one-half or
more. The bar-keepers would be Civil
servants and persons of good character ; the
respectable employees at present in the trade
would mostly find work under the new
regime, but a clean sweep would be made of
all the broken - nosed pugilists, jockeys,
spielers, drunkards, and women of loose
character who form a large percentage of
the drink-selling community. All the bars
attached to first - class hotels would be
taken over by the State at a rental based
upon a fixed percentage of the profits,
and in those localities where no respectable
bars existed, clean and commodious saloons
would be opened ; but all the dingy, unclean,
ugly and unsavoury little dens, with which
the slums of every Australian city and the
remoter regions of the bush are profusely
dotted, would be entirely wiped out.
* * *
The State would be the sole brewer, dis-
tiller and importer, and would sell no liquor
except after strict analysis. Therefore the
trade in poisonous grog, which eats away the
brains and the intestines and drives the
customer to death or idiocy or homicidal
frenzy, would come to a sudden end. So,
also, would the business of illicit distillation,
except on a microscopic scale, for the
abolition of the private publican would
practically close the market for uncustomed
alcohol. Fully half the persons who now
live by this unproductive trade in its
various branches—importers, wholesale mer-
chants, clerks, salesmen, travellers, publi-
cans and their hangers-on, excise officers,
and others—could be dispensed with, and
every means whereby the unproductive classes
of the community are diminished adds to the
aggregate wealth of the nation. Further,
the consumption of drink, under the pro-
posed conditions, would be vastly reduced,
for the Government servant, being a salaried
official, would have no inducement to push
trade, and would merely meet the demand
without attempting to create one. And, in
addition to all these things, the revenue to
be derived from a Government drink mono-
poly would be an enormous assistance to the
depleted treasuries of Australia, at a time
when the old sources of incomes are in a bad
way and new ones are hard to find, and woe-
fully disappointing even when they are
found.
* * •
The teetotal faction is, and is likely to be,
the worst obstacle in the way of an effectual
reform of the drink traffic. All the evils
which have attached themselves to the trade
without being in any sense a part of it—the
immense superfluity of hotels, the low
character of many publicans, the nefarious
quality of the liquor sold, the dirt, the
gambling, the immorality, the trade in illicit
spirits, the “ lambing-down ” which is prac-
tised in bush shanties, the frowsy houri of
the bar, the blotched and bloated bar-
keeper, and all the rest of the ugliness and
moral and physical unsavouriness of the
business —form the greater part of the
teetotal fanatic’s stock-in-trade. It is on the
gratuitous assumption that these things are
all essential parts of the liquor business that
he bases his demand for total Prohibition,
and the establishment of the trade upon a
footing from which all these unwholesome
elements were excluded would practically
destroy the branch of eloquence by which he
makes his livelihood. But the nation at
large, which does not earn its salary by
hoarse fulrainations against alcohol, has to
consider things as they are. The present
system of badly supervised drink-shops is
bad. The Prohibition system, which means
as many or more drink-shops under no
supervision at all, is worse. And the only
visible remedy is a State monopoly, which
will make the supervision constant, effective,
and practically automatic, and abolish all
inducements to evil practices by making the
drink-seller a public servant whose salary
depends upon good behavior, instead of a
private speculator carrying on business for
his own profit.
“ My Relation, the Judge.”
Purity of justice is invaluable. A Judge
has always a difficult part to play—a part for
which divinity itself would be none too
perfect. Yet a Judge is but a man—with a
man’s passions, a man’s foibles, a man’s
nature. Therefore, we do our best, by
tempting his manhood, to make him a bad
Judge.
* * •
There is force in family relationship.
“Blood is thicker than water,” says the
proverb. A man is naturally drawn to his
own kith and kin. Therefore, in ordinary
life, we do not attempt to outrage nature.
Sportsmen will not choose as judge in their
contests the relation of one of the com-
petitors. Business men will not choose as
umpire in an arbitration the relation of one
of the disputants. But, when we leave the
field of sport and business—matters of
limited social importance— and enter the
arena of Justice—a thing of permanent
national importance —then we say: “Let
justice be a family affair. Here nature may
be outraged with impunity.”
* * *
To a Judge, money is a comparative trifle.
His mind is large enough to spurn the folly
of amassing wealth for wealth’s sake. He is
paid a high salary; he has, even if stricken
by paralysis two minutes after his appoint-
ment, a large life pension ; his future i s
secure. Yet, when he is asked to decide a
case in which he has the remotest money
interest, he descends from the Bench. “ The
er “ in f °l, Ju ® tice ’” he sa y s > “is easily
sullied. She should avoid even the appear-
ance of evil.” Good. And when the same
Judge is asked to decide a case with which
the life-success of his son, or his daughter’s
husband, or his brother's son, is or may be
intimately concerned, he stays on the Bench
He may be influenced by money, you see •
but he is proof against fatherhood or friend-
ship. Good,
• * *
The law has many branches. A lawyer
may choose to be a solicitor or barrister to
begin with. A barrister, he reflects,’ i s
brought most closely into contact with’the
Judges. He becomes a barrister. Then he
may choose in which of several Courts he
will chiefly practise. He chooses the Court
in which he thinks he will most speedily
obtain professional advancement. In N.S. W
there are four barristers who have chosen
each to practise chiefly in a Court over which
a blood-relation presides. Four —related to
several different Judges, and each practising
before the Judge to whom he is related”
What but the hope of turning relationship to
professional account guided this singularly
unanimous choice 1 These barristers rely, as
we all rely, on reasoning based on accumu-
lated human experience. “Blood is thicker
than water.”
* * •
It is unusual for a barrister to succeed in
his profession from the outset. He often
waits a long time before he meets the golden
shower of guineas. In New South Wales
there are eight barristers closely related
to six Judges. These eight have all suc-
ceeded from the outset, leaving far behind
them in the race their competitors of equal
standing, of equal ability. If such friend-
ship of solicitors is not due to the knowledge
that relationship counts for something in the
battle of the briefs, to what is it due 1
* * *
Law is not an exact science; it leaves always
loopholes for disagreement. Sometimes the
merits of a case are so nicely balanced that
the best judge is unable to fully convince
himself on which side justice lies. At such
times a feather will turn his mental scale.
Would the unconscious harmony of relation-
ship be such a feather ? It is likely. “ Iron
sharpeneth iron ; so a man sharpeneth the
countenance of his friend.” How much
more will hereditary predispositions chime
to prepare a father’s intellect to be sharpened
by a son’s arguments ?
* # *
But, it is said, if the bias exist, the
J udge is conscious of it, and fights against
it. Neither a Judge nor any other man is
wholly conscious of the play of sympathetic
influence. A wife frequently becomes, both
in thought and feature, a living replica of
her husband. But neither is conscious of
the steps by which this result is reached.
Therefore, as to that part of his mental pro-
cesses which is beyond his control, the
Judge is biased. So far as he knows him-
self, he may fight against natural impulses,
even to the extent of consciously favouring
his son’s opponent. But that very con-
sciousness will by-and-by breed a recoil ;
and, either way, conscious favor is harmful.
« * *
It is not in the public interest that Brass
the solicitor should prepare cases for Brass
the barrister to argue before Brass the
Judge. 'though the Judge be absolutely
just, the public knowledge of the effects of
consanguinity in ordinary life will create
disbelief in his justice. And if such disbelief
exist, whether it be well or ill-founded,
the administration of justice is prejudiced.
Thoughts are things.
« • *
Justice is attained by law, which is fol-
lowed as a profession, for profit. It is not
in the public interest that the profits of law
should be monopolised by certain families.
The law is already a close corporation
better not make it a rotten borough. The
prospect of the creation of a caste of legal
Levites, knit together by ties of blood and
interest, handing down their privileges from
generation to generation, and jealously
defending them against those outside the
pale, cannot be contemplated with satis-
faction. It is not possible or desirable to
close the profession of law to any eligible
person on the ground of his relationship ; i fc
is possible and desirable to so far abrogate
individual freedom that relationship shall
not interfere with public justice or degrade
the public conception of justice.
* * *
On these grounds The Bulletin approves
of the Parliamentary proposal to deprive
lawyers of the right to practise before their
kindred by blood or by marriage. Little
real hardship will be caused when that pro-
posal becomes law. And, in any case, it
seems not wholly unreasonable that Justice
should be considered before her parasites.
The severest moralists kill their fleas;; and
here there is only question of asking the
fleas to prey on another part of the carcase.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Commonwealth’s President.
One of the accompanying symptoms of the
slowly simmering sentiment in favor of Austra-
lian federation is a tendency to “Resolve ” that

the Governor-General of the federation should
be elected by popular voting. This, if any-
thing, is a healthy sign of the sub-conscious
attitude of Australian manhood towards Re-
publican Independence. Sydney D.T. is hard
set to explain away this awkward fact, and
clumsily endeavors to pervert the force of its
significance. The D.T. declares that the
phenomenon only “show's how loose and im-
mature popular opinion may be with regard to
—good old grandiose wheeze !—constitutional
principles.” The citizens don’t care two straws
about alleged “constitutional principles.”
Australian manhood simply knows that it wants
to elect all its own rulers, no matter what
happens to supposed “ constitutional prin-
ciples.” “ But there is no widespread popular
movement in favor of ‘ cutting the painter,’ ”
says the D.T. No ? When a man proposes to
knock out the keystone of an arch, according
to this logic, he has no notion of demolishing
the arch. Because Hori Grey, a democratic
tribune, saturated with the bias of aristocratic
heredity, performed the jesuitical feat of per-
suading himself that Australia could elect its
own Governor-General and still remain a
British dependency, the D.T. argues that demo-
crats of democratic heredity all think the same
way. Not so. The enthusiastic thousands
who cheered Grey at Sydney as no man was
ever cheered before in Australasia applauded not
Grey, the born aristocrat, but Grey, the cham-
pion of equal voting. The ordinary would-be-
free man doesn’t make subtle “ constitutional”
distinctions; he merely strives to attain each
object as it becomes to him desirable. All the
same, the demand for an Australianese Gover-
nor-General means, bluntly and squarely,
“cutting the painter.” The D.T. scorns the
idea that such a desire exists. But the facts
prove otherwise. “Cutting the painter” has
been seriously threatened by Australian Cabinet
Ministers ; its possibility is recognised by the
Anglo-Australian officials in England ; and its
dangerous likelihood has been, time and again,
urged by the very Toryish papers of which
the D.T. is a type. If there existed no
such Australian sentiment, what need would
there be for the periodical Fat Man boast, when
Fatmanish interests are jeopardised, that
Great Britain knows better than to oppose
Australian aspirations too strenuously lest Aus-
tralia should break away? How often have we
not heard that self-satisfied boast ? The plan
of Australian procedure is plain. Let Austra-
lians move doggedly on towards those objects
which they feel they must achieve, which they
feel are necessary to their healthful progress,
and —damn the consequences ! You can’t grow
a stately, full-sized araucaria in an earthen pot.
Inter-Australian Reciprocity.
Editor Bulletin, —Prior to the present dis-
cussion on inter-Australian Customs Treaties, I
laid before you a simple plan for a measure of
interprovincial Freetrade. My proposal was
that where identity of tariff exists, it should,
and could, be taken advantage of at once. The
road to Federation is not easy, as anyone who
studies the tariffs can see. The assimilation of
our domestic legislation would not offer nearly
the number of difficulties that impede tariff-
unity. Leaving aside the taxes on fruits, dried
fruits, and stock, I instance the following items
taken at random :
In these instances there is at once identity and
disparity. N.S.W. and Tas. agree about
ploughs, and Queensland, Westralia, and Tas.
agree about stationery—but the other differences
are tragical. Compromise seems far away.
Yet there are most important identities of
tariff. South Australia and Victoria have
identical tariff's in these directions :
M >st boots and shoes, many acids, bicycles, blacking',
butter, carriages and most vehicles, china and porcelain,
dried fruits, jams and jellies, jewellery, pipe and sheet
lead, silks and satins, etc.
New South Wales and Queensland agree for
the following :
Arrowroot, barley, biscuits, cement, chocolate, cigars,
clocks and watches, cocoa, coffee, cordials, liqueurs and
spirits, galvanised wire, hoop-iron and nails, pickled and
dried fish, wheat and flour, onions, jams and jellies,
kerosene, lace-stuffs, lead, many oils, etc.
But the most important identities of all exist
between South Australia and Queensland. In
my opinion it lies with the Ministries of these
two provinces to strike a powerful and dramatic
blow for Federation. Their tariffs are identical
on the following items :
Ale, most apparel, axles, baskets and wiokerware,
biscuits, spirits, cordials, etc., blacking, boots and shoes,
brassware, cabinetware, most carriages and vehicles,
cement, copperware, much ironwork, most tools, harness,
jewellery, hats and caps, furniture, etc.
With a wide range such as these items cover,
the tariffs of Queensland and S.A. are practi-
cally without difference. A bold federal policy
in Brisbane and Adelaide could bring Queens-
land and S.A. into Customs union next week.
It is actually true that legislation would not be
needed. It is not a question of collecting
Customs. It is a question of not collecting
Customs, and a Ministerial mandate is all that
is actually needed. The Ministers of these
two provinces have it in their hands to do a
great service to Australian unity—and may we
nope that the course of action is not too
simple, too devoid of the pomp of conven-
tion and congress and debate ! If, however,
the move is too daring—let legislative authority
be sought at once. My scheme does not con-
cern the difficulties of difference—only the
possibility of identities. I believe that the
demand for further interprovincial agreement
would be ten times intensified by one good
working agreement such as now proposed in
this and previous letters. Federation would be
recognised in its true light as the one way to
national strength, and I pity the man who, at
such a juncture as this, is too weak to be bold
in such a cause.— Fluffy.
The Republican Victory.
The Congressional elections in the United States
have ended in a Republican majority, reported
by the Foreign-trade S.M. Herald as 104 strong
in a House of 356 members, against a previous
Democratic majority of 80. The last semblance
of the alleged Freetrade reaction is thus gone
for good. But, as a matter of fact, there never
was any Freetrade reaction except what was
manufactured by the shoddy press in England,
Australia and elsewhere, and by the Single-tax
enthusiasts in the States. The Democratic
party took off the wool-duty and reduced the
import-tax on silks and various other luxuries ;
also, it increased a few other taxes, got rid of
the surplus and replaced it by a big deficiency,
made it necessary to borrow money for the first
time since the war, and brought about the worst
commercial crisis within the memory of this
generation. Now the Democrats are utterly
routed; the author of their new tariff is unseated;
their stronghold, New York State, is captured by
the high-tariff party, and all is over. The Sydney
press is now busy explaining away the disaster.
U.S. Consul, Colonel Bell— who was sent to
N.S. Wales to preach Freetrade so that the
simple-minded Cornstalk might be induced to
keep his ports open to Yankee manufactures—
is interviewed by the Telegraph, and cheer-
fully undertakes to account for everything.
“ M'Kinleyism,” he says, “ will never be re-
vived,” and “ Protection is on the down grade ”
in the States ; while right over his statement
stands a cablegram announcing that M‘Kinley
himself has been returned by a majority of
140,000 votes, and that his party proposes to
run him for President. Further, the Colonel —
who as an explainer has no rival, save Mrs.
Mellon —says that the result is quite natural:
the President is elected every four years and
Congress every two years, and “ there is
nearly always a reaction against the President
at the election following.” Considering that
the avowedly high-tariff party held office with-
out a break for 24 years, and in all that time
there was no such reaction, the Colonel’s assur-
ance that the reaction is quite “ usual ” is very
steep indeed. Premier Reid made even a worse
exhibition of himself than the Colonel. Being
asked in the Assembly if he proposed to send
along another congratulatory cablegram, this
time to the Republican party, he cheerfully
replied that the victory only meant that “ one
Tammany party had overthrown another.”
The remark was none too brilliant, and it puts
Mr. Reid in an awkward position, seeing that
he and his friends sent a cable-message (which
simple-minded greeting has never yet been
acknowledged) to that other “ Tammany ’’
crowd, a very few years ago, congratulating it
on being the party of progress, honesty and
enlightenment. His implication, now that his
party has gone utterly broke, that it is oi\Jy a
gang of thieves and corruptionists whose defeat
is of no significance, is vaguely suggestive of
awful perjury somewhere. Altogether the job
of explaining away the Republican triumph is
getting on very slowly indeed.
Finance and Mud.
New South Wales Premier Reid’s Financial
Statement has a ring of honesty about it, but,
at the same time, it ought to wreck his reputa-
tion as a heaven-born financier. The Premier
is so intent on throwing mud at his prede-
cessors that he has time for very little else, and
on the present occasion he spent so much time
in mud-throwing that he wound up his
Financial Statement without giving any
financial statement at all. The main points
of his deliverance were: (1) that the
Dibbs Government produced surpluses which
it had no right to produce by arbitrarily-
sawing amounts off the estimates of expendi-
ture sent in by heads of departments —a thing
which every Government lias done for many
years past —because if every head of a depart-
ment got all the money he asked for the public
expenditure would be illimitable; (2) that
there is a deficiency for 1894 of some .£300,000
or .£400,000 ; and (3) that there is going to be
a deficiency instead of a surplus for last year,
because the new Treasurer is going to add on to
Mr. See’s estimates the£2l6,ooo which was then
saved out of the roads and bridges expenditure,
and charge it to the late Ministry. In other
words, he is going to spend the money last
year, from which it appears that G. H. Reid
can turn the universe backwards, and stand
chronology on its head, and can overtake yester-
day. The deficiency for ’94, he admits, is
arrived at by taking the figures for the 10
months that are gone and estimating those of
the two months to come “on a different
basis; ” whether there would be a shortage
if the estimates were made on the same basis is
not explained. Finally, he announces that
Freetrade is postponed till next June, and ex-
plains, as every other Treasurer has done, that
everything is going to be conducted honestly
and capably henceforward, and that the
monetary sow which was washed has sworn off
for good. The late Treasurer See was a pretty
bad failure, and his perky successor promises,
in his own well-meaning fashion, to be another.
A Voice from Rue Australia.
Seventy lost Rue Australians are petitioning
New South Wales Government to give them
passages back to their native land. It wouldn’t
cost much, and in the name of common humanity
the small boon applied for should be granted.
The chief arguments advanced against it are
that the Rue Australians should be left to
suffer the consequences of their own folly, and
that if the'Government interferes it will only
encourage other visionaries to go off on similar
expeditions and trust to a paternal Legislature
to "see them through in the event of failure.
Neither argument will hold water. The
children, and to some extent the wives, of the
battered argonauts are not responsible for their
miserable situation, and to abandon them on
account of the foolishness of their fathers and
husbands is sheer brutality. As for the fathers
and husbands, the law exists mostly to protect
people from the consequences of their own
folly, and no exception should be made in their
( se. If they are to be left to starve in a hungry
1 I because they allowed themselves to
be taken in by a glozing enthusiast, then
the law should also refuse to take cognisance
of the injury done to depositors in fraudulent
banks, to the victims of confidence-men, to
people who are taken in by bogus companies,
or whose houses are burgled through want of
reasonable precaution, or who can’t get their
wages because they trusted to knavish em-
ployers. The legal system which spends money
to protect all these people, and to recover a
dividend for them wherever possible, can t
reasonably object to lend a hand to its stranded
subjects in Paraguay. Moreover, there is
abundant precedent for such a course of action;
one of the great objects of the consular system,
which is maintained at great expense by all
civilised nations, is to look after stranded sub-
jects in foreign lands, many of them stranded
by their own foolishness. As for the assertion
that the assistance proposed to be granted to
the lost Rue Australians will encourage others
to follow in their footsteps, it rests on no theory
that can bear inspection. If the spectacle of
the Paraguayan pilgrims coming back to start
life afresh, penniless, ragged and gaunt, foot-
sore with tramping over the stony ways
of South America, after having sunk all
their life’s savings in a hopeless ven-
ture, is calculated to induce others to
start on similar expeditions, then there
are some features in the human character which
have hitherto escaped observation. The return
of Lane’s adventurers is more calculated to
serve as a deterrent to similar expeditions in
the future. When they return, it will be as if
a number of hanged men arose from the tomb.
If they remain in South America they will
speedily die out, or vanish from public view
and cease to be heard from, and before long
they will be practically forgotten. If they are
brought back they will scatter to their former
haunts all over Australia, and carry their story
with them, and so long as they live it will be a
difficult matter to get up another Paraguayan
scheme in this country. Considering how much
good red gold that ill-fated movement took out
of Australia, and how much more another such
movement may take out at some future time
unless it is nipped in the bud, it should pay an
actual cash dividend to bring back the 70
haggard petitioners, if only as an object-lesson
to the next crowd of enthusiasts who come
along.
The Same Old Story.
“ There had not been any boat-drill for the
last six months,” said the chief officer of the
Wairarapa at the enquiry into the causes of the
wreck. This corroborates the assertion of the
Seamen’s Union secretary in a recent Bulletin;
and coasting passengers will know henceforth
what they may have to fear in case of a wreck
—covered boats, rusted davits, missing gear,
plugs out, incompetent crews, and “ the Lord
ha’ mercy on us !” And if steamers are crowded,
as they frequently are, the boats wouldn’t go
round anyhow. Thanks to a beautiful provi-
sion of shipping law, which considers only
steerage passengers as “ passengers,” and deems
saloon passengers guests of the captain (! !) a
vessel can (and does) carry as many souls above
her license-number as greedy owners choose to
dump into her. That is, if a vessel is licensed
by the Marine Boards to carry 200 passengers,
there is nothing whatever to prevent 1000 from
being carried, and the owners are liable to
no penalty provided the surplus are but
“guests of the captain,” i.e., saloon-passengers.
Marine Boards never bother their heads about
these things. “Marine Board” is merely
a synonym for a set of fossils who sit
and spin red tape and draw fees and ascribe
to “ the visitation of God ” accidents due
primarily to their own recklessness and in-
capacity. Above all things they dread “ a
fuss ” which may shift them in their shells, so
they “ keep in” with the shipping owners and
give as little trouble as they possibly can.
And every now and again a wreck happens and
100 lives are lost, and the Marine Board con-
cerned wails and beats its breast in sympathy,
and an enquiry is held, and an officer (who has
probably worked 12 or 14 hours a day without
a break for the month before the wreck) is
disrated, and then the Board folds its hands
and goes to sleep till the next wreck. Then
the process is repeated, and The- Bulletin
prints a paragraph like this one, and tells the
public it is an Ass not to insist on stringent
laws and competent supervision on behalf of
travellers; and the public reads, and agrees
that it is an Ass, and says “ something should
be done.” And nothing ever is done, or will
be done till the Day of Judgment.
More “ Silken Bonds.”
London Times suggests the appointment of
Australian Judges on the British Privy Council
to adjudicate on Australian cases, and other
“colonial” Judges to decide, in like manner,
cases from their own “colonies.” This is, of
course, another devious, anti-Republican method
of “ tightening the silken bonds.” Apart from
the silken bonds, if Australian Judges are to
decide Australian appeals, why should they go
all the way to England to do it, and make
suitors crawl 12,000 miles at enormous expense
in pursuit of them ? Why shouldn’t the same
Judges hear the same cases and decide them
according to the same intellectual process right
here in Australia ? Or, if there is any reason
not yet explained why they shouldn’t do the
business in Australia, why should they do it in
England any more than in Fiji or Honolulu ?
Also, if Australian Judges adjudicating on
purely Australian cases must not hold their
courts in Australia, why doesn’t England hold
its Appeal Court in Nova Scotia or at the Cape
of Good Hope ? The only possible reason for
dragging Judges and counsel half-way round
the globe to decide some simple matter which
could be decided quite as easily in Melbourne or
Sydney, is that the Australian Judges will thereby
wear the coveted distinction of P. 0., and being
located nearer the Fount of Honour will have a
better show of being made lords ; but that is a
purely personal matter, and there is no earthly
reason why suitors should be ruined in order
that J udges may grovel after titles which are
of no use to anyone save the groveller. More-
over, the Australian local courts and execu-
tives are quite competent to hang a man, or to
condemn him to life-long imprisonment, or to
re f use _ as i n Morrison’s case—to a man under
sentence of death the right of Privy Council
appeal, even when such lawyers as Salomons
and Barton, Q.G.’s, hold that he has good
grounds for carrying his case to the highest
tribunal in the empii e. And if they can do all
that, they are fully competent to decide finally
on all cases—even those affecting the blessed
interests of Property. The law dealt out by
Appeal-Judge Smith in Sydney would be
exactly the same quality as that dealt out
by Smith, P. 0., in London, and to make liti-
gants go, at their own expense, all the way to
London merely that Smith or someone else
may be a P. 0., is an obviously lop-sided
arrangement. Let every man pay for his own
title.
Christianity to Measure.
Pastor Jefferis, the ex-Sydney land-specu-
lator now in Adelaide, is “much pained to
observe the great growth of Socialism, and
feels it devolves upon him as a teacher of truth
and righteousness to combat this tendency.”
When land-values rise and Parson Jefferis is
relieved from the worry of looking after lost
shekels, he may find time to read a book called
the Bible, which contains the most perfect creed
of Socialism yet announced. Moses (or whoever
wrote Leviticus and Deuteronomy) was a
Socialist, and condemned in no doubtful language
the usury which is the prop of the conventional
church. All the Jewish prophets were
Socialists, and Isaiah the greatest of Socialists.
Pastor Jefeeris will find his denunciations
of those who “ grind the faces of the
poor,” and of those “ who decree un-
righteous decrees to turn aside the needy from
justice,” as applicable now as when they were
written. And when Mr. Jefferis gets to the
New Testament, he will notice that the
Socialism of the Gospels goes as far as Com-
munism. The rich are repeatedly and terri-
bly denounced. The poor are blessed. The
voluntary surrender of property for the benefit
of the poor is recommended to the rich young
man (the Saviour didn’t advise him to join
a National Ass and defend his wealth).
“The ideal of the Christian society,” says
Graham, “ was equality of social condi-
tions, or, if any inequality, it was to be an
inversion of the existing one, requiring from
the greatest the greatest sum of services and
sacrifices ; no private property ; no competition
save to do the greatest good, with mutual love
making all possible and warming and vitalising
the whole community.” Of course, this teach-
ing is quite incompatible with the existence of
the fat-clergyman system ; and when Pastor
Jefferis does read the Bible he will probably
see it is to his interest to keep its socialism in
the background. In the meantime, it is to be
hoped that pious old Adeladies will endeavor
to assuage the “ pain ” of their speculative
shepherd by liberal prostrations before the
little god Saucer.
Apparently New South Wales Railway Com-
missioner Eddy has not been squarely treated-
He came here on the distinct understanding
that his salary was to be increased if his work
was satisfactory. His work was emphatically
satisfactory, and his salary remained at the
original figure—principally, as it seems, because
the understanding wasn’t embodied in the legal
agreement. And now Commissioner Eddy—
perhaps the best civil-servant Australia ever
had—is returning to England, presumably
because, being a man of unique ability, England
is prepared to pay him better. The Bulletin
doesn’t hesitate to say that if money would
induce Commissioner Eddy to stay, no money
should be spared. If £IOOO a year doesn’t pay
him, then give him £SOOO, or £6OOO. Under
the circumstances, an extra thousand or three
is of no account whatever. Money cannot be
put into the balance in comparison with brains.
If Commissioner Eddy was worth £3OOO when
he came to New South Wales, he is worth
double now that he is thoroughly acquainted
with local railway needs and conditions. He
has saved the province hundreds of thousands
of pounds, and his salary is a flea-bite in com-
parison. An incompetent man in his place, or
even a man not quite so competent, may cost
the province in a week many times Mr. Eddy’s
salary for a year. So, The Bulletin repeats,
if money can persuade the retiring Commis-
sioner to remain, he should be allowed prac-
tically to fix his own figure. Ability and
reliability united are priceless.
S. P. Reynolds, an inconsequential person who
owns a tannery, and who sat in Vic. Parliament
as representative of N. Melb. for a couple of
sessions, broke out in bitter lamentation at an
entertainment given him by a local Employers’
Union the other night. The wild woe of
Reynolds arises from the base ingratitude of
the N. Melb. electors in returning a better man
to Parliament at last election. “I find bene-
volence does not secure votes,” wailed the flab-
bergasted tanner. “ Men for whom I have
obtained employment, and whose families lived
on my charity, voted against me. As an em-
ployer of labor, I have distributed £IOO,OOO in
the electorate during the last six months, yet
men in my employ contributed to the deposit-
money of my opponent. The electors have
taken the most inferior men, men of no business
and no business capacity, and given them the
management of the country.” Tears. Reynolds’
lament is an ample justification of the judgment
of the electors of N. Melb., if his conduct in
Parliament, where he see-sawed on a rail, had
not justified them in firing him out. The man
who thinks he has a prescriptive right to repre-
sent the electors simply because he sends to the
local soup-kitchen the crumbs from his table
and “ gives ” work —quite a fin de sie.de touch,
this latter—can’t be argued with.

The Westraliau Nigger.
A famous Yankee-German mining man who
has travelled the earth and has just come from
Westralia after a long stay on and about the
goldfields, tells The Bulletin that the Wes-
tralian nigger is as abject a slave as ever the
n if?g er was in the States. He works in scores
of cases chained to his kind master’s barrow.
And here is a letter just received by The Bul-
letin from Coolgardie :
The Westralian nigger is an unhappy wretch whose
wants are few, and it takes him all his time to supply
them. Heretofore it was thought he had nothing to give
in return for salvation, and, as a consequence, he was
never suspected of owning a soul worth saving. But had
the man with the long coat and the white choker and the
itching palm known that gold was to be found here, and
that the nig. was a good “specker,” a brighter future
might have opened for the nig. aforesaid. But he said
nothing about the gold and went on living as his fore-
fathers did—on lizards and snakes, and bardies and
sandal-wood nuts, with an occasional blow-out on a kan-
garoo or emu. When he could find nothing to eat he could
always fill up with water, which he had taken some care to
conserve in namoi-holes in the rocks. Unfortunately for
him the secret of his gold became known, and he—especi-
ally in the neighborhood of Mount Margaret—is no longer
cheerful. Diggers and new-ohums and ex-bank-clerks
and city larrikins swarm down on him with horses and
camels and rifles, and drink his scant supply of water, and
shoot his game and borrow his gin and daughter and
kidnap his son, and riddle him with bullets if he puts
up a protest. If the whites desire possession of the
auriferous parts of his country, surely some non-
mineral-bearing portion of it should be set aside for
the black’s especial use, where he might be allowed
to die something approaching a natural death. As it
is, he has only the alternative of b ing shot on his
own land or clearing out to the spinifex country where
there is neither game nor gold nor gabbie. If be tries to
ward off starvation by stealing a little tucker from the
spoilers of his hunting-ground he does so at the risk of
his life, and if he ventures out of the scrub and asks for
gabbie to quench his thirst he gets lead, and if he gives
way to his savage and natural instincts and “goes for’’
his enemy a raid is made on him, and he and all his rela-
tions are wiped out. When he has the legitimate digger
to cope with his case is perhaps not quite so bad,
but, u iluckily for him, there is a class of men here
calling themselves diggers whose chief delight it is to sit
around camp-fires and boast of the number of niggers
they’ve shot, not in self-defence but simply because they
were niggers. If the meek missionary is too busily en-
gaged in converting the distant eocoanut-and-pearl-shell-
and-rich-land-endowed nig. to bother about this unfor-
tunate, is there no one with the cause of humanity at
heart prepared to come to the rescue ? From Coolgardie
to the Murchison, a distance of at least 400 miles, every
rook or soak where a drink of water is obtainable is made
the camping-ground of whites, who quarrel among them-
selves for possession, whilst the unfortunate nig. cools his
swollen tongue by sucking the tap-roots of trees.—Aus-
tralian, Coolgardie, 25/10/’94.
More Sermons from tlie Gallows.
The Victorian Executive has duly strangled
Elijah Cockcroft, the illiterate jockey, who
knocked his lady-love on the head and sliced open
her swan-like throat in the ecstacy of jealous
delirium, which several frigidly dissecting scien-
tists and two or three armor-plated prison-officials
have said was not insanity. If the ignorant
jockey was not insane so far as Fanny Mott was
concerned, will these expert authorities please
describe his actual condition ? As was the case
in a recent Queensland sensational girl-hacking
tragedy, Cockcroft’s motive for murder was his
real, red-hot, genuine, animal love for his Fanny.
Unlike the Queensland woman-killer, Cockcroft
gave no handle to the teetotal-intemperance man
to indict Der-rink as the mother of murder, and,
beyond the fact that he was ignorant of ordinary
school-lore and predisposed to religious impres-
sions, Cockcroft betrayed no outward and visible
sign of being an inward assassin. He was
probably a decent enough fellow, or he would have
been a more distinguished jockey. Once more the
conclusion is limned in inerasable vermilion : All
True Love is Insanity. Now, insanity, when its
hallucinations are humored, is harmless to the
humorers ; but, when its gospel is impeached and
ridiculed, it explodes like a melinite bomb. Love,
as a form of insanity—real nitric-acid, boiling-
ammonia sort of love—in its repulsed phases,
always spells Murder. The love that can resign
its object, for its object’s own happiness, is a poor,
puerile counterfeit, a poetical chimera that has no
rootlets in the seedbed of virility. Love is the
most selfish and greediest absorbent known to
nature, and it means Death to rivals and the
sacrifice of the beloved if she prove unwilling.
Real three-ply, all-wool love always has the
courage of its mission and the recklessness that
chances every hazard. If any besieged woman,
who wants real Love, doubts her lover’s bona-Jides,
let her put him to the test of murdering her or
letting her go ; and if he doesn’t start to make her
into statuesque cold meat she had better let him go.
Law, civilisation, re igion— nothing— can dam up
the molten lava of true love boiling in the
volcano of stirred manhood. Any yearn that
can be restrained is counterfeit. It may be
passably pleasing, it may be lengthily endurable :
but it lacks the inspiration that begins and ends
with —“ My life and your life.” Love is insanity,
and every real lover is as Cockcroft was in his
murder act. Those who are less afflicted with
love merely experience the passing phantoms of
idiocy. Cockcroft’s heart was too mighty for
his brain. He wanted Fanny Mott more than
life. Life without Fanny was less cheerful
than death. Fanny didn’t want Elijah. The
pair were out in a paddock together driving
home some horses. Like the prophet of old,
Elijah had his chariot of fire. He seated Fanny
i n it. The cause of his insanity being removed,
Elijah became once more a harmless maniac. That
his brain was weak is apparent from the reported
incident that, being asked on the gallows “the
usual question,” he said—“l love Jesus,” and
then sobbed. Well, throw the clods on the coffin.
The law of Victoria has strangled another lunatic;
Madness has once more avenged Love ; and Elijah
has gone to meet Fanny in Heaven. It reads like
the termination of the old style of novel —“they
lived happily ever afterwards.”
A low agitator has had the hardihood to say
that it was a disgrace to Bendigo (Vic.) City
Council that men employed by the municipal
contractors should be paid as little as 15s. per
week of 72 hours. Worse still, this hardened
ruffian (by name Egan, secretary of the Trades’
Hall), when the “city fathers” indignantly
denied his allegation, was brazen enough to
prove it by producing affidavits from two of the
men so employed and paid. One of these is
here reproduced from Bendigo Evening Matt.
of Oct. 27 —
I, James Quinn, of Baxter-street, Bendigo, in the
Colony of Victoria, do solemnly and sincerely declare that
I am employed by , a contractor under the
City Council of Bendigo ; that I am a married man wit h a
family of five children ; that 1 start work at six o’clock in
the morning, and am seldom finished before six o’clock in
the evening, being allowed one hour for dinner ; that I am
paid wages at the rate of 15s. a week without board ; that
I have been employed by at the same rate of
wages for a period of over twelve mouths ; that I am
working on Council work.
A similar affidavit from another man, but with
his name omitted, was also published. Next
day both the men were “sacked ” by the con-
tractor aforesaid. Yet this contractor had
been allowed .£127 more this year than last, in
order that a decent wage should be paid. The
Bulletin thinks this case breaks the record.
