846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
November 13, 1894.
My Dear Moorabinda—
Melbourne Cup (a piece of plate this year) of
’94 incontestably proved that age cannot wither
nor burst booms stale its infinite variety. Despite a
reduced stake and other dismals, the Cup was the
usual fine 16-hands spectacle, where people were
so packed that men, in saluting their acquaint-
ances, knocked other people’s hats off in trying to
raise their own. For it was a crowd, though not
so great as last year.
The Guvmentonse party were all in line, as
usual. Lady Hope.toun’s Cup-gown was the
black-and-white-checked silk, with lurid intervals
of magenta, in which she graced Rand wick Spring
meeting. Lady Duff did the lawn with Mr.
Pennyman, in white spots —I mean, he was. Her
dress was a sea-gull combination of grey and
white, and a black toque hat. Lady Norman
Cup’d it in guinea-heu colors. Lady Gormans-
town (her gigantic Lord regarded the Cup horses
as if mentally dividing them into chops and
steaks) looked skittish in salad-green and white,
and a Spring-like toppmg-off. Lady G. is a
medium-sized, fair-haired personage with a bold,
Roman-mother profile. I have seen many fairy
queens in pantomime the living image of Her Ex.
of Raspberryland.
Lord Hopetoun wore a pallid aspect and iron-
grey cap-k-pie unrelieved by a boutonniere. The
sonsie Kintore wore pink flowers, the color of his
roseate cheek. Sir Thou Art My Soul Robinson
wore pink flowers, too. Anybody who saw Wal-
lace Brownlow as The Mikado has seen Sir Wil-
liam Robinson, by the way. On dit that His
Musical Ex., of W. A., is quite a lamblike person-
age to handle. During the recent rehearsals of
“Predatoros,”at the Princess’, Wallace Brownlow
transposed a note a tone or so lower one day to
rest his voice. “You don’t sing the higher note,
Mr. B.,’’ meekly said His Ex. (who was glued to
every rehearsal) —“ Do you think it would aound
better to sing it that way?” Such unnatural
modesty seems to point to Sir W. R. as the coming
Gounod.
But, to return to the Cup. Miss Kate Duff wore
a Cup gown that the most retiring woman could
safely go shopping in. Mias Heather Duff wore
blue and butter color lace. Miss Grace Norman
(people said she was the prettiest of the Y. R.
damsels, though I considered Mias Kate Duff
“got her nose in front,” as the jockeys say) was
cool in mauve and white. Miss Bowden-Smith
looked rather like a toilet-table in maize covered
with window-pane check white muslin. By the
way, I placed Lord and Lady Glasgow among the
princes and rulers at the Derby, whereas they
never crossed the seas. The odd part of it was I
knew they hadn’t, but when somebody who ought
to know said “Lady Glasgow” to a comfortable
dowager, all boots and bonnet, I forgot that the
Maoriland Ex. had omitted the trifling ceremony
of coming across. Whenever anybody indicates a
female form with hips as large as if the owner had
swallowed a life-belt, and a waist to match, also a
very after-date bonnet and a general expression as
if the wearer bad a tendency to go to sleep stand-
ing up—whenever anybody points an impressive
forefinger at that sort of person and says “Lady
Munro ” in a hoarse, melodramatic whisper, I fall
into the trap at the word “go.”
One of the handsomest dresses was very pro-
perly sported by Melbourne’s “society ” Madame
Mautaliui. It was really a good gown—the tender
hue of a cup of cold-and-milky tea, the skirt of
wide striped satin cut perfectly plain and short—
postillion bodice and whopping sleeves of cold-tea
velvet with a pleated vest of white chiffon and
side-pieces of real ecru lace. It was stylish, un-
aggressive,' and expensive, and I think when you
can hurl these three adjectives at a get-up and hit
it hard every time, there is nothing more to be
said. Mrs. (Dr.) O’Hara (who won money on
Ilium, a gentle gee-gee owned by Mr. Purves,
Q. C.) wore one of the neatest black gowns—striped
crepon and jet, with a bonnet trimmed with pink
clover. It seems to me that unless you wear
clothing like an explosion in a dye-vat and do the
lawn backwards or some way equally eccentric,
you stand a better chance of being seen in black
than anything else at the Melbourne Cup.
Some day women will dress for the Y.R.O.
festival as men do for fishing excursions. They
will see the hollow mockery of going out with big
leg-of-mutton sleeves and getting home with pan-
cakes in lieu thereof. And as they sew on furlongs
of ripped-otf flounces, some day they will rise up
and take a line from Daniel in the lion’s den, and
go down into the raging Cup almost as economi-
cally clad as he was. For all its reputation as a
rag-fair, the lawn at Flemington on the first Tues-
day in Nov. is really the worst place to spread
your tail-feathers in that ever was. No time for
“ settling” your skirts and posing against a balus-
trade there, mother. The burning question is
how to keep the other woman’s parasol out of
your eye.
Mrs. Byron Moore, in electric-blue figured silk,
with cornflowers (it was as much a cornflower Cup
as Carbine’s was) in a black jet bonnet, had
chosen a good color to go with Byron Moore’s
spruce-grey race-suit, which is more than most
wives think of. A certain auburn-haired official
personage has a Joan whose penchant for pea-
green amounts to a positive injustice to Darby,
who toddles along looking more like a glowing
wax-match than a person who could be toned
down to chromatic neutrality if his wife only
would wear a red feather.
Patterned stuffs were all the go at the Cup.
Things like red tarantulas swimming for dear
life in a pea-green sea appeared here and
there, but every yard of really fashionable
stuff was patterned, either checked, striped,
flecked with things like tadpoles in articulo mort is,
telegraphic tape-marks, flowers, spots, or fruit.
In many instances, the only plain space on a
toilet was where the wearer came to the surface.
Good lace was very plentiful, and augured well
for the Customs’ returns if everybody got their
dues. Airy gossamer fabrics like crdpe, gauze
and chiffon were on every gown—the typical
fashionable bodice was of silk or satin, covered
over with chiffon, drawn down to a ribbon at the
waist. Accordeon-pleated sleeves were a strong
card. Crepon was a favorite stuff—the rougher
the better. There wasn’t a pin’s point of dif-
ference between a brunette from Sydney in
carrotty ploughed-up-looking ordpon and a red
retriever pup, so far as wild rumpledness of out-
line was concerned.
I really think that a tall, fat, mining millionaire,
who carried three umbrellas (shut) at one grab on
Cup-day—and they only looked like so many
slender willow wands, he was so massive—l really
think he ought to have chosen a plumper wife
than the palpable light-weight who occupies the
proud position. In this age of thin-legged
mediums, with their hosen peeling off from the
attenuation following on the incarnation of
Oissie, Josephine and Geordie, you cannot see a
fat-thin couple without morbidly thinking that
the fat party is the vampire battening on the thin
party, whose socks or stockings, as the case may
be, are probably corkscrewing round their ankles.
I have been very much struck lately by the fact
that the fat is most unequally divided in many
otherwise well-regulated families.
Nellie Stewart, in a creamy silk skirt with a
pink silk bodice, reminded me of a Neapolitan
ice, but you musn’t think she didn’t look alto-
gether fetching, all the same; Mrs. Tapley was
on the lawn in the mauve frock she wore at the
Sydney theatrical f6te; Maggie Moore wore a silk
dress shot with bubble-colors and a rather down-
the-harbor hat; Lady Somebody— not Lady
Munro, mind you—wore what I considered the
most shrieking dress on the lawn. This “ con-
fection,” which, metaphorically, almost gave me a
black eye, was of white serge trimmed with frills
of red and white plaid. The bodice had flaming
scarlet sleeves and capes like a fish’s gills of the
tartan mixture as before. A black bonnet corked
a turn-out that would have made an ideal costume
for a living “ad.” to encourage folk to drink
M’Tavish’s whisky. Second place in the outni
costume stakes was filled by a Sydneyite in grey
with a Quaker fichu of white mull and a Quaker-
shaped bonnet of steel beads. The face inside it,
however, reminded you of every sort of lady but
Sister Ruth. No particular color was first favorite
at the Cup, though cornflowers rather made the
running, as they did in ’9O for bonnets and bou-
tonnieres alike. I was sorry to see that black
roses and violets had a good innings. No woman
of taste would mount such a frost-hitten trimming
at any time, for it looks as if she had been stand-
ing on her head in an ink-bottle and is an
outrage on nature. In these matters we are yet a
long way off the French, who never wear an arti-
ficial flower unless it’s naturally in season. These
burnt-cork floralities come from Germany, by the
way.
The “Butterfly fringe” was the Cup style—a
wildly fluffy bit of front hair, not poodle-curled,
but artistically waved. Its advent seems to toll
the knell of the bang-net, for which relief much
thanks, say the Johnnies who spent hours that
might have been more—er—profitably employed
in untangling Guinevere’s fringe-bag off their
shirt-studs after every engagement— or so the
flirts of both sexes inform me. One thing, if the
Butterfly fringe catches on any of Lancelot’s
surface inequalities it’s all up with the lady
since it is generally only the wearer’s own
in the sense that she has a receipted bill for it.
The onion-net fringe gave a sense of security to
the wearer that the butterfly bang can never hope
to emulate. “Mind my net,’’ was a generally
respected war-cry when hair-pins had blundered
and there was danger of the whole thing coming
off; but the wavy butterfly, which tempts an
amorous swain to toy with its fluffy disorder, at
awful risk of its coming off and leaving the lady
with a forehead half-way over her caput, would
put Amaryllis on pins and needles to get out of
the shade as soon as possible. I venture to pre-
dict, while the style is still in its infancy, that
“Noli me tanyere ” will be the motto of the
butterfly bangers. White veils with black spots
were all the go at the Cup. A more femininely
becoming fashion never rose in the east, though
the people who sport them do sometimes remind
you of unboiled currant-dumplings, especially
when they have a plethora of spots.
I was present at a rose festival at the Austral
Salon, Melbourne, on Monday week. The Salon
swells as it proceeds (in more ways than one), and
if it gets much more rushed the cosy room on the
Block will only be large enough to hold the
disciples’ goloshes. The Salon is to be congratu-
lated on always arranging a picturesque mise-en
action. On Monday the girls singing and recit-
ing, the youths fiddling and ’celloing among lashins
and lavins of roses, the tea-cups set about, the
subdued light—the artistic fixings—all made up
into an ensemble that reminded me of many fine
pictures I have seen. Captious people might
urge that though one maiden had a strong and'
thoroughly cultivated voice, she sang a passionate
love-song as if it was a multiplication-table set to
music instead of choosing something brilliant and
unmawkish—like the “Dashing White Sergeant,”
say ; but I have seen people gladly pay honest
silver to listen to much worse vocalism than the
Salon serves up gratis to its friends and backers.
Two of the Salongers will shortly have a novel
apiece on the market. Miss Melbourne Punch
Murphy goes ’Ome to publish, in the form of a
tale, her seasoned opinions on the questions of the
day, and Miss Osmond has a book of stories on
the stocks. “Of making books there is no end.”
The prophet wouldn’t say “ All men are liars ”
nowadays. He would put it “All women are
story-tellers.”
The garden-party at Melb. Government House,
on the 9th, was a much smaller assemblage than
last year, but it was a great success backed by ex-
tremely lively music. Rather a gloom was cast
over the afternoon by the suicide of Major Inglis
the same morning, which sad affair came as a
great shock to his many friends. It even had
the effect of making Lady Hopetoun (in pink and
cream) thaw conversationally, and gushing people
took the opportunity to introduce themselves
and their friends to Her Ex. in her melting
moment.
But, in spite of momentary gloom, joviality in
all its phases sat upon the host and hostess. From
the subdued joy of the prisoner who hears the
releasing key turn, to the more hilarious expres-
sions of a fat man getting away alive from a can-
nibal island, you traced all shades of intense relief
in the manner and conversation of the Hopetouns.
For the G.P. was the last big affair, after which
their Excellencies were to depart for their country
residence “ contagious ” to Geelong until their
departure. “So we won’t be very far off,” said
Lady H. in a spurt of amiability to an enquiring
tormentress, quite forgetting that up to very
recently it didn’t matter a row of pins to the fat
woman (who yearned to make a sister of her)
whether Her Ex. was at Collingwood or the North
Pole. Many people farewelled the ’Opetouns
then and there, although the real wrench
won’t come until April. The premature good-
byes were most affecting, tearful adieux to the
Gov. at the last big revel of his reign, although he
sojourns on for several more moons ; it looked
dreadfully as if people were waving their farewell
handkerchiefs, so to speak, at the beer-and-skittles
aspect of Government House.
I consider the Hopetouns the living illustration
of the horsey proverb, “It isn’t the distance, bub
the pace, that kills.” They shirked nothing
during Cup-week, and gave entertainments almost
every night, and the result was that, on Oaks-day,
Lord Hopetoun staggered home semi-sunstruck,
and had to be laid out in lavender in the hospital.
The brazen Australian sun evidently means to
leave off as it began. At Lord H.’s first levee, he
nearly fainted before he had squeezed the digits
of half his subjects, because the sun had its eye on
him through one pane of glass.
It was a day of sorts for the G.P. The muslin
brigade said they melted, and the woolly ones
drew your attention to how cold the breeze was.
The aide-de-kong’s long-lost sister (double mean-
ing on the long) turned up with five black plumes
in her hat, and suggested five shots a penny.
Lady Duff wore flame-color bengaline and black
lace. The Duff demoiselles, in white mull over
yellow and pink and white respectively, seemed
rather out of it on two chairs at the edge of the
crowd. Gov. Duff burst forth into lavender kids,
and must have heard that the Viotoriennes
thought him handsome, judging by the way he
twiddled his moustache.
I saw some pretty dresses. Mrs. Francis Hart
(W.A.), wife of the “Predatoros ” librettist, wore
a very taking cape of black velvet and white
moire, with a trimming of white ostrich ; but in
one or two cases the wrong colors were hoisted.
Who would think, for example, that a woman
with a toast-colored complexion would wear a
curry-tinted frock? One of Melbourne’s ex-
beauties, in an elaborately girly white gown, had
a string of coral beads round her neck—tho’ she
certainly looked as if she had cut all her
teeth—wisdom included. Melbourne’s alleged
prettiest woman (who is also a crack bil-
liardist) came in washing-blue velvet and satin.
Mrs. John Eales wore a white silk gown with
bands of insertion that became her bridal blushes
to aT. But imagine a gladiator-red frock with
silver tinsel bodice, like Henry Vee before
Harfleur; and another of purple velvet with blue

Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, November 13.
According to local fashion scribes, Cup-day’s
grey and threatening morning was responsible for
a falling-off in the splendor of the dresses. But I
don’t quite see it myself, as the weather was fairly
settled by starting-time, and most women are
plucky enough to take a certain amount of risk
over such a stake as a new gown—“ wear it to-day
and wail it to-morrow ” being a most womanish
motto. And it would be hard to find a more
enthusiastic exponent of this spirit than a lady I
saw on the lawn, the daughter of a former Banana-
land Agent-General, who, with her parents and
sisters, was wrecked in a sailing vessel off the
Queensland coast at the outset of their voyage to
England. Ultimately, they got ashore in boats ;
but while still in danger, and in the intervals of
prayers and hymns, the damsel in question was
warned to prepare for the worst. “I’ve just been
thinking,” replied Miss Sixteen, buoyantly, “that
if we do go down, this wreck’s been a jolly lark,
anyhow!”
The men of the vice-regal party were a little less
resplendent than at the Derby. Lord Hopetoun’s
grey was of a darker shade, and most of the others
affected black, with not a single bright flower en-
livening their white carnation bouquets. Gov.
Gormanston, careless of rain or shine, was, out-
wardly, the homespun patriarch of yore, though
his fellow-countrymen claim that in the case of
“ Yenico Preston* Viscount Gormanston, premier
Viscount of Ireland,” there’s no need for painting
and decorating. His title, I believe, is more
awfully ancient than those of all his brother
Governors, in these latitudes, put together.
Lady Gormanston, who, being a second wife,
is much younger than her husband, looked
remarkably handsome at the races—her black
eyes, golden hair, and bright complexion set off
by a stringless, gold-crowned bonnet, crested with
black plumes, and a delightful pinky-golden
sunset satin, its bodice ruffled with pink and black
chiffon. The description, perhaps, sounds a little
stagey, but the costume and its really queenly
wearer caught on very well with male observers.
Women, as a rule, 1 think, preferred the cream
blue-grey twilight melange of Sydney’s vice-regal
dame.
Miss Feilding’s red and green checked silk,
banded with moire and butter guipure, was a vast
improvement on the floppy blouse and nonde-
script skirt of her Derby get-up. But a blouse,
unohaperoned by an outer jacket, always does
lack dignity. Which reminds me that Miss
Nellie Chirnside, the young lady of the clan (vice
Mrs. Everard Browne) adorned the Cup in a
bright pink accordeon-pleated vest, which looked
exactly like a lamp-shade under her tan coat.
That’s the worst of accordeon-flutes, they have
such a papery effect; and another woman, who had
ventured on an entire brown costume of that ilk,
might just as well have been done up in a sous 6
envelope.
To return to blouses, the haughty Countess
ceases to be impressive when she sets out for a
morning’s shopping, in a nice bodice, showing
that familiar rear-rift between it and the skirt,
which, despite patent clasps, still provokes women
to that nervous spine-clutching so bewildering to
uninitiated man.
Flemington was delightfully green and flowery
on Tuesday, and prominent among those Zingari-
ing over its pleasant pastures was braw Kintore,
seemingly more in tune with things in general
than the Potentate of Perth who sat communing
with himself all day. Westralian visitors, con-
sulted on the subject, gave it as their opinion
that he was thinking of “the old ’un ” —‘‘old
’un ” not being, as one might suppose, an irre-
verent pet-name for her ladyship of those regions,
but merely a Dickensese phrase used by t’other-
siders to indicate that their viceroy is off on his
old tack, viz., music. As the gubernatorial opera,
“ Predatoros,” was to be produced at a Princess’s
matinee on Nov. 12, the proffered explanation of
his Ex.’s abstraction was no doubt correct. He
was wondering, perhaps, whether his official con-
nection with the Crown would permit him to
accept a call before the curtain, and if so, whether
it were better to walk boldly to the fore or let
Williamson and Musgrove drag him on.
Nellie Stewart’s pink - and - white array was
as successful as her toilettes ever are, though
it couldn’t possibly be more becoming than
her “cheeky’’-cheeky Derby suit. The coif-
fure of plaited hair, affected by la belle
Rosette off the stage, is quite a rarity in an
age of coils and rolls, carrying one back to the
Arcadian days of old when overnight braids pre-
luded innocent festivity. The tongs fiend has
changed all that, and is turning the nation pre-
maturely grey. Though gold must be tried by
fire, the same ordeal apparently doesn’t answer
with hair.
The worst of it is that the fatal facility and
superior seeresy of the new method has extended
its popularity beyond our sex. The man who
would die rather than face a burglar in a night-
cap of curl-papers, experiences no qualm at ithe
idea of a locked door, a lighted gas-jet, and an
odour of burnt feathers. So widespread has the
pernicious practice become that an important
Vic. inland town boasts a popular parson, whose
back-tilted shovel hat displays a forehead fluff of
curls, which his lady parishioners declare to be
artificially waved. In fact, they aver that much
as they esteem and revere their shepherd_(who,
by the way, is a bachelor), nothing would induce
them to accept that fringe as natural—-no, not if
its owner swore to it on a cartload of Bibles.
The pretty Kintore cousin was again to the fore
in black grenadine fichued with cream lace, as was
Miss Grace Norman, looking rather soubrettish, I
thought, in heliotrope-muslin and a pert hat.
This damsel, since her arrival in Brisbane, has
acquired to a T the appearance and manner of a
flananalander. This is intended as a compliment.
Those who know say that the Northerners, if not
the prettiest, are the most fetching girls in Aus-
tralia. They are not burdened by the evidences
of rude health, though their complexions pass
well enough at night, but they have splendid eyes,
are born dancers and desperate flirts to a woman.
Mrs. Rupert Clarke was responsible for the
smartest of the “Cup teas,” but the Cliveden
family are not visible at present except at church
or charitable entertainments.
Which reminds me of a certain very smart
recent Toorak fete, whioli enabled a society goody
guild to flash its light before the eyes of men.
The tea buffet was superintended by a row of
tony dowagers, masquerading in muslin gowns.
To how few is it given to look distingue amid
squalid surroundings (if the spilt tea and crumbled
cake of Toorakia may be so coarsely described) !
I am bound to confess that the dames in question
were not among the rare exceptions, and customers
discussed their shilling snacks haunted by remi-
niscences of steamboat stewardesses and waiting-
room ogresses. It was in this connection that the
shocking faux-pas of a lately-imported johnny is
said to have occurred. “(No; I; can’t think of
troubling you. Miss Z.,” he observed gallantly to
the pretty Hebe who would have replenished his
cup. “I’ll hand it to old Dishwalloper here!”
indicating with a lordly wave of his hand an
attendant matron, nearly and dearly related to
his horrified hearer.
lam afraid we women are selfish wretches. I
noticed many couples at the races, whose ap-
pearance made it glaringly apparent that the
small sum available for decoration had been put
on “the Missus.” There was on the lawn one
most prominent and sour-visaged woman, the
cynosure of all eyes, who obviously had bullied
her smaller half into the outing, over-ruling all
excuses as to lack of clothes, on the grounds that
if he gave his hair an extra dab and invested in a
paper-collar he’d pass in a crowd —she, meanwhile,
pluming herself in the consciousness of an outfit
saved out of the butcher’s bill. It was a
costume, and no mistake—loud enough to be heard
at Rand wick, 600 miles away. Bright yellow
muslin over ditto sateen, very short in front to
display No. 9 white boots, and gloves to match.
Just let me add that the wearer was 48, if a day,
and of a scrub-saucepan complexion, and you will
agree that it wasn’t the consciousness of his own
grimy hands and dusty boots that worried the
poor old man so perceptibly and made him squirm
shamefacedly through a long and trying day.
The white-gowned wife of a much-advertised
doctor (by the way, L.L. dropped a modest .£3O
over the Cup, and mourned its loss cheerily) wear-
ing a precipitous metallic hat and accompanied by
some quaintly-clad youngsters and an unobtrusive
stepdaughter, was a prominent figure; while
Mdme. Dejardin looked handsome, foreign, and
animated as usual in blue and black. A Q’sland
visitor sported a gown of yellow glace silk with
pink floral stripes—a really effective garment, had
it not been creased from collar to hem in the most
heartrending manner. I am told that accordion
pleating is all a matter of pressing, and my idea
is that the crumpled damsel aforesaid may have
made some amateur attempt of the kind with the
family mincing-machine.
Tall, girlish Mrs. Isaacs was very soothing in
grey crepon, black satin and white silk. Hubby
kept close to her side all day, his presence recall-
ing the opening of Parliament, where the new
Premier, all in drab-color, conveyed che impression
of a cross between Noah’s dove and a pet rabbit.
He (Mr. Turner) looked aggravatingly meek
and conciliatory—as if he wag saying, “ When my
fall comes, which I almost hope will be soon, don’t
say I was proud in the day of my power, because
I’m trying all I know to show what a mild little
mouse I am.” Slim Mrs. Sylvester Browne
trotted beside her giant spouse in blue, set off by
some good black lace that told of Coolgardie divi-
dends ; and a handsome gown of black and green
was sported by Mrs. Albert Miller, the gold bonnet
of “Mrs. Albert” being also very glorious
when the sun caught it fair in the middle. From
a number of eager remarks passed upon Violet
Varley, I gathered that the glamor of operatic
bridehood dies hard. Tapley’s petite wife—she
promises to be the plumpest of partridges before
she gets into the thirties —looked happy in a helio-
gown (not the best of fits) and Tuscan
hat. Another stage lady much on view was Elsie
Cameron, a substantial embonpointed vision in
pink and grey. Wherever Elsie went, her chum,
Mrs. Ernest Brodribb, was also bound to go. The
holiday appearance of Mrs. E. B. took one’s
thoughts back to the time when “plunger” Brod-
ribb was himself a conspicuous promenader. I
didn’t see the good-natured little hubby on Tues-
day—indeed, he is back in the arid West hunting
for another fortune, I hear.
As a loyal Melbournian, I blush to admit that
Cup Day, despite all said and done, was only a
respectable ghost of its old historic self. After
luncheon, when Lady Hopetoun, putting herself
in charge of a diminutive, infantile Johnnie in a
long frock-coat, descended from the State-box and
made her way through the throng, the show on
the Lawn nearly set me weeping. Where could
one note to-day such dressing as used to thrill or
stagger us when Lady Loch ruled the roast ? The
falling off in the number of paraders was as pain-
fully evident as the huge reduction in splendour.
Carriage parties in the luncheon paddock made a
comparatively feeble muster, and every seat on
the Lawn had standing room to spare while the
race of the year was happening. I couldn’t see
the Maribyrnong Stand at this supreme moment,
but an hour earlier it was a superfluous structure,
tenanted by not more than twenty disconsolate
creatures who, as I imagine, desired a far-off
place to ruminate in.
As soon as Patron had won the Cup a cloud of
woe settled upon the occupants of the grand-stand.
The greatest and the least of the merrymakers
seemed to feel the prevailing depression. Me-
thinks I can still see Excellency Hopetoun peep-
ing at a cold world from under the rim of a hat
tilted forward to shade his eyes, whilst the
Countess sat with crossed knees and folded arms,
as though defying Fate to do anything much
worse than this.
VAN M.
Coincidences. The three women lately
arraigned in Melbourne on separate murder
charges, Anderson, Needle, and Yenarelle, had
the same name—Martha. Evil has fallen upon
Scriptural cognomens.
The London Literary World of Oct.
5 republishes “Rolf Boldrewood’s” acknowledg-
ment to Louis Becke, and remarks that it is “a
curious advertisement, the true inwardness of
which is left to be inferred.”
The S.M. Herald’s statement that F. A,
Morgan, ex-Mayor of Rockhampton (Q.), who
died last week from the effects of a paralytic
seizure, “ was one of the discoverers of the famous
Mount Morgan mine,” is notoriously unhistorical.
Some of those Melb. “ society ” girls and
youths who are in the habit of attending the
Princess’ amphitheatre in a party, democratised
themselves still further on Cup day, when they
hired a van and drove to the Flat in true Colling-
wood style.
The two German skin-buyers, the brothers
Munk, who committed suicide by shooting at the
Oxford Hotel, Sydney, left a cheque for .£5O to
cover the funeral expenses. The supersensitive
unfortunates might have saved themselves the
trouble, as no bank will cash a cheque bearing the
signature of a dead man.

Dear Bulletin, —The report of that
Maoriland tragedy sets one thinking of the report
of another murderous assault on three men, in
Queensland, some years ago. The sworn evidence
on that occasion set forth that Morgan was struck
on the head, that Johnson was struck on the bal-
cony, and that John Whalen was struck on the
24th inst. Whalen died, if I remember rightly, so
evidently the 24th inst. is a vital spot. Yours,
&c., Titus Salt.
From the Star’s (Sydney) report of the
latest Melb. wife-murder Toner resided m
the house with his wife and a blackfellow and his
mistress. He returned home and had an alterca-
tion with his wife, and in his mad passion seized

was powerless to interfere.” What an epitaph :

mistress.”
A Ballarat (Vic.) bench has decided that a
man who sold to a local pawnbroker a silver-gilt
chain, which the wretched Mosenstein paid for as
gold, has committed no offence because he didn b
actually say it was gold. He simply kept silent
and lay low, and there is nothing about lyiner low
in the Act. Yet in the same city a silent China-
man, who sold a leaden brick neatly gilt over, is
now doing two years. And how does the new
reading of the law apply to a man who silently
passes a bogus sovereign or a forged cheque?
Mrs. Mellon, despite all said to the con-
trary, is in the best of health, and will within the
next few days hold a private seance, to which only
a few of the'” faithful ” have been invited. There-
after, she proposes submitting to a thorough test,
the conditions of which have been mutually agreed
upon between the medium’s husband and a certain
committee. Mrs. Mellon has any quantity of
assurance, and says she will either stand or fall by
the test seance ; but feels sure when it is over
that she will be “triumphantly vindicated.”
“Great embarrassment is being caused in
the Cabinet by the conduct of Mr. Frederick Sar-
good, both in his place in Parliament and by his
utterances outside,” snorts Melb. Age. The
impossible soft-goods colonel will have to gather
up his cocked hat and go, one of these days. He
glories in the idea of doing it, too. Sargood has
informed the Leg. Council that if the one man
one vote question ever comes up he will oppose it,
and may even resign as before. Nobody will care
whether he resigns as before, provided that the
calamity occurs soon.
There is one Vic. officer who is glad at the
departure of Major-General Tulloch. This swash-
buckler and Tulloch, the story goes, were never
friends after a snub the General administeted
when he first arrived. The warrior entered
Tulloeh’s room at the barracks one day, whistling
gaily, and called out in his bluff way, ‘ Good
morning, General.” “Who are you? ’ enquired
the General. “I am Colonel was the
reply. “Oh, then, Colonel, when I want you, I
shall not whistle for you.” This was firing low
and laying him out! The Colonel nursed that
snub, and Tulloch was rather proud of having ad-
ministered it.
Old John Walter, of London Times, news
of whose death is cabled, owned Printing House
Square and the machinery with which the
Thunderer is printed. Although the Times is
now owned by a company, John Walter re-
tained the controlling power. He owned three-
sixteenths of the shares of the paper, and
other members of the Walter family own eleven-
sixteenths. Two-sixteenths only were held by
outsiders. When he was in America, some years
ago, Walter admitted that the circulation of the
paper was only 50,000 a day. It was the late
John Walter who first introduced the system
of multiplying stereotype - plates for printing
newspapers, and also the cylindrical form of print-
ing presses by which a roll of paper can be printed
on both sides at once. One of the young Walters
was in Melbourne awhile ago, and is now in
Maoriland seeking health. He is a Theosophisb.
Sydney Daily Telegraph emits a fierce
and joyful whoop because Vie. Premier Turner,
alleged Protectionist, says nothing about Protec-
tion in his budget. Yet it also “goo-goos” with
satisfaction because N.S.W. Premier Reid, cham-
pion Freetrader, says nothing about Freetrade in
his budget. This would be curious enough if the
two issues claimed to be on similar footings. But,
whereas Freetrade is idealised as an “ eternal
principle”—a sort of essence of the_ “justice-
though-the-heavens-fall ” verity,” an incumbent
duty to be fulfilled at any cost and no matter how
many victims are sacrificed, while Protection is
only’categorised as a policy of expediency, the
incidence of which must be varied according to
the various needs of the community, it is more
curious still. For, it is evident that there may
exist a nation so peculiarly placed that Freetrade
would be its policy of expediency. Or there may
exist a people so judiciously protected that its
financial pilot has no need to introduce further
protective variations. But, if Freetrade is at once
a humane and quasi-religious duty (as alleged by
the D.T. ad nauseam) and a universal panacea for
all political difficulties (as also reiterated by that
organ), Reid’s action in even temporarily main-
taining the present N.S.W. Protective duties can-
not be justified, and the plea of keeping “dirty
water until he gets clean ” is a meaningless flower
of rhetoric.
The suicide in Melbourne, last week, of
Major A. Russell Inglis (husband of one of the
daughters of a very celebrated doctor) recalls
memories of the Melb. Cup of ’73. Russell Inglis
was then an articled clerk in the office of a Melb,
legal firm; and James Wilson, who was one
of his bosom friends, consented to run “ Don
Juan” (which Inglis had bought privately) for the
Cup. “Don Juan” won, and Inglis and Joe
Thompson scooped no end of money. It was
this win that made Thompson, and to celebrate
the event he built a mansion in East Melbourne,
which he called after Byron’s amorous hero.
Inglis retired from the law and went in for
squatting. His suicide coming so soon after this
year’s Cup suggests one thing pretty plainly—
that his good fortune of 21 years ago was not re-
peated—though his bad turf luck was probably
not his only reason for killing himself.
The Bulletin Christmas Number, to be
published Dec. 15, will be original from cover to
cover ; the track of the kangaroo will adorn every
page on which the emu hasn’t already built a nest;
and all the old Bulletin favorites have combined
with picture, song, and story, to cram as much
miscellaneous joy into it as 44 pages will hold.
Non-subscribers are requested to order at once
from any bookseller, or to send seven stamps to
The Bulletin office for postage to any address.

comparative sombre character of feminine ap-
parel at the Vic. Parliament opening For
instance : “Lady Hopetoun wore a pale-green
dress with a pink scarf, and a straw hat trimmed
with black ostrich feathers, and so in dress was in
harmony with the general quietness.”
Many persons and. newspapers confounded
Dean McCarthy, killed in Red fern railway acci
dent, with the burly Father “Tim” McCarthy,
the friend of Archbishop Folding, who did good
work in the Carcoar district in the bushranging
days. Yane, the ’ranger, stuck him up uninten-
tionally and then surrendered to him. Father
“Tim,” after a dispute with Archbishop Yaughan
over a funeral oration upon Dr. Folding, went to
Ireland and died in his sister’s house in Cork.
Ere departing for ’Ome to the blare of
trumpets and the beat of drums, Major-General
Tulloch, Victoria’s ex-commandant, lectured on
the passing of the children of Israel through the
Red Sea, and proved the truth of the Mosaic story
to the complete satisfaction of the Argus. The
“ Majah ” founded his little song on the fact that
he once saw a shallow lake blown away by a steady
desert wind, and, he said, a similar wind blowing
from the East would produce “ exactly the effect
represented ”in the Bible. Exodus says, “ And
the waters were a wall unto them on their right
hand, and on their left.” The wind that would
keep two such walls standing would have blown
the children into the heart of Africa. The faith-
ful aren’t grateful to Tulloch for trying to explain
away their favorite miracle. The harder a thing
is to believe, the more credit the truly pious find
in believing it,
The Vic. Council has commenced to gnash
its store-teeth at Turner’s programme, C. J.
Ham demands stringent instead of
rigorous taxation, and talks, with tears in his eyes,
of suburban property which is being “ sold for the
mere rates,” and people who offer to break stones
in lieu of rates they cannot pay. Those people, as
it happens, will not be hurt by the proposed land-
tax, whilst those others who will get wounded in
their tenderest spot are not likely to ask for n
stone-breaking job—but no matter. The joke is
to hear Ham, of all people, spouting thus ; for Ham
was one of the chief creators of unearned increment
(mostly bogus, as it now appears) in the boom-
time. He was one of those who valued the big
properties, and solemnly declared that a man who
bought 50 acres of land in 1887 was about .£50,000
the richer by his purchase in 1888. Perhaps he
would have been, if he could have realised. The
careers of land-auctioneers and dealers have been
one everlasting argument in favor of a stiff land-
tax.
Sydney jobbing printers’ strike has begun
to fizzle. It’s a pity ; but what can the men do!
The offices are filled with “rats,” who are
rapidly learning to be useful, and the great hog-
public (including N.S. \V. Railway Commis-
sioners, by the bye) follows its snout to the
cheapest market. In the early days of the strike,
when a 25-sheet poster came into an office where
25 raw hands were employed, three men were
started on the job, but, after they had struggled
for an hour, the whole 25 were on it. They
took a show of hands as to the best way
to set it, and the majority decided to
lay it all out on the floor. And they
did—2s sheets of it, one man to a sheet.
Probably they’d have tried to lock it up
on the floor as one forme, only the office hap-
pened to be out of 25-sheet poster chases. But
these things don’t happen every day ; and even
the yarn about another bright importation who
thought it a waste of time to take off the page-
cord before locking up a job—didn’t the cord act
as a buffer and save the type from being battered
by the furniture? —has failed to revive the
flagging spirits of the strikers. Nothing has come
of the co-operative project, and the chances are
the strike will die by degrees for want of breath—
and bread. It’s a pity ; but what can the men do?
Dear Bulletin, — What’s the use of prating
about “ written instructions ” when cut-throat
competition, both for steamer-trade and steamer-
billets, is going on, and a captain may think,
however erroneously, that it’s as much as his post’s
worth to make a slow passage? Suppose yourself
a captain, called into the manager’s office, and
this colloquy following :—Manager : “ I see you
were four hours late last trip, Capt. Nautilus?”
Captain : “ Yes, sir ; the weather was very thick,
and I couldn’t pick up the lights, and, as you
know, the instructions tell us to look to safety
first, and .” M.; “Oh, yes, to be sure, the
instructions. But, how is it that Oceanicus
managed it with his boat? It gives the opposi-
tion a bad lead, you know, Nautilus.” 0. :
“Well, sir, he must have taken a lot of risk.”
M.: “ He got there all right, anyhow, and it will
seem strange to the public that our boat can’t do
as much. The reputation of the line must be kept
up, you know —nothing people hate worse than a
slow boat. That will do, Mr. Nautilus.” A
month elapses, and the captain is again called up.
M.; “Look here, Nautilus, it seems only last
week that you kept the boat for a bit of
mist or something, and here she’s six hours late
again—this won’t do, you know. Here’s Oceani-
cus starts at the same time as you and gets in six
hours ahead.” C.: “Well, sir, you know he has
the devil’s own luck, and our instructions
M.: “Oh, damme, don’t put the blame on the in-
structions ! How do you think Oceanicus does it ?
If he can, I suppose you can, and that’s the long

Literary men, as well as' artists and men
who have to think much, arejconsiderably given
to the love of tobacco —to wit, R. L. Stevenson,
Rudyard Kipling and Prince Bismarck, French
statesmen form the exception. Marshal Mac-
Mahon had to abandon it for the sake of his
health; Carnot for his wife’s sake; and Thiers
never used tobacco. But it is only fair to add
that they did not live to see the birth of the
Cavour cigars. Smokers are more fortunate in
that respect here in Sydney, where this refreshing
and fragrant smoke is obtainable everywhere.
They are sold in packets of eight for Is.
The Melbourne Cup of 1894 was won as
much by the jockey as by the horse. Dawes was
not in a hurry with his mount, and, riding with
great nerve and judgment, never troubled him
until a mile and a-half had been travelled, when,
with consummate judgment, he worked him into
an excellent position. He handled the son of
Grand Flaneur with great skill, brought him on
just at the right moment, and catching flying
Devon, wore him down in the last twenty strides.
“It’s dogged as does it,” and the best man is the
man that can pound the longest. Who knows how
the great race would have gone had Dawes got
flurried, or had his splendid nerve not been
strengthened and perfected by that miraculous
stimulant and sustainer, Marshall’s Tonic Nei vine?

Mr. Robert-Lucas-Tanglefoot-

The contest for the Mayo-
ralty of Sydney this year lies
between Alderman Matthew
Harris, who is very rich by
heredity, and Mr. Samuel Lees,
M.P., who is a teetotaller. Both
are nobodies, from an outside
point of view, and it is dreadful
to think that either of them
should be shoved forward to re-
present a big city. However,
consolation must be sought in
the fact that nobody who is any-
body was ever an alderman.
W. A. Hamilton, new
member for Bendigo in Vic.
Assembly, a coach-builder by
trade, hails from S.A., and is
young but not so young as he
looks. He has some admini-
strative ability. Boycotted
when sec. of Bendigo Trades
Hall at the time of the mari-
time strike, he started a co-
operative bakery and engineered
it to complete success. It has
now 518 members, and to meet
their growing demand the society
has built what is probably the
largest and best - appointed
bakery in Australia. Trade-
societies all over Australasia
should study the history of
Hamilton’s co-operative bakery.

over .£lOOO per annum. His £2 per day travelling

abolish the practice of giving brandy to criminals
just before execution. Presumably Wood is
afraid that the hangee will be so muddled by drink
that he will lurch against the gate of Sheol.

was to meet Adelaide’s most-recent lordly visitor,

turned his back on me and began picking his
teeth !”
Dr. Odillo Maher, whose fame as an oculist
is Australasian, has not by any means recovered
from the injuries sustained by him in the Redfern
railway accident, and as he has long been low in
health his condition gives anxiety to his medical
friends.

case, Pillow v. Pillow. The other day the case of
Bolster v. Bolster was listed. A sad state of
things is evidently prevailing among the bed-
clothes.

“They say, Mr. Black, that you are like
the Wandering Jew ?” said a venerable statesman
to Black, M.P., in N.S.W. Parliamentary Library
the other night. “Well,” returned Black, “ you
ought to know—you ought by now to be quite a
connoisseur in Jews.”

Tasmanian Parliament the “rig” of one of the

duct of the ceremony consisted of a black dress-
coat, black (ordinary) waistcoat, light trousers and
white belltopper ! Economy ?
Blood and blubber ! Apropos last week’s
cable re the attempted bombing of Hanging Judge
Hawkins, The Bulletin reads a London news-
paper article saying that “there lives no more
tender-hearted man than Sir Henry Hawkins.”
How tender-hearted all these hangmen are !
Tas. Governor Gormanston (says a
“towney”) was, as a schoolboy, tremendously
fat, and now, in his mature years, he doesn’t seem
to have departed from the solid standard of
youth. He hails from County Meath, where his
family are Irish, very poor, and highly honored.
Gov. Gormanston’s father was known as “The
Gooseberry Lord,” through most of his small
income coming from the gooseberries grown on his
estate.
N.S.W. Justice Foster—who has just had
a year’s leave of absence on full pay—is now
applying for more leave. The almost immediate
end of the matter doubtless will be that this gen-
tleman, who, considering his legal mediocrity,
milk-and-water character, advanced age, and
physical infirmity, ought never to have been ap-
pointed to the Bench at all, will retire on a life-
pension of £lBOO a year in consideration of having,
for seven or eight years, been so kind as to draw
£2600 a year whether he was on leave in Europe
or not. The next N.S.W. Supreme Court Judge
should be a man of well under 50 years, certified
by a big life-assurance co. to have before him at
least 15 years’ expectation of vigorous work. The
community sympathises with Mr. Justice Foster
in his bad health, but it is not prepared to sympa-
thise with him or anybody else to the extent of
£36 per week pension, which he has not haP
earned.

As it was Prince of Wales’ birthday, 14
“drunks” were sentenced to only lOmin. each at
Sydney Central on 9th Nov. This is possibly the
most delicate and agreeable compliment which
Albert Edward has yet had paid to him. The
Bench itself should get drunk next birthday.
They all do it, and some of them do it in
a hurry. N.S.W. Premier Reid, only a month or
two in office, has already had a life-sized portrait
of himself painted—for Sydney National Gallery,
so it is indicated in the Ministerial organ. The
extreme modesty of this move, likewise the dip-
lomacy thereof, will be evident to the meanest
intelligence.
A lately-deposed Melb. pen-lady returned
her cards of invitation to the race-week functions
at Govt. House. Secretary Wallington wrote
acknowledging the lady’s courtesy and said : “His
Ex. and Lady Hopetoun would be glad to extend
the invitations to you privately but can see no
record of your name in the visitors’ book.” He
had only looked back a month for her name.
Etiquette of this kind is the very breath of Govt.
House nostrils. Society and the public are
curious to see if the next Governor is going to in-
sist on the visitors’-book farce. The Lochs didn’t,
though they entertained much more than the
Hopetouns. But Lady Loch went for the Popu-
larity Stakes and won hands down, while Lady
H. has not even gone into training.

fesses to be signed by another political corruptor,

burys.

jle turned up at Govt. House (Vic.) ball without

An early applicant for admission to practice
as a solicitor in N.S.W, will be Mr. Kong Sing, a
young Chinese, chiefly educated at Newington
College, Sydney. Miss Lan Hoy Ah Ghee, the
16-year-old daughter of a Tumut (N.S.W.)
Chinese merchant, passed the recent Trinity
College musical examination, Sydney, with 95 out
of a possible 100 marks.
Mr, E. J. Ball, formerly M.P. for Goul-
burn in N.S. W. Assembly, and twice Mayor of
Goulburn, died last week. He was a barber by
trade, and not above his business, for when Lord
Augustus Loftus visited Goulburn as Governor,
and was received in state by the local corporation,
H.E. recognised in Mayor Ball the tonsorial
artist who had scraped his chin earlier in the day.

During Hancock’s last term in
Yic. Parliament, he remarked, re the
bank crisis, that all necessary to start a
bank was “ a hook-nose and a counter.”
Just before his last contest at Foots-
cray he arranged for the hire of a local
hall for .£l, and the proprietor, a Jew,
inquired his name. “ Hancock,” replied
John. “Hancock! John Hancock!”
yelled the infuriated Hebrew. “Hook-
nose and a counter. I know. Well,
you can’t have this ’all under 305.”
John declined, and held an open-air
meeting instead. That Semitic land-
lord is now despised by the rest of his
race for allowing personal feeling to
interfere with “pishness.”
Dear Bulletin, Don’t you
think the fascinating Mrs. Besant quite
overdoes the photo, business ? I have
seen about a score of different** studies ”
of the gifted priestess, and they smack
of mummery and advertising, and give
proof of the fact that A.B. is not
superior to one little womanly weak-
ness at least. In the portraits that have
come under my notice the subject wears
many different expressions—the ma-
ternal, the pensively thoughtful, the
intense, and the alert being most
favored ; and the query naturally arises,
does the photo, artist follow Mrs.
Annie about, and catch the expressions
as they occur, or does the lady qualify-
ing for a Mahatma go to a studio in
cold blood, like Mrs. Brough, and de-
liberately assume expressions to order ?
—S. S.

Sydney Evening News editor Hogue,
member for Glebe in N.S.W. Assembly, is court-
ing local popularity with a vengeance, and is now
a never-failing chairman at Bands of Hope, cold-
water spreads and mothers’ meetings. When the
parson tells of the boy who was born in the slums,
and died a millionaire because he never drank
anything stronger than water and weak tea, James
smiles angelically.
A somewhat sycophantic pressman with
an ambition to do solid work has compiled some
pages on ‘‘Great Men I Have Known,” among
whom are Parkes and Gladstone. Fishing for
materials lately he informed Parkes, at the end of
a long interview, that be proposed heading a
particular section with “The Australian Glad-
stone.” The old man held up a finger and pro-
cured a pause. “ Wouldn’t it be better,” he
inquired slowly after a minute’s silence, “ to put
something else instead of that and to head the
Gladstone section with ‘The English Parkes?
It is not many months since The Bulletin
had to contradict a report, then current, that
Gabriel Read, discoverer of the Otago goldfields,
was dead. Now it has to record the demise of the
veteran, on Oct. 31, at New Norfolk (Tas,), where
also he was born. Through the old man’s life—he
was over 70 when he died —ran a heavy vein of
sadness, for he suffered from an intermittent
mental malady, the result of a fall on the hunting-
field in his youth, which at times took charge of
him and drove him to extremes of violence and
eccentricity. The full consciousness of his defect.
A Coolgardie Celebrity : WARDEN FINNERTY,
which Read possessed, was always a black cloud
over his life, and haunted him till his death. He
died among kind friends, who smoothed the way
to the country where a prospector pegs out once
for all.

The late Martha Needle, it seems, was not
Quite the circumspect widow, or tenderly-affeo-
tionate mother, represented by her counsel. On
the night of her youngest child’s passing hence
she had a little outing with a gentleman. Imme-
diately on her return home, Martha went to the
bedside of her offspring and forced another dose
of physic between the patient’s unwilling teeth.
That was the finishing touch to the mysterious
sickness of Miss Needle, who shortly afterwards
expired.

Take a couple of Fletcher’s Pills every second
night; they are a prompt and sure cure and give
certain relief.
FLETCHER’S PILLS are a quick, sure and
reliable remedy for all complaints arising from
torpid liver, indigestion, weak stomach, and
impurities of the blood. They are prepared on
rational and sensible lines and do not contain a
particle of calomel, gamboge, or any drastic
purgative, but operate by perfectly natural means,
by stimulating the liver and increasing the flow of
bile and thus producing easy evacuations and
natural catharses, without the annoyance and pain
of griping and purging.
Beware of the many harmful substitutes on sale
in many shops. None are genuine unless they
bear the signature of F. Moore, Clements on the
outside wrapper. CLEMENTS TONIC and
FLETCHER S PILLS have become such house-
hold words that imitations are frequently offered.
We contend that this imitation is the sincerest
proof of their virtues, so see that you always get
the genuine, bearing the name and address of F. M.
Clements, Newtown, Sydney, N.S.W.
HOW TO KEEP CHEERFUL.
Cheerfulness is preferable to mirth. Mirth is
an ac t —short and transient. Cheerfulness is a
habit of the mind—fixed and permanent. A
cheerful man is not only disposed to be affable
and obliging, but raises the same good humor in
those who come within his influence. You cannot
help liking him. People endowed with cheerful-
ness should take care how they cultivate it, or,
like a delicate plant, it will wither. They, on the
contrary, foster their happy inclinations by
smoking Frossard’s Cavour Cigars, which are as
pleasant as their own temper, and as aromatic as
the finest havanas, although costing one shilling
only for a packet of eight.

Glassey reprobates, in Q. Parliament, the
idea of spending public funds to help back the
unrealised ideals from Paraguay, on the ground
that there are worse off realities in Queensland.
Glassey’s position is an interesting instance of
sympathy with a cause masquerading as antipathy
to its promoters.
Sydney Telegraph, in last
Frday’s leader, states —what is
quite true—that Premier Turner
does not propose to interfere in
any way with the existing pro-
tective tariff in Victoria. Then
a few sentences further on :
“Next to the manner in which
the new Vio, Government turns
its back on Protection the most
striking feature about these pro-
posals,” &c. Moral, maintain-
ing Protection is turning your
back on it. And maintaining
Freetrade is ?
Says Zeal, M.L.O. (Vic-
toria): “If the railways were
leased to-morrow they could be
worked at one - half the cost,
with one-half the labour, and
double the profit at present ob-
taining” (Cheers). Which, if it
were true—of course, it isn’t
true—only means that they
would be worked at one-half the
cost, said cost representing
money spent in Victoria ; with
one-half the labor, so that only
one-half the wages would be
spent in Victoria; and at double
the profit, which profit would be
taken out of the country to pay
divs. to an English syndicate.
At present, by the way, the Vio.
railways yield no profit at all,
and twice nothing is how
much ? And, just here, it
occurs to The Bulletin that if
Zeal were leased to a syndicate
which had absolute power over
him he would probably do about
six times the work he has
hitherto done, and at much less
than half the cost, and would
Produce 32 times the profit he
has hitherto realised. Leasing
the railways also means leasing
the railway employes, and that
18 just as moral as leasing Zeal
to someone who would probably
"se him for brick-carrying pur-
poses for 16 hours per diem.

fallen out of three more Vic. village settlements.
The Leongatha settlement, during last year, earned
•£54 by the sale of vegetables, and cost .£5BOO.
About £5OOO came in the shape of Govt, grants
3nd loans and public contributions, and the rest
seems to have been mo-tly the original capital of
the settlers. The Tucker village settlements, at
Ee l Hill and Wonwondah, are now valued, with
a h improvements, at .£2lOO, and unless the State
comes to the rescue they are likely to be seized
and sold by .£5OOO worth of bowellesa creditor.

taking him out to a patch of indifferent land, there
to grow turnips by instinct, may be pastoral and
benevolent, but it pays no dividends.

Premier Turner (Vic.) absolutely shone on
his Budget, capsizing all the anticipations of the
Patterson crowd. There was the one indispens-
able quality of lucidity, and Turner, at a bound,
leaps into the position of Victoria’s heaven-sent
financier. Even the Argus is charmed.
Tonkin’s Amalgamation of Legal Profes-
sions Bill, down for second reading on Dec. 4 in
N.S.W, Assembly, may be worth passing, but as
regards its claim to cheapen law for the public
one need only look at Queensland, where Lumley
Hill carried a similar bill with a similar claim a
dozen years ago.
One mischief about the pensioner is that
he enters into unfair competition with people in
business. A horde is turned loose by the Vic.
Government to recruit the assemblage. They
have more than enough to live on, but yet perforce
must dabble in trade, because comparatively few
are men past work.
Is Australia as drunken, and France as
sober, as Max O’Rell asserts? According to
“Mulhall’s Statistics,” the consumption of all
kinds of liquor in France is equivalent to 3 - 5 gals,

“If England demand taxation without
representation,” says Edmund Barton, “then
Australia might claim independence.” Bub Bar-
ton favors the appointment of a Governor-General
by England, acting solely on her own motion, and
paid for by taxation of the Australian people.
That comes close to the given hypothesis.
In apparent contrast to the Pitt-town
(N.S.W.) labor settlement is the progress made
under the Queensland Co-operative Communities
Bill. Minister for Lands Barlow (no friendly
critic) speaks in high terms of the work done and
progress made at all the settlements save two.
But several of the Q. settlements are bound firmly
by religion.
N.S.W. Premier Reid has promised to
demonstrate his economising hona-fides by intro-
ducing the Maoriland method of voting the Sup-
plementary Estimates, Ministers are to be limited
to .£50,000 for “unforeseen expenditure,” and are
to be personally liable for any sum they may spend
over and above the allowance and without Parha-
tary sanction.
When the present N.S.W. Premier, G. H.
Reid, first came over to democracy—about the
time the solid club plural-vote was knocked out in
East Sydney—he cast about for some action to
prove the genuineness of bis conversion. “ I have
it!” said he, after much thought; and he forth-
with drew his parliamentary salary, which up to
then he had scorned.

he got gratis from N.S.W. Govt. Printing Office
and distributed at Tamworth during his election
campaign. Among other things, the writer states
he admired George’s sturdy “independence,”

National Defence League, banks, financial
institutions, and the general champions of pro-
perty have used, and are using, all kinds of
influence to secure the defeat of the Taxation Bill
by the S.A. Legislature. They roped in several
Assembly waverers, and in tbe Council managed
to “stiffen” some alleeed Liberals. However,
up to date, the measure still lives.
Sydney Daily Telegraph
The announcement of a definite date for tariff changes
and direct taxation to come into force, has inspired
confidence.
Does the Telegraph wish to kill the Reid Govt. ?
It would seem so. “ Inspired confidence !” The
phrase stinks. Anyone who deliberately “inspires
confidence,” nowadays, deserves gaol.
S.A. declines to accept the 2d. rate of
postage to England, because she cannot afford the
consequent loss of .£lOOO per year. That sounds
rather paltry —still the general public would get
greater benefit from the reduction of the local
postage than in the saving of on their foreign
correspondence. An agitation for Id. postage
within the limits of Adelaide has already begun.
Among the N.S. Wales M.’sP. who made
most fuss about the alleged employment of ex-
members of the late Parliament in Govt. Depart-
ments, was one man just re-elected who himself
got a Govt, appointment after a former defeat,
and resigned it to run for Parliament. Had ho
been defeated he would, in all probability, have
retired to his snug billet until another election
came round.

Dear Bulletin,—How is it that even
Parkes never noticed that payment of members
would play ducks and drakes with the Constitu-
tion? Bagehot, the foremost authority, says the
Americans tried to copy the English Constitution,
and succeeded in producing a far different one.
Through their representatives the English people
can turn out a Ministry. In ninety-nine cases onb
of a hundred Aberdeen would have been a better
Prime Minister than Palmerston. But the him-
dredth case, the Crimean crisis, arose, and “ the
Amerite.
This would i n i PF during a fixed
S 4 italtoir.rSi, I* people
would have tur A out his successor if they had
the power. But they hadn’t, and were forced to
wait years to do what the English did in as
many days. Now, payment of members tends to
keep the' Ministry in power until Parliament
expires, and thus produces the effect noted by
Bagehot.—Z.

Dear Bulletin, —Your recent par., com-
paring Moran’s action in W.A. politics with De
Hamel’s, is not warranted when the seamy side of
the latter is shewn up. De Hamel only went in
opposition when he found that he was not to be
included in the first Ministry formed by Forrest
on responsible lines. He put it about that he was
sure of being Attorney-General, and used this cry
to help his return against Thomas Campbell, a
far more sincere and accomplished politician, who
was great for the single Chamber and the referen-
dum. De Hamel will advocate any democratic
measure, no matter how advanced ; and yet, when
he landed in Albany, about six years ago, the
writer was one of a few who had a perusal of his
newspaper scrap alburn, where he proudly exhi-
bited a report, several columns long, of a banquet
tendered him by the Newcastle-on-Tyne Con-
servative Club, full of grateful allusions to his
services to the party. Another big item was his
presentation at Court, and, to emphasise the last,
at the opening of Parliament De Hamel was the
only member in the whole House who appeared in
Court-dress. Heaven help W.A. when she has to
call on such men to lead her. Better borrow
Henry Parkes. —Yours,
BLACKBOY.

Yesterday morning the Premier received a deputation
representing the Licensee! Victuallers’ Association to
protest against the introduction of a local option bill
without compensate...
Mr, Varney Parkes said literature of every country was
full of traditions of the old business. The old tavern of Old i
England had been one of the oldest associations of all the 1
sentiments of British-speaking people. It would be a very
sad thing, he thought, to destroy that old business from the
opposite sentiment, which said that a man should not drink
and avail himself of the pleasures he had been used to get
in.all generations out of public-houses.

Society
The R.C. nuns are moving very cautiously
towards trousers. At a recent Melb. convent
entertainment some 30 or 40 damsels had to take
the parts of men. Whether huntsman, king,
squire, or knave, each maiden was attired to the
waist in masculine garments, but from the waist
downwards there was a skirt continuation.
* * *
The daughter of an eminent Brisbane
citizen and M.L.C. is about to be married. The
lady’s wedding-dress is a perfect marvel of gor-
geousness, and is to be dedicated (in imitation of
a Continental custom) to the church in which the
ceremony is performed as a decoration for the
altar. How will this work when the sex has
donned the breeches ?
* • #
The Journal, which is the evening issue
of the S.A. Register, turned its column-rules
and went into solemn black because of the death
of the Czar; but no special mark of mourning
was exhibited over the death of 130 Australians in
the Wai rarapa. Why?
* * *
When Women’s Suffrage comes will the
women still retain the first right of entry into the
life-boats in time of wreck at sea? The spirit of
Maoriland woman-suffrage seems to have brooded
over the wreck of the Wairarapa.
• « •
Woman’s rights and privileges again. S. A.
Register recently wrote of “ the late Mrs.
Needle.” Why not also of “the late Mr. Deem-
ing? ”
• • »
Even in these days of economy vice-
royalty has privileges. A special train was put
on to and from Adelaide to enable Gov. Robinson,
a passenger by the mail-steamer which onlv stayed
at Largs Bay a few hours, to attend Lord Kin-
tore’s garden-party to the Gormanstons.
* # *
The Bulletin hears that when the S.M.
Herald compositors were informed by the
manager, through their officers, that the Hat-
tersley composing and distributing machine was
to be introduced into the office by Xmas, they
were not much surprised, but they were taken
aback when told that it was intended that girls
were to work the distributors, and that any
employd having a daughter eligible to learn the
work would have the preference. The men cleai ly
perceive the subtlety of the arrangement, which
will prevent the Typo. Society having any in-
fluence over the girls ; it will also, they think, so
reduce wages as to debar men from accepting
employment.

N.S.W. Judge Windeyer, in re the Te Kloot
case, has decided that it is not fair to refuse a sue-
cessful petitioner his divorce because he has failed
to pay up the costs, said failure arising because
petitioner (who has had to provide his wife with
the cash necessary to carry on the war against
hirnself) had no money of his own, and couldn’t
get his own costs from the insolvent or evanished
co-respondent. Therefore, it is definitely settled
that a divorce isn’t an article of merchandise to be
handed over in return for the costs on the C.O.D.
principle. This may be law but it isn’t business.
That rule absolute should have been put up for
auction to satisfy the sharks unpaid bill Ihere
are any number of people in N.S. Wales who
would pay quite a large sum for a divorce if it
could be had without the usual law proceedings.
’ ' ’ * ‘ ‘ *
Fancy Judge Windeyer auctioning that decree :
“ For positive sale, at the risk of former pur-
chaser, a rule absolute; quite new; never been
worn; constructed of the best materials and
warranted durable; can be altered to suit the
size of the buyer. Cohen—Cohen —Kohn 1 ” Or,
Te Kloot might have been granted his rule abso-
lute with permission to the unpaid alligator of
the law to put in a bailiff and seize it in settle-
ment of his bill. A lawyer can seize pretty nearly
everything else in the wide world for his unpaid
bill, and if he isn’t allowed to seize upon a man’s
divorce then there is obviously sometliing wrong.
# . * *
Wholesale gambling has been going on in
connection with St. Patrick’s Cathedral Fair
(Melb.). The promoters of “art-unions ” held in
connection with the A. N. A. annual demonstration
were fined by the courts, but the church gambles
for big money prizes are smiled on by the law.
Games of chance, such as are rigorously sup-
pressed by the police on Flemington Flat, are
practised at the fair with perfect impunity ; heaps
of gold and notes are seen on the teetotum table.
It is difficult to understand how what is immoral
in the city and on the racecourse becomes sancti-
fied when pursued in the interests of the professed
teachers of morality. Archbishop Carr and
Barney Allen are really in the very same line of
business when the matter comes to be looked at
squarely.
# * *
That Cathedral fancy-fair ought to pan out
a good div. The race-game is run with 14 sub-
scribers at Is. each, but the winner only gets Bs.
as his prize, 6s. going to the machine. Talk about
the totalisator !
• • •
When they asked Martha Needle on the
scaffold if she had any remarks to make, she
replied according to the usual formula ; “ I have
nothing to say.” They always ask if he (or she)
has anything to say, and in the very few cases
where the prisoner has a desire to make a few
observations to the reporters, the law allows him
to get them off his chest without opposition. But
some day or other a prisoner with a few remarks
to make will stand up on the gallows and hold
forth with an easy flow of eloquence for nine
hours, like Jawbone Neild, till the hangman and
the sheriff and the other officials are nearly drop-
ping from fatigue and hunger, and it is too late
to report the execution in the evening papers.
Then they will give up asking if the prisoner has
anything to say.
* * *
Fitzroy (Vic.) policeman Devine, pleading
for an “exemplary punishment” upon a poor
wretch who had attempted suicide, remarked that
the offence was becoming very frequent; people
seemed to think it a trifling one.” Policeman
Devine is a zealous officer, who would not hesitate
to arrest Death.

Anti-cremationists are now busy pointing
out that if the body of Louis Juncken had been
burned instead of being interred, there would
have been no possibility of exhumation, and the
arsenic in the deceased would never have been
discovered. The cremationists point out, on the
other hand, that just because there is no evidence
to be got after combustion, the law, when man’s
destination is ashes instead of dust, will hold a
post mortem on every deceased, and under their
system the poisoning of Juncken would have been
discos'ered long ago, also that of many other per-
sons who haven’t been exhumed and never will
be. Here the cremationists have decidedly the
best of the argument. Everybody ought to be
post-mortemed before burial. For one Juncken

The steamers that go fcn Westralia these
times have their decka loaded forward with
cattle,'sheep, horses, machinery, fodder, and all
manner of cargo. Sometimes there is no deck
space whatever lefb for the stee rage passengers
who have to any exercise they want on top of
the deck-loading. In some cases the boats are
inaccessible, and the gratings which are supposed
to afford air to the stifling mass of humanity
below are partially covered, and in one instance,
according to the report of the Adelaide Marine
Board, even the poop was partly blocked with
sheep-pens and other loading. Apparently there
is all the raw material for a first-class tragedy on
the W. A. coast the first time a respectable cyclone
comes along.

thrice, and crying ‘Buz,’ he could takb away a
man’s life (though, in truth, he could do no such
thing) yet this were a just law made by the State,

* * *
One N.S.W. Judge, two ex-Minisfcers of
the Crown, one religiously-inclined Under-Secre-
tary, several M.sL.O. and M.sP. were turned
quite silly-side-out by Mrs. Spook-raise Mellon,
and one member of the N.S.W. Legislative Coun-
cil so frequently attended the Mellon seances
that he is now perfectly crazed on the subject.
Every night he goes to bed with a slate under
his pillow, and hopes against hope for a written
message from his dead son. A certain Judge’s
wife was as hopeless a victim as any. Forbidden
by her husband to recognise the Mellons, she
actually continued, while the Judge was on cir-
cuit, bo hold Mellon seances in her own house.
Now that the exposure has come the lady is biting
her fingers. One of the most unfortunate of Mrs.
Mellon’s victims is the legal dignitary who was
so impressed by the manifestations that a year ago
he engineered a J.P.-ship for Mr. Mellon.
• * •
The Melb. Institute of Journalists is about
on its last legs. The amount of members’ sub-
scriptions in arrears is £230, and those who owe
say they don’t see the force of paying up to an
organisation that has never done a single thing to
justify its existence, and has not proved of the
slightest use to any mortal pressman. The idea
of making a close profession of journalism was
doomed to failure from the very beginning.
• • #
Confidence in Chow doctors is pending re-
construction at Ballarat. Wing Fat dosed a man
for miner’s disease, and the man died right off.
The Govt, analyst reports that Wing’s “ medicine”
was composed of treacle, liquorice, and sugar-
candy. The writer knows a family that favors a
Little Bourke-st. Chow medico because he gives
such a lot of medicine for the money. He deals
out his vile stuff in two-quart bottles and orders
half-pint doses.
m * *
The Mayor proper of a certain large Aus-
tralian city has abandoned, with sorrow, the hope
of getting his boots satisfactorily soled and heeled
for half-a-crown. For many years he groaned
under the infliction of three shillings for repairs
neatly executed, and awhile ago he transferred
his patronage to a cobbler to hose fee was sixpence
less. Finding, however, that the new man’s soles
were not watertight, the magnate returned to
the other artist with an offer to let him have the
job as of yore at the lower rate. “Mr. Blank,”
said the old reliable bootmender, “the leather
costs one - and - tenpence (unless I give you
brown paper), and do you think eightpence is
fair pay for the labor?” The Mayor, as a right-
thinking person, argued that eightpence was nob
to be sneezed at in times of depression, bub he
finally agreed to bang the odd saxpence.

A ladies’ Orange Lodge, the first of its
kind, was started in Sydney a week or so ago.
Forty-two dames and demoiselles gave in their
names. There is talk of inaugurating a ladies’
Freemasons Lodge also. So endeth the reign of
guzzling buffoonery and silly secrecy.
• * «
Since the passing, in 1890, of the Shiels
Divorce Act (Vic.), 635 suits have been instituted,
as against 164 during the four years previous to
the passage of that statute.
• • «
Undoubtedly one of the lessons of the
Redfern, just as of the Windsor (Vic.) accident,
four years ago, is that locomotives should always
be run funnel, and never bunker, first. In the
latter disaster the driver and firemen undoubtedly
lost their lives by the bunker - first practice.
There should be a turn-table at every terminus, and
a tender between the engine and the train on
suburban trains would also be an additional and
comparatively inexpensive safeguard.
On a Sydney
suburban railway,
fruit-cases were of-
ten opened, and the
best fruit abstract-
ed. One fruit-
grower resolved to
detect the thief.
He sent along a
good - looking,
weighty case —one
seemingly made for
choice fruit. When
the train pulled up
at a station the
guard rushed out of
his van, screaming,
and trying to beat
off the bees which
he had disturbed by
wrenching open
that good fruit-case.
Which settled the
matter.

A concert which took place at a Tasmanian
country town recently was advertised as being
held to replace certain spoons “mysteriously
missed ” from a ball-supper.
• • •
A N.S.W. man who inherited some valu-
able city property from his father (a “First
Fleeter”) had a German merchant as tenant of
one of his warehouses. Regularly every month,
when he came for his cheque, the German treated
him to a little dissertation on convictism, inter-
spersed with pointed allusions about “ the scum ”
that inherited property in N.S.W. At last the
“First Fleeter’s ” descendant got so enraged that
one morning, after receiving the German’s rent —
and his perennial dissertation in connection —he
went into the office of a merchant close by and
offered him the property at a ridiculously low
figure. The merchant promptly accepted, and a
few months after sold the property at a profit of
.£13,000!
* # «
In the Age report of Shiels’ speech, at the
Colonial Bank meeting, there is a missing line
represented by sundry traces of battered type,
where some words have been hacked out of the
stereotype at the last moment. Shiels is reported :
“It is true the Conservative journal, the Argus
agreed with Mr. Thompson Moore.” The
Auaira reports that the missing words were
“of which I wish to speak with respect.”
Alas for the goddess of Truth trepanned by a
cold chisel!

# • •
Ex-Premier Patterson enjoyed one little
thrill of rapture on Cup Day. That was when
his nephew George, son of auctioneer Patterson
and brother of Madge Tempest, came rampaging
along the course on a corpulent steed, at the head
of Hopetoun’s military escort. George is a
capting, or something, in the Mounted Rifles, and
by the earnest desire of his himperial uncle, no
doubt, he commanded the troops on this national
occasion. A noble sight was George, holding his
naked sword in a line with the family nose whilst
his Excellency and party climbed in or out of
their barouches. The ex-Premier was proud of
him. The jingo soul of the exploded meat-man
was in arms, so to speak. He glared at the vulgar
democratic multitude, on the Elat, as though
thinking of how nevvy George and his raw troopers
were created to put down insurrections if neces-
sary. George himself is not an unamiable sorb of
assistantauctioneer in plainclothes. With his buck-
ram on, and the safety of the Governor under his
supervision, George is a terrible fellow.
# # *
A woman charged her husband in a
Sydney court with getting drunk on Saturday
nights, smashing the furniture and regularly
thrashing her. The man could not get bail for
better behavior and was sent to gaol in conse-
quence. Now the woman is without support. It
thus comes about that a woman married to a
brute must either take her thrashing in silence or
go hungry. In most parts of Germany a man
convicted of such offences is, by local law, allowed
to go to his work as usual, but his wife gets his
wages, and he is locked up only from Saturday
night till Monday. This German way is certainly
a commonsense advance on the Australian way,
and would deserve a trial, if only as a test of
national temperament.

* # •
Mrs. Macnamara, the Rue Australian lady
who has been giving “ a piece of her mind ” to
Sydney Star, and putting into practical language
the general complaint of the matesses that Mrs.
Dictator Lane had a nasty way with her, and lin-
gered in luxury at Villa Rica, and wanted a house
of her own (“ with a fence round it, too ”) in order
to keep the common bush female at a distance, is
a told-you-so lady who was hopelessly embit-
tered at the outset by the loss of a large trunk
which held “ all my best silk dresses and my hus-
band’s evening dress clothes,” &c., as she told a
visitor to the Balmain camp. The only comfort
left her was that she had stipulated for the
privilege of teaching her children Scriptural
geography. “ That,” she added, with a dignified
sweep of the hand, “is my strong point.” But it
is evident that Mrs. Macnamara over-rated the
consolations of Scriptural geography. Had she
lost the geography and found the silk dresses
Lane might still have had a devoted disciple. Silk
dresses are a consolation which can never pall;
but there is no solidarity in linsey-woolsey.

History stands a chance of repeating itself
in Adelaide very soon. In ’77 the Governor
(Cairns), the Chief Justice (Way) and the Mayor
(Peacock) were all bachelors. In a month or two
Mr. Way will be Governor and Chief Justice, and
Aid. Tucker will probably be elected Mayor.
Again all bachelors,
