At Port Macquarie (N.S.W.) show, prizes
are offered to children for singing “God save the
Queen.” It is appropriate that loyalty should
linger longest in the old convict settlements. A
dog once well whipped eternally crouches.
• * *
A queer story of lovely woman’s inconsis-
tency (which should especially appeal to Sarah
Grandites) was told in a Melb. law-court the other
day. It is the lady’s own sworn narrative, filed
with a view to upsetting a decree nisi. She was
married in ’BB, and lived on pleasant terms with
her husband up to Jan. last, when she left him on
discovering that he was the father of an illegiti-
mate child. Her indignation wouldn’t permit her
to remain under the monster’s roof any longer,
“but she continued to meet him about three
times a week up to March 26 last.” The outraged
wife then agreed to make herself scarce for a time
whilst her husband “took proceedings for divorce
to please his people.” This private contract was
faithfully adhered to by both parties. The hus-
band made out a satisfactory case, which the wife
didn’t dream of defending, and in due course the
nisi was obtained. Petitioner, by the way, pro-
mised to be “good” to respondent, and allow a
quiet 12s. 6d. a week for her support. The couple,
having been divorced as per arrangement, resumed
their little meetings and maintained harmonious
relations until the divorcee's father got hold of the
alleged facts of the case and induced her to apply
for reinstatement in her original conjugal billet.
This story is a warning to wives who impetuously
leave the “old man ” before they are prepared to
dispense with his society altogether, the moral
being that where a woman can’t get a whole man’s
career, past, present, and future, to herself, she
should be prepared to accept a dividend.

department no end of thousands.
TO A “NEW WOMAN."
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Press on, rny sweet!
Till custom’s ramparts fall before your feet,
Till chain and bar
Are broken, and each cynic who would mar
Your work is quelled ;
But there’s one power will never be repelled,
One bond your strong, lithe hands will never rive;
And from Love’s durance, though you strain and
strive
Its bolts to shatter and its walla to shake,
You cannot break.
Grey heights ascend,
Whence Wisdom beckons, or the Muses bend ;
And laurels wear,
Twined in the circlets of your mazy hair;
But seek no more
Higher than my heart’s altitude to soar !
Orlando-taught, I bound your flight, as he
His love’s sweet stature; past this one degree,
Despite the lofty yearning of your eyes,
You cannot rise.
Your knight am I,
To follow where you lead, and to defy
The old, blind world,
Into whose teeth your rebel glove is hurled;
But come what may—
Despair, or dole, or death, in this one fray
We fight not side by side, but face to face ;
Nor shall you pity gain, nor any grace—
War, if you must, with Love; but, warring, know
I am your foe!
You claim your rights,
And with your own my willing voice unites :
Ah, have no fear,
Your cause is mine, to live or die for, dear !
But this alone,
Of all that you demand, I will nob own —
Nay, for I swear by all the powers that be
You have no right to mock at —are not free,
By grant of gods about us, or above,
To banish Love.
E.H.M.

“Swag-
man’s Prayer”: —
O, Lord, thou knowest but too well,
The swagman’s life is one long hell,
The rain thou dost from Heaven send
lias flooded every river bend,
The fish won’t take the bait at all
They eat their comrades if they’re small.
My boots are heavy with blue clay,
I’ve knocked off quite a ton to-day ;
I’ve got no work, or even bread !
I’ve got no place (o lay my head.
There are no empty huts around
Where lice, and ticks, and fleas abound.
I’ve got no ’bacca, Lord —or soap.
To wash my shirt there is no hope.
Though Thy son in a manger lay
He’s better off than me, to-day,
For grass-seeds prick me in the leg,
As through the swampy ground I peg,
My matches they are soaking wet—
Another box I cannot get—
My drinking-water’s full of weeds,
My shirt a new tail sadly needs,
My limbs are stiff from humping bluey,
And one of them’s a wee bit skewey.
These are hot trials few of mine,
To which, oil Lord! thy ear incline.
Oh, God ! 1 trust I’ll get a job,
Or some poor devil 1 shall rob.
So stop the rain and stop the flood—
Mosquitoes, too, who suck my blood,
Oh, God 1 then hear the swagman’s prayer
And make a note of it—up there.
General Debility.
The term General Debility means a depletion of
vital strength, a general break-up of the corporeal
health, due to over-consumption and wasting-away
of the life-giving constituents of the blood and
nerve tissue, really a wearing-out of the animal
structure. If your life is worth saving it is worth
your while to treat your case thoroughly. If it is
worth your while to treat your disease at all, it is
worth your while to continue the treatment until
your health is thoroughly re-established ; you may
feel all right after the first few bottles of Clements
Tonic, so prompt are its remedial and curative
effects; but a medicine no matter how good cannot
banish disease in a week, the seeds of the com-
plaint will still remain in your system and break
out afresh if you do not thoroughly eradicate them.
Don’t forget that a relapse is twice as difficult bo
cure as the original attack, so if you want to get
cured give the remedy a fair chance and recovery
is certain. Clements Tonic is a genuine article,
it is prepared for a specific purpose and contains
the material to make it an effectual remedy, and if
used faithfully it cannot fail. We do not claim it
is a cure-all, we do not say it will cure lock-jaw or
bad spelling, bub for all cases of impaired vital
strength it is a scientific and infallible specific.
Moreover, the cost of using it is so small that you
can have a year’s course of it for less than you can
procure a week’s medical attendance, and a tenfold
better remedy than any physician can prescribe
for you. Thousands of cases have been cured by
Clements Tonic, and it will cure you if you give it
a fair chance. Read the case of Mr. Wm. Scott,
manager A.J.S. Bank, Bega, N.S.W., who writes
on April 21st, 1893 :—I have pleasure in stating
that I have from time to time found much benefit
from using your Clements Tonic, which I consider
a really splendid one. As a rifle shot I have found
it useful in clearing the sight and steadying the
nerves. —Yours faithfully, Wm. Scott, manager
A.J.S. Bank, Bega, N.S.W.


The Wild Cat Column.
The latest drop in wheat brings things to such a
fioint that it is estimated it wouldn't pay to grow grain
n England this year even if all the land on whioh it is
produced were rent-free. Also, at present prices the-
bottom must pretty nearly fall out of Australia’s grain-
export trade, and out of that of other countries as-
well. By a curious turn of events the Argentine farmer
Is the man who now has the best of the deal. He sella
most of his surplus in England, and is paid for it in gold.
The gold, when it returns to Argentina, is exchanged for
three times its nominal value in depreciated paper. Said 1
paper is of no value for foreign trade, but for purchases;
of purely local produce it goes almost as tar as it ever did.
Therefore, the Argentine farmer, by reason of his depre-
ciated paper, gets over twice as much for his exported!
grain as he otherwise would. From this standpoint Aus-
tralia badly wants a depreciated paper currency.
• * • •
A scanty item of information which arrives by
cable shows that the London Bank of Australia is doing
none too well. Its profits for the half-year are about.
£7OOO, and £4600 of this goes to pay a div. of 5J percent,
to preference shareholders. The ordinary shareholder*
get nothing, which is the usual fate of such people nowa-
days. The earnings are at the rate of a little over one
per cent, per annum on capital, which is bad enough to
suggest that the London has a very large proportion of its
money locked up in investments which yield no interest
and from which the principal is very reticent about coming
back. The accounts are, however, only a shade worse
than those of the previous half-year, which is, perhaps, a
cause for alleviated congratulation. The London Bank
made a dismally bad bargain for its ordinary shareholder*
when it arranged that preference holders were to have sJ-
per cent. divs. before the ordinary crowd had a show, and
as the result of this contract the chances of the
latter are very remote indeed. Also, it is making
no progress worth mentioning towards creating a
new reserve fund, or doing anything else of a
specially cheering character. The London Bank professed
to be earning over £BO,OOO a year right up to the date of
suspension, and the fact that it dropped immediately after
the suspension to about one-sixth of that amount, and has
continued there ever since, suggests that it was pretty
sanguine in these good old days. With about three ex-
ceptions, there has been, among the resurrected banks,
such an awful discrepancy between their earnings imme-
diately before and immediately after suspension, that the
country is still wondering, and is likely to keep on
wondering, on what possible basis these former big profits
could have been estimated.
« * • «
The average land-boom transaction which turns
up in the courts is eminently calculated to harry the
Bench into an early grave. For instance, it was solemnly
attested the other day before Judge Williams, of Melb.,
that seven years ago the N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Co.
sold a property in Collins-street to the Real Estate Bank
for £210,000. It received £30,000 in cash, £30,000 by
mortgage, and £150,000 by another mortgage. The
£30,000 mortgage was afterwards paid off, and the Real
Estate Bank issued £150,000 worth of debentures, whioh
were guaranteed by the N.Z. L. and M. Co., and with the
proceeds it paid off the £150,000 mortgage, whioh was
then transferred to three trustees as security for the
debenture - holders. Next the land was sold for
£265,000, and the buyer put two more mortgages on it
to the Bank of S.A., and was then sold up by his creditors.
The man who bought the property with the three
mortgages on it was also sold up a little later on. Now
the Real Estate Bank is burst, and so is the Bank of S.A.,
and the old N.Z. Loan and Mercantile is dead, a recon-
structed institution having taken its place, and the pro-
perty, which is mortgaged for over £200,000, is worth less
than half the liability, and the debentures are about
worthless, and all these banks and institutions, and the
two other mortgagors, and the trustees and liquidators of
their bankrupt estates, and sundry other people, all
applied to Judge Williams on the ground that they dunno
where they are, and want to have their respective posi-
tions defined, and to find out what they are to do to each
other. This is the sort of thing whioh makes a judge get
a medical certificate and go off to Europe on 12 months
sick leave.
* « * *
The Permanent Trustee Co. of N.S. Wales seems
to have finally turned the corner, inasmuch as its balance
of profit on hand has increased during the year from £24
to £7BB. The £24 represented six years’ work—a fair
example of the slowness with which the public accepts
new ideas. The Trustee Co., with a capital of £1,000,000,
is immeasurably safer and more reliable as an executor
than any private individual can be, but either the public
realises this very gradually or else those persons who have
appointed the Co. as their executor are a long time
dying. Even now the actual business done doesn’t pay
expenses, and the net profit only represents part of the
interest received on the co.’s own invested capital of
£2s,ooo—the balance of interest having gone to make up
the deficit between receipts and working expenses. In
another year, however, with a reasonable mortality among
clients, the business should yield a substantial profit.
♦ • • •
One financial institution which was sick so long
before it died as to furnish unusual facilities for big
shareholders to stand from under, is having even more
difficulty than usual in finding the people that it wants to
collect calls from. It has just discovered that one share-
holder is a large brown retriever. The man who trans-
ferred his shares to the retriever has left the country, so
the liquidator is denied the pleasure of telling him what
ha thinks about this little flash of humor. What irritates
the ex-board more than anything else is that a certain
comic individual made himself unusually disagreeable at
a meeting some time ago on the strength of holding the
retriever’s proxy.
• • • •
The City of Melb. Bank offers 5 per cent, in-
terest on calls paid in ad\anoe, and even with that induce-
ment the calls in arrear are now the bigger item of the
two. The item, “ drafts in circulation,” has fallen in 18
months from £227,000 to £74,000—a fair evidence of how
the liquid business is drifting away ; the coin on hand has
diminished from £617,000 to £364,000; the Govt, deben-
tures from £451,000 to £65,000; the reserve of £200,000
has gone altogether; and the miscellaneous assets have
sunk from £4,632,000 to £4,176,000. What a beautiful
fading away it is 1 The gradual and pensive decline of
little Eva is what it most resembles.

Newcastle (N.S.W.) Borough Council, the other
day, floated its electric-lighting loan of £lO,OOO locally
with the Bank of N.S.W., at 4J per cent, for 20 years, each
£lOO debenture fetching £loolss. Which lends additional
point to the clumsy foolishness of Balmain Council,
which sold its whole £59,000 loan at 5$ per cent., without
public competition, to a man named Cohen, and instead
of getting 15s. per cent, premium actually gave Cohen a
large commission for taking the debentures! Saying
nothing of the lost premium and the lost commission,
the difference in interest alone will cost Balmain £ll,BOO.
• • • •
It is a melancholy fact that no one ever sees a
sovereign In Australia that isn’t brand-new. Australian
sovereigns drift away so fast to England in payment of
rents and interest to absentees, that they never have a
chance to grow old in this country.
• * * *
The reason why the interest on deposits in the
Melb. Commissioners’ Savings Bank was reduced to 3 per
cent, is now apparent. The Savings Bank last year only
earned £3 19s. per cent, on its invested funds. A large
proportion of the money must be sunk in investments
whioh yield little or no interest to account for this fall.

The National Bank directors announce that
they sympathise with the amalgamation movement,
which probably means that they would be glad to take
over the Royal or the Victoria on reasonable terms. But
it doesn’t mean that they want to have anything to do
with the Colonial, or the City of Melbourne. Shiels, who
says in a hollow voice that he is interested in seven re-
constructed banks, and who looks like it, would gladly
see the two comparatively strong Melb. banks take over a
weak one apiece and hold it up, but the National isn’t on.
Even a tolerably sound concern has lots to do in holding
itself up just now.

♦ • • *
The reason why many big Melb. solicitors went
to the wall after the boom was simply that their Invest-
ments of trust moneys looked so promising that they
were tempted to do a little on their own account—which
ended in some of them investing their clients’ cash In
their (the solicitors’) own
N.S.W. Supreme Court promises, in future, to
punish any solicitor found mixing his clients’ money with
his own, no matter whether a loss is discovered or not.
* • *
Why does not the Supreme Court, when it
strikes a Gent. One off the rolls, forward notes of the
evidence to the Attorney-General for his consideration?
This is where the legal I9,che comes in. In other matters
where a criminal offence is disclosed in the course of pro-
ceedings the Court sends the evidence for the inspection
of the Attorney-General, and sometimes, as in the case
of perjury, practically calls upon the Crown to prosecute.
In most gent, one cases a criminal offence is disclosed,
and so long as judges limit themselves to striking a
culprit off the rolls they must expect the public to think
that there is one administration of law for the offending
layman and another for the offending lawyer.
Rumored that a big Australian municipal
potentate is “cronk.” Moral: Lay to your book. The
pursuit of aldermanio glory hardly ever crushed a cash
div. in the long run.

A certain Northern bank having, with three
others, determined to make an influential merchant insol-
vent, has thereby grossly offended one of its most power-
ful directors, and he is retiring. The fact of that director’s
firm having dropped £72,000 in cold cash (no security)
over the merchant in question has been kept very dark
hitherto, but the merchant’s insolvency will bring the
fact to light. Hence these tears.

There is a lot behind the big purchase of a
Melb. hardware business by a crockery house, nominally.
The purchasers certainly paid spot cash with their own
cheque, but it is confidently asserted that the biggest
retail firm in N.S. Wales is making this stock the nucleus
of a gigantic universal providing establishment, which
will wipe out everything of the sort in Melbourne.
* * • •
At the National Bank meeting, chairman Pratt,
M.L.C., stated that the interests of new depositors would
always be regarded as having a first and just claim for
special protection. To most people it will seem that
special respect is due to age in deposits, and the reason
why these fresh young investments should take prece-
dence of the old reconstructed risks is not easy to dis-
cover.
• • « •
The two New Australias—the one presided over
by Lane and the one nominally bossed by Kidd—are at
loggerheads over an attempt to settle the accounts.
The Lane crowd, so far as can be understood from
a somewhat confused report of the shindy, is still
legally the owner of the original settlement, and the
settlement owes a large number of debts, therefore
the bosses of the movement demand a mortgage on
the property for the amount of the liabilities. The Kidd
crowd points out that the debts are owing by the associa-
tion to its own members, and that the idea of giving the
Head-Lane crowd a mortgage for the money which it owes
to itself is altogether preposterous. The assets are set
down as £2908 and the liabilities at £5170, and the Kidd
crowd protests that this awful and unaccountable state of
things makes its brain swim. The assertion that the Royal
Tar, which was bought with the association’s money, is
mortgaged to Lane for £2520, apparently makes its brain
swim a good deal worse. Head, on the part of the
Lane crowd, and Gilbert Casey, for the Kidd crowd, are
understood to be wading through the accounts and
explaining these things at great length to each other, with
the result that the balance-sheet of Rue Australia now
looks like a procession of inebriated crabs wandering over
a Chinese picture. To all appearance the discussion gets
no “ forrarder,” and Gilbert Casey’s explanations in the
papers are about the most inexplicable features of the
tragedy. Few men are blessed with the gift of ex-
planation.
v • • «
Dear Bulletin, —There is one circumstance in
connection with the “scheme” of the Australasian Rights
Purchase Association which you have overlooked, but
which tends to prove that the promoters are anxious to
shut out all chance of future competition. The Grose
and Colo rivers are separate streams, miles upon miles of
country lying between them, and sufficient power to serve
the purposes of the A.R.P.A. could be obtained from
either one or the other of these streams. Is it not plain,
therefore, that in seeking to obtain the exclusive right to
use the waters of both the Grose and the Colo, the syn-
dicators are desirous of establishing a huge monopoly?
If there is money in the concern, as you pertinently re-
mark, why should not the State make it and dump iti into
a depleted treasury ?—Yours, &0., Anti-Shark.

In a letter picked up the other day on one of
the N.S.W. boom mining fields, the writer said he was
well known to the editors of the metropolitan papers,
and if the prospectors of a particular claim would give
him a fifth share therein he would report the find so
favorably in Sydney journals that a rush would set in to
the field at once, thus enabling the prospectors to sell out
at a profit. Good old Archimedean lever 1
• * • *
A few hopeful Melbournians have formed a
Bass River Coal Prospecting Syndicate with a view to
striking a seam somewhere on the margin of Westernport
Bay (Vic.). The site of operation is only three miles
from deep water, so the spec, is based on the certainty of
cheap carriage for the coal if they can only find it. The
Govt, geologist gives a vaguely encouraging report, and a
bore from Korumburra will soon decide whether the
syndicate’s “ little bit of splosh ” (£200) has trickled down
the hole never to return, or otherwise.

New way to make money. A head school-
master in town, a few months before he thinks of retiring
on a fat pension, exchanges with an up-country school-
master for, say, £lOO or more, stays about three months
and then leaves.

• • • •
For the first time since Melb. Standard started,
the Herald appeared on the Saturday night following the
amalgamation with only four pages. “The advantages
of a monopoly are obvious,” writes a correspondent. The
Bulletin’s idea of an evening paper (and a morning
paper, too) is four pages. In four pages you can print all
the good, bright news and other matter, and all the
decently-paid advts. procurable. Four pages, if you
know your business, means a small expense, con-
densation, vigor and writing with a knife. Eight pages
means huge expense, maundering, flabbiness, and licking
the boots of the big advertisers. When a vigorous epigram-
matic four-page daily does come along, with a
habit of finding out and telling the truth about every-
thing, especially financial matters, it will make the heavy
eight pagers squeal.

• * « *
At Melbourne, Hollis and Co., a celebrated
English house, sued for £15,000 damages a firm of gun-
makers who had been stamping cheap guns with the
name of Hollis, and trading them off as genuine. A verdict
of £1250 was entered by consent, and a perpetual injunc-
tion assented to. Now, wasn’t defendants’ offence
forgery, pure and simple ? And why should the wretch
who obtains 255. on a forged-cheque signature get two
years, while the criminal law apparently takes no cog-
nisance of the forger of gun-signatures?
• • • •
Judge Williams (M.L.) holds that a married
woman cannot be arrested for debt. Judgment is not
against her personally, but against her property, she not
being bound to pay a debt personally.
• * * *
Vio. Minister for Railways thinks that by Oct.,
’95, Vic. coal will be landed in Melb. at 10s. per ton, and
that not a ton of N.S.W. coal will be used in that pro-
vince.
• • * •
The well-known and modest Fitzgerald Moore
has gone to England to take part, it is said, in the flota-
tion of the alleged Wealth of Nations mine. In view of
current rumours, if the English public take this mine at
anything over 4s. 6d. without having first obtained the
independent opinions of one or two of the best men avail-
able—say, for instance. Prof. Nicholas and Mr. Bratnober,
both of whom are well known in London —they will richly
deserve any misfortunes that may overtake them.
The story goes that hundreds of thousands are
to be asked for the Wealth of Nations—for which very
experienced and reliable men who have visited the field
would not on spec, give £5OOO as a mine to work. This
big-float business is an Australian national question, for
one or two grabs on a rotten basis will, if big enough to
be thoroughly remembersome, “ queer” bona-fide mining
for years, so far as outside capital is concerned. However,
perhaps another half-million or so is neither here nor
there in the present insane Coolgardie scramble, as the
W.A. companies already floated in London cannot pos-
sibly, taken all round, yield investors half-a-crown per
cent, per annum.

An Age writer makes an important error in
comparing the gold yields of S. Africa and Australia. He
states that with a yield of 170,0000z5. per month the
Randt heats U.S.A. as well as Australasia. As the Randt
output is quoted in gross ounces of comparatively low
grade, the comparison is unfair with the high grade of
Australian gold. However, the estimated yields for ’94
actually give Australia a quantity advantage without
reckoning the quality advantage:—
Randt .. .. 2,020,0000 z. gross.
Australasia .. 2,065,0000 z. gross.

* * • •
The alluvial find made in a cement formation
at White Feather is said to be turning out splendid gold,
and the holders claim that they are on the main lead.
They are reaping a rich harvest. This is the best defined
local lead yet found, but some people assert that it is not
true alluvial, but a big cement reef, that will go down.
There is a chance of litigation over the matter, as most of
the ground was pegged out some time ago.

A late cable states that the Cassell Gold Re-
covery Co., which owns the M'Arthur-Forrest cyanide
process, has been beaten at law by the Cyanide Gold
Extraction Co., which it sought to restrain from using

boon to Australia, where now 10 per cent, is levied.
• * • «
One of the most successful and experienced of
Australian speculators, whose name is a household word
among the mining men of the three eastern provinces,
writes The Bulletin after a visit to Coolgardie: “The
place has come to stay, and all the good things have not
yet been found. Some reefs have been applied for which
will, I am sure, put the sensational ones in the shade in
the long run. It’s only a question of time getting water,
I think, and as it is always blowing half a gale on Cool-
gardie, windmills will be a strong help towards developing
the reefs. But what a lot of duffers are now being cracked
up as good mines 1 Among the promoters of these things
your own common sense and judgment, developed by a
quarter of a century’s experience of reefing on many
fields, go for naught if you listen to the talk. Stone that
shows nothing is cracked up as soz. at least, and some-
times “proved” in a mortar generally in use for pound-
ing up specimens I * Mustard gold ’ is a favorite expression
on the field. You can imagine what that is like, and how
much will be saved by water used more than once 1 At
least 80 per cent, of the leases applied for will be aban-
doned, 10 per cent, more will be fairly tried and found
wanting ; the balance may pay handsomely.”

That sterling goldfield, Gympie, long compara-
tively neglected, threatens to boom. Already the flota-
tion of one or two more or less unpromising shows is
being manoeuvred. For October, 7314 tons ore were
crushed for 86920 z. gold. Nov. yield will, according to
Pope’s Circular, probably be about the same. Tonnage
for year to date, 67,852 tons, which has given 91,4910 z.
Divs. for Oct., £16,641, and for the 10 months, £189,438.
Oct. divs. exceed calls by £B5BB. Divs. for the year to
date exceed calls by £111,221. Estimated present value
of Gympie mines, £581,057, an increase for Oct. of
£20,224. (By the way, the Londonderry is being floated
in London for £700,000: Would any sane man give all
Gympie—as a field to be worked—for a dozen London-
derrys, or for all Coolgardie ?) On the present value
Gympie divs., for year to date, pay interest at £32 15s. 6d.
per cent, per annum ; net profits for same period, ascer-
tained by deducting calls from divs., pay £l9 2s. 9d. per
cent. Legitimate mining this, and when mining is legiti-
mate it is no more gambling than selling eggs or preach-
ing the Gospel.

Some very fine specimens, up to 30 and 40oz.
each, have lately been found in the thirsty Milparinka,
N.S.W. district, where, better still, artesian water is now
flowing.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Tedalderman Keys took his Cup “gruel” squeak-
ing. In other words, the owner of the favorite that
finished eleventh lodged a complaint of interference
(promptly dismissed) against the rider of the second horse.
This was a fitting crown to Moran’s wild hypothesis that
he would have got home easily on Ruenalf if Devon hadn’t
‘‘bumped” Nada, who “rolled against” Taranaki, who
“ threw him (Moran) on to the rails.” Moran and Keys
seem to enjoy a monopoly of this opinion. Everybody
else made out that Ruenalf was caught because he could
go no faster, just as the leading horses swept round the
final turn and began to race for home. About this time
Paris stumbled and Nada’s jockey was palpably shifted in
his saddle, but Paris and Nada nevertheless finished fifth
and third respectively. As for Ruenalf, he came in
number eleven, beaten. He was in front and pulling
double at the mile and five furlongs, after which he ceased
pulling and lost ground at every stride.
These monotonous complaints of bumping,
Jostling, fouling, etc., bear out Tub Bulletin’s oft-
repeated statement that the average Australian jockey
rides without head, hands, or system. The finish for a
Derby or Cup suggests a crowd of cockneys walloping
their way across Hampstead Heath on broken kneed nags,
hired at threepence per short gallop. Circular courses,
it goes without saying, are mainly accountable for the
barbarous spectacles which dishearten backers and delight
the “books." No jockey can afford to run wide all the
way round the circus. He must go for the rails most of
the time, and then comes the bookie’s glorious chance of
“ bad luck ” happening to this, that, or the other horse.
And as every big field for a big race includes duffers which
are there merely for the purpose of increasing the number
of bad-luck chances, the crowding on the rails is closer
than it would be in a field of tryers only.
Let punters note how the Argus gives this
jostling game away, without a thought of sarcasm upon
public faith in “form.” On Cup morning the Arous pro-
phet wrote : —“ Provided Ruenalf can stay, his success
would appear to be a question of average luck in the race.
But I have seen many apparent certainties go down, and
staying capacity given in, a little of the bumping which
occurred in the Caulfield Cup and the Derby might
easily prevent his winning."
Two years in succession has Tim Swiveller
been rammed down the throats of punters on Cup
morning. This year he was going to “ make amends for
last year’s disappointment,” in the face of much respeo-
able evidence that he wasn’t good enough for the job. It
was the same with Chicago (also a Caulfield Cup winner)
in 1888 and ’B9. On the first occasion Chicago was a
pretty general fancy and a tremendously strong “tip ” in
certain quarters. He ran like a non-stayer, but next year
all the “ clever ” people Were cocksure that he couldn't
ose.

Sydney Wool Sales.
M'Burnby, Wallis & Co. report:—We offered a repre-
sentative and attractive catalogue this week. Prices for
faulty descriptions of greasy fleece were about a farthing
per pound under last week’s auctions. Lamb’s wool and
bright w’ell-skirted greasy fleece elicited good competi-
tion, but values are still considerably under rates ruling
last season. Crossbred is still in disfavor as compared
with the high prices paid for this class of wool last year.

Bruin, who finished fourth, is the sort of animal
that never wins a hard-run race in first-class company.
It taken to the very front as in the Newmarket flutter,
Toorak mile Handicap, and Caulfield 1J mile Cup, he fails
when collared, whereas he can run like a stayer as long as
he is kept from the lead. In moderate company, with
nothing to test his “ heart," Bruin performs brilliantly.
Patron’s Cup win was a wholesome corrective
to the “ handy weight ” doctrines of writers who think
the “cracks" can always be slopped by a few extra
pounds into the saddle. Patron is a good three-year-old
matured in a good four-year-old, but his improvement
was not at all remarkable. Carnage, Sailor Prince, Patron
and Loyalty (leaving Light Artillery out of the question)
were lengths ahead of all other three-year-olds last
season. Only one older horse, Portsea, could rank with
them at weight-for-age, and with Portsea gone to the
stud, this reasonably well-handicapped quartette deserved
first consideration for the Cup of '94. They got first con-
sideration in The Bulletin, and the analyst clung to the
hope that Carnage was still the daddy of the crowd.
Neither Malvolio, Glenloth, nor Tarcoola (up
to time of writing) has won a sixpence since capturing the
Melbourne Cup. It was time that the big handicap fell
to a real racehorse.
Ruenalf’s boil-over justified a Bulletin sugges-
tion that clever owner Keys, when he scratched his
“ moral ’’ for the Caulfield Cup, was throwing away sub-
stance for shadow. The shorter race was indeed a good
thing for Ruenalf. How Keys, on the night of Nov. 6th,
must have cursed the Ruenalf ticket-holder (C.C.) who
wouldn’t take him into partnership 1 And how the
ticket-holder must have cursed Keys 1 To draw a fortune
in a sweep, and then to have it scratched from your
grasp, must be an awful experience for a pound specu-
lator, who probably does not know the comfort of true
religion.
Power declined to ride Dreamland in the Cup
after the remarks passed upon his Derby handling of that
animal. The Cup result was not exactly balm for
Power’s feelings. Dreamland, with Tiernay up, made a
lot of running with Ruenalf, and was bang in front of the
lighter-weighted Malachite at li miles. In the Derby he
had never made a bold show at all.
It may be many years before Carbine’s Cup time
is beaten. The champion had to get away from the crowd
ere the jostling began, and make the best of his way
home. There were at least seven jockeys chasing him all
the time with orders to prevent the best horse from get-
ting a fair run. But they never arrived at their hoped-for
opportunity. Carbine was too fast for them. This was
one of the rare instances in which virtue defeats the deep-
laid plans of villany. Ask the four or five “prominent
melallicians” who so pertinaciously laid the favorite with-
out decent regard for the appearance of their little books.
“,I got near 'irn once,” said one of the blocking contingent
to his Hebrew patron, when they were discussing the
fiasco in a pub. the night of the race. “ Well, yer ought
to 'ave caught him by the blanky tail and ’eld on,”
observed the child of Israel.
From a Melb. Age article published the Mon-
day after the Derby:—“ Meantime our streets and public
resorts are crowded with the vulpine faces of the pencil-
ling fraternity, and Federation has not progressed further
than the intercolonial combination of these beasts of prey,
who annually rob our too-confiding and simple citizens."
Wh.oh reads like truth, tinged with bitterness of one who
had dropped his money on Dreamland.
Five to one Devon for a “ place ” could be had
on Cup day in the paddock until half an hour before the
race, when he shortened down to 3’s. Even that was
b itter than taking the same price about Ruenalf winning
outright.
Killiecrankie, the slippery youngster, is said to
roar vigorously, but the wind complaint may not grow
upon him to any great extent. He is by Loohiel, whose
sire, Prince Charlie, was the greatest roarer and the
fastest miler that ever befriended the British aristocracy.
Languid swells made a handsome living for a time by
laying the odds on Prince Charlie. His last public per-
formance was a victory over a French horse in a match at
Newmarket. The Frenchmen dropped a mint of money,

Sydney Truth plumes itself on having tipped
the absolute winner and one of the placed horses, Devon,
in Melb. Cup. However, it was Editor Taylor and not
the horse-reporter who was the prophet.

The alleged fact that when O’Sullivan, M.P.,
and his N.S.W. Parliamentary friends were engaged in
table-tipping, the spirits named Patron in advance as
winner of Melb. Cup, doesn’t convince Tub Bulletin.
Let O Sullivan give us his table-tip for the Australian Cup
or any other big, open, near event, and there are plenty
of people who will on race-day get him current book-
makers’ odds up to a hundred or two against it. By the
way, one notices that O’Sullivan's faith in his table only
amounted to £25, whereas his Hebrew friend, Collins,
M. backed the horse for £2OO.

' • * * •
The S.M. Herald horse-reporter reaches out at
the eloquent and impressive:—“Dawes was hard on
Patron, who responded to every call with the greatest
gallantry. With a face set like death and teeth clenched,
Robson rode for his life, and rode well, but the post was
far too distant, and the big-hearted Patron had reached
the leader’s girths. At the half-distance the whips were
raised and fell on reeking sides ; closer and closer came
Patron at every stride, but Devon still struggled on des-
perately. Looked level in a deathly bitter struggle one
hundred yards from home, they fought out the terrible
battle with the greatest resolution, and then with one
supreme effort, Dawes drove the son of Grand flaneur to
the front, and, never flinching or wavering under the
dreadful strain, Patron came on, wearing down Devon,
and won a brilliant Cup by three-quarters of a length.

The Melb. Cup is on the wane. The newspaper
accounts are not at all about that of this year, but salma-
gundi of ten years ago. It is like dragging Madame
Eistori out again. .
« ♦ * *
This is how the Melb. Cnp pots work up. If
the “books” have only laid once against a horse they
don’t want to lay any more. But the public comes
forcing them, and the shortage of the odds is unnatural,
which maddens people, and they fancy it must be a sure
thing. ,

Pony-racing in Sydney: “Three horses were
running, and one was winning easily, when a spectator
tried to lasso its jockey."
• * • •
The collapse of both Ruenalf and Carnage at
the finish of the last two Cups favors the English theory
that a big race cannot be won all the way—that a horse
cannot make his own”running and win too. Patron, like
Lord Clifden, was, to all intents and purposes, out of it at
half-way, but “ his horses came back to him.”

Cup winners are Tim Whiffler (’67), his descendants being

the Cup in ’97.

Jockey “Chris.” Moore has a record no other
crack can boast: protested against in the Caulfield Cup
one year, and in the Derby the next.
• * » •
They wiped out jockey Moore for getting left at
the post on the Oaks favorite, but unless the V.R.C.
stewards had undoubted proof that Moore wanted to lose
the race their decision was cruelly hard upon a much
suspected man. In the writer’s opinion, Moore thought
it was a false start. True, he could have gone after the
other four horses and caught them before they went three
furlongs, yet he was not to know they would all be con-
tent to crawl as they did. Anyway, there was no reason
in the starter’s permitting’ Moore to stand a chance of
getting left. The filly, Quiver, obviously objected to face
the starting machine, and with such a small field to man-
age it would have been best under the circumstances for
Watson to send them off together in the old way.
* * * *
Dreamland showed bull-dog courage in the
Royal Park Stakes. lie ran under the whip for more
than half-a-mile, and eventually forged tar in front of all
except Portunatus. Had Dreamland won, the multitude
would have brought out one of chose rare cheers which
give a genuine sporting tone to horse-racing. The
?• books” are the first to applaud a game ’un, unless they
happen to have laid it very heavily. In which case they
merely say— “Blanky blank blank! the blanked blanker
deserved to win, s’elp mel"

The Melb. Cup, 1894, points several morals.
One of them is—Have nothing to do with any heavily-
backed horse whose joint owners’ interests are conflicting
in the event of that favorite getting home.

Melbourne Herald, the Friday before Cup day,
mentioned that as Paris, winner of Caulfield Cup, was
No 1 on the board; Preston, who won Moonee Valley
Cun was No. 8 ; and Tridentate, winner of Maribyrnong
Cud was No.' 9 ; it would be strange if the numeral want-
ing to denote the remaining figure of the present year—
viz 4—were carried by the winner of Melb. Cup. Yet
No!’ 4 (Patron) won, thus completing the figures denot-
ing the present year, 1894.
One of the winners of Tattersall’s Caulfield Cup
sweens was a Vic. railway-pensioner named Richardson,
who drew over £SOOO. The old man intended visiting his
home in Scotland, hut seems destined not to enjoy his
good luck, as at latest he was reported to be dying.

can lav to my “prophetic” soul is that my three selec-
tions—Tridendate, Ronda, and Ruenalf—were all at long
Drices when first mentioned in these columns, and they
finally started fifth, third, and first favorites, and ran—
well, respectably. Only this, and nothing more.
Bruin’s excellent achievement must not be over-
looked, and his Jeweller-like run at the finish fully bears
out the oft-repeated saying of the veteran Mat Dawson :
“If they can only go fast enough for six furlongs they
can all stay.” t
The running of most of the horses, bar Devon
and Bruin, knocks public form into a cocked hat, and
students of “the book” may well weep and wail and close
the volume.
On the Melbourne Stakes form, it was all
Australia to a bunyip on Ruenalf beating Patron,
whilst the Maribyrnong Handicap finish seemed to point
out that wherever Patron ran in the Cup Indentate must
be in front of him.
However, ’tis all over now, and with a word of
warning to look out for Tridentate in Tattersall’s Cup, 1
will bid the Cup of ’94 adieu.
“Blood will tell” has again been forcibly
exemplified, as the bulk of the winnings was secured by
descendants of that rare old sticker. Musket, whose scions
—Grand Flaneur, Trenton, Musk Rose, Nordenfeldt and
Carbine-nil sired winneis during the campaign.
From Queensland, I hear that the Q. Derby
winner, Czarina, an own sister to that honest horse.
Realm (Archie-Empress), is a real clinker, and must be
watched. 'Tis time the land of bananas produced another
first flighter, for since Megaphone nothing has been
brought out that can be classed Al.
Little Triton, an ex-Queenslander, has now
scored four minor wins in succession at Sydney suburban
meetings, and his consistent form should draw attention
to Trident as a sire. This horse, lam convinced, if he
had the mares, would beget racehorses of the very first
flight.
Until Xmas racing comes round cricket will be
the all-absorbing topic.

AN APOLOGY.—The Proprietors and Pub-
lishers of The Clipper withdraw all state-
ments and comments appearing in its issue of the
9th June last that might be understood as in any
way accusing the Evening News, published in
Sydney, N S.W., of being a party to the Mount
Huxley conspiracy, and admit that all statements
and comments in such issue, so far as relate to the
Evening News, or its proprietor, publisher, and
staff are wholly incorrect and unwarranted, and
we hereby apologise for having made the same.—
FATON & O’BRIEN.

The Caligraph Writing Machine *
THE CALIGRAPH embodies all the highest
developments of applied mechanics, and its
principle having stood the test of 15 years, its
success towers above the fantastic devices of mush-
room competitors.
“I like Caligraph writing better than that of
any other typewriter because the type comes out
so sharply and clearly.” The prominent Anglican
clergyman, who used these words, had hit upon
the chief characteristic of the Caligraph without
exactly knowing what brought it about. The
result is attributable to the fact that the platen of I
the Caligraph being bevelled the type strike
against a flat surface thus producing a full and
even impression. When you see typewriting with
only the middle of the letters clear you know that
the machine used was not a Caligraph, but one
fitted with the ordinary round platen. When you
read a typewritten letter, with some of the type
leaning to the right and the rest to the left, you
may be sure that your correspondent has a crude
machine, with curved typebars, which naturally
become more crooked, and thus twist their type
out of perpendicular. Then remember that the
Caligraph has straight typebars which, like every-
thing else in the machine, are fitted with adjust-
able bearings.
The Caligraph manifolds 15 carbon copies at
one operation, and used with the Edison Mimeo-
graph reduplicates 1500 copies from one stencil.

None of the industrious classes are more seriously
affected by the depression down South than the spielers.
An eminent member of that perfesh was overheard, on
Caulfield Cup day, explaining to a friend that he wished
to be perished, also afflicted with loss of vision if there
weren’t “over 150 of us in the bloomin’ ring for every
jay.’’
* • • •
Australian Elevens have ruined more cricketers
than anything else. Blackham and Bannerman are prob-
ably the only two amongst those who have had several
trips anything the better for their envied privileges.
Palmer is practically an underpaid professional bowler at
South Melbourne; the veteran Boyle is prospecting; while
M'Donnell, Bonnor and any number of others would have
been better men to-day had they never seen Hingland.
* * * *
The retirement from the scene of action of a
certain punter must prove a big relief to a certain “out-
side ” bookmaker. The pair sat down to hazard one
night, and the bookie rose from the table £600 to the
bad. Punter could not get the money, and “ the Club ”
refused to adjudicate, as Punter was not a member and
the default was not over a wager. Bookie told Punter
to get it as best he could, and Punter took about £100 of
it out in lumps knocked off the bookie’s face at a race-
meeting, and another ±100 or so at another race-meeting.
Punter reckoned to take out the balance later on, but
pressing business has caused an indefinite postponement.
The degenerate Australian horse ! A Cloncurry
(Q0 paper says that J. Dailey and C. Whitaker rode from
Bridgewater Station to Urundangie and back (total dis-
tance, 340 miles) in four hours under four days. They
rode the same horses throughout; and did not travel
after sundown. The horses were just off grass, and had
nothing but the natural herbage during the journey. If
the horses were exceptional, so were the men who had
endurance for such a ride.
• * * •
A Bulletin correspondent:—The late Martin
Loughlin some years ago sat watching a scientific spar at
the now defunct Melb. Athletic Club. “Damn your
science!” said Marlin—“a few good, hard, straight
punches and they soon forget all that. I’ll lay 50 sovs. I
get a lad with no science at all to beat the best of them.”
A certain member (now a Chief Justice) smiled and took
the wager, and backed the club’s instructor, Billy Wil-
liams. Martin’s nominee was Jimmy Murphy (the Tor-
pedo’s brother). The go will long be remembered. It
was science and hard punching. Result; five rounds,
science a wreck (minus an ear, broken nose, optics black,
a cab, and a week in a private hospital). Hard-puncher
drank his pint, laughed, and swore it was the easiest
money he ever got. Hadn’t a mark, in fact.
* * • •
Like Larry Foley, but unlike most other boxers,
Corbett not only makes money at the game, but knows
how to keep it. He is already well-off, and the coin is
daily coming in from his theatrical and other ventures.
Sullivan should be worth £100,000 now, but lives from
hand to mouth.
* • • •
It is usual to describe Corbett as a drawing-
room boxer, but probably he would make mincemeat of
any man alive at the bare-knuckle business. Though
cautious, he has hitherto shown pluck enough when the
time came. Then, he alone of the big men is a good
wrestler, and wrestling, of no use in Queensberry, is all-
important under P.R. Mace was not a champion among
wrestlers, but his knowledge of wrestling made him
formidable in the P.R.jmd helped him to win many fights.
Mick Dooley, himself a skilled boxer of the first
order, says that Peter Jackson, as a scientific boxer, is
unapproached among big men. Slavin was a somewhat
better boxer than Goddard, but the latter's wonderful
endurance at one time more than counterbalanced this.
Dooley gave the Barrier man enough punishment to beat
half-a-dozen, but found him grumbling for more and got
disheartened. Having fought good battles with all three,
Mick is worth hearing on the subject.
* * * *
Grace has by now, probably, completed his
century of centuries. At latest he had made his 99th
three-figure score.
* • • *
W. G. Grace, junior, finished his first county
cricket season with an innings of 148 not out, against
very fair bowling.
* • * *
That mine of brilliant originality, the Leader,
published for “our annual Cup supplement” a full-
length portrait of W. R. Wilson, entitled, “The Master
of St. Albans." These fat, unwieldy turnip periodicals
are incapable of treating anj- subject without a touch
of grovel. The Master of St. Albans! However, Mr.
Wilson as Wilson is_ not a very remarkable-looking sub-
ject for a two-foot picture, and perhaps it was as well to
give him a title. When Xmas comes the Leader will
witch the world with a beautiful portrait of the new
Czar, we guess.
• • • *
The Bulletin abhors the scoundrel who invited
a few of the elderly male worshippers of both the local
Catholic and Protestant Churches toattend a fight between
dogs of these rival creeds, at St. Hilda slaughter-house, at
dawn on a recent Sunday. Had not about a dozen dis-
guised invitees turned up at intervals and the Catholics
been informed by the joker that the fun was off owing to
“ that_ ruffian So-and-so (a Protestant) giving it away”
(and vice-versa when he had to deal with a Protestant) no
harm would have resulted ; but there were at least three
private human “ scraps ” that morning before cburoh-
time.
• • • •
F. : I saw the Melb. ’93 Cup from Tattersall’s
stand. Before the race, as the horses passed out of the
adjoining bird-cage gate, the horsey crowd around me
vented their opinions. I listened most respectfully to
one possessed of a lovely flow of language and tobacco-
juice. Track form, public form, and appearance were
A B C to him, and he picturesquely unburdened his lesson
till Tarcoola came—and then for a minute he chewed in
silent surprise. This done, he called on Tarcoola’s aniseed
ancestors and the monstrous mothers of the petrified public
before him to witness his opinion that Tarcoola’s appear-
ance “packed ’’all the knock-kneed knackerson the course.
His wonder rose to fever-heat. His last bean was “ blued ”
on that carnation Carnage—it was a sacrifice to Tarcoola.
He condemned the field and the public and the starter for
ever if Tarcoola could lose. The lowest Tartarus was
good enough for him If Tarcoola didn't win. Much im-
pressed I went down to Grimwood and paid half a sov. for
a ticket in cipher on the subject. I returned to the
stand and saw Tarcoola win. In gratitude I waited for
the Orator and touched his shoulder as he passed. “ You
were a good judge about Tarcoola,” I said humbly.
“Tarcoola!” he responded, “I never mentioned the
(alliterative)abortion’s namein my life.”
At New Angledool (N.S.W.) “Bob the
Swimmer” is a local champion, and every annul is
always asked “ Can you swim ? ” If he says “Yes” he
has to swim Robert the next Saturday. Sometime hack
a “ raw 'uu “ struck New Angledool. He could swim,
and wouldn’t mind trying Bob for a fiver—“or you can
make it a tenner." A good start was effected, and at
half-way the favorite had a big lead. The punters were
thinking what their drinks would be, when the “raw ’un ”
dived down and came up in front of New Angledool’s
champion ; then he dived again and came up in the rear.
This performance was repeated once or twice, Robert not
knowing how to take it. Eventually the new-chum put
on a spurt and won anyhow. Then he took his tenner
and departed. The punters want Ernie Cavill to strike
New Angledool again.
* • * •
Jenkins, the crack amateur cyclist who made
his reappearance on the racing track in Sydney the other
Saturday, was nearly killed, 12 years ago, in a “big
wheel ” race. As he dashed first past the post a dog ran
in the way of his machine and capsized it. Jenkins fell
forward on his face and hands, breaking his right fore-
arm and left wrist, and almost losing one eye.
• • » *
The inhabitants of Hannan’s have formed a
racing club and are going to establish a municipality.
The racing club always comes before the municipality in
Australia.
• * * »
Grand Flaneur promises to prove as great a
success at the stud as on the track. What price a horse
sireing first and second in such a field as started for the
Melbourne Stakes?
• • * ♦
At Germanton (N.S.W.) Show Miss Hyland,
daughter of the horse-tamer, was beaten by 12min. in a
taming competition by one J. Todd. But her horse was
the quieter when finished with.
Mr. Albert Sharp, the discoverer of the
new Gannalin remedies for piles, announces that,
in future, there will be a single charge of one
pound for the six weeks course of treatment.
This will avoid delay in serious cases from remote
parts of the colonies, as a pound-note can be sent
where postal-order is not available. Applications
must be accompanied by a full description of the
ease, and be addressed plainly to 257 George-st.,
Sydney, N.S.W.
j The other day one Mahoney, a very new-
■ chum Irishman, was arraigned before Judge Casey
(Vic.) for serious assault, consisting in Mr.
Mahoney’s having fractured the skull of one
Morris with a block of road-metal. The provoca-
tion given by Morris appeared to be that he had
scornfully alluded to Mr. Mahoney as a recent
arrival. Mr. Mahoney pleaded guilty, but added :
“Well, your riverence, Oi don’t know phwat
kind av heads these colonials have—they can’t be
anny thicker than aig-shell. Why, in Oireland
ye can pelt a man from daylight till dark and the
divil a scratch you’d find on him if he was anny
sort av a man at all, at all.” This story was
being retailed to a lot of the Koroit bhoys, one of
whom said ; “ Begorra, thin, Mahoney was right.
Why, I have in my head av a row at eliction-
time, when the O’Meallys got hold av one av the
Ryans, and all ye could see of Ryan was a bit av
bis ear poking out av the mud.” “And was
Ryan killed ?” “ Killed be damned 1 No, he was
pounded fair into the mud, I tell you, and all you
could see was the pint av his ear, and when the
O’Meallys got off him he got out av the mud and
gev himself a shake like a dog and said : ‘Come
on, you divils, I’m ready agin for yez !’ Ye see
the pounding gave him time to draw his breath.” |

Nervousness Cured.
The well-known and popular representative

SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.

MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess's Theatre “ Nitouohe.”
Theatre Royal “Jack Sheppard.”
Buoo “Skipped.”
“ Hands Across the Sea,” which now per-
vades Sydney Royal, would be a robust melo-
drama with no specially obnoxious features, were
it not quite so full of remarks about the British
Flag. As it is, the flag grows very heavy towards
the end, and it is further aggravated by the im-
pressive manner in which J. P. West tells his
minions to hoist it on important occasions. Even
when it isn’t actually visible it infests the premises
in the spirit, and an aroma of British Flag is
noticeable all through the piece. Also, there are
many ringing allusions to the “ laws of In-n-n-ng-
land.” The author of the drama was evidently
weak on points of law, by the way, and on a few
other things as well. When a cold and cynical
French convict, after being paraded in chains on
Circular Quay, Sydney, to induce him to tell the
truth, still refuses to exculpate the bright young
English hero, he is publicly shaken and bumped
by an Australian detective in order to awaken his
sense of rectitude. This may be a stage custom,
but the real detective bumps his prisoner pri-
vately. Moreover, English law doesn’t allow any
man to be shaken in order to extract evidence.
Later on, however, a comic person, who is
unknown to the policeman, points out a by-
stander in a brown suit as the man who
really did the deed, and thereupon the police-
man releases the hero, despite the fact that
he is sentenced to penal servitude for life,
and the hero remarks enthusiastically, while
embracing Miss Yirgie Vivienne, that all his
troubles are now over. Having been honorably
acquitted by the policeman on Circular Quay, in
full view of Hill, Clark and Co.’s wool store and
close to where the North Shore boat starts from,
he undergoes no further disturbance. An attempt
has been made to gratify Australian tastes by
causing certain hard-up English characters to
come to this country, and make a large fortune
apiece, and return to England with the proceeds;
it being probably imagined that the Australian
never feels so thoroughly good all over as when he
sees such individuals as these taking immense
sums of money out of the countrp, and going
“’Ome” to spend the evening of their days in
hoisting the British flag. The English characters
in “ Hands Across the Sea ” are mostly noble, and
show up splendidly against a dark back-ground of
French scoundrel. Miss Virginia Vivienne makes
a pretty heroine, particularly excellent in sundry
tragic passages where she struggles against a great
accumulation of French counts and other vil-
lains. Messrs. Alfred Woods, Geo. Lash Gordon
and Leonard are three capable actors, and save
the drama on many occasions from looking so
foolish as nature intended it to do, and a number
of minor characters, and some of the scenery, back
them up manfully. A perfectly square Pacific
Ocean, Mr. J. P, West, the British Flag, and the
policeman who acquits the convict on Circular
Quay are, however, expressly excluded from this
commendation, and so is a certain raw-meat verse
about the old English “ blood,” which the hero
recites on Circular Quay, alongside the Mosman’s
Bay ferry wharf. This verse is a truly dreadful
little bathos, and no man with a sense of the
ridiculous would recite it anywhere within 40
miles of a human habitation.
** * *
That soulful little curtain-raiser, “ To Call
Her Mine.” which had altogether too much of the
wash of the old grey sea in it, has been taken out
of the Lyceum programme, and “ True as Truth ”
occupies its place. The change is decidedly for
the better, for the other piece, when hitched on to
the “New Boy,” suggested a goblin at a picnic,
or something of the sort. Concerning the principal
comedy, The Bulletin still adheres to its
conviction that the boy himself is somewhat
lacking in humor, and that but for Felix
Roach, and possibly Bullock, senr., there would
be comparatively little comedy in it. Miss
Edith Housley, whose mission it is to be-
come engaged to all the big boys in the school,
and to make large, expressive eyes at the French
master, is partly a replica of Dr, Grimston’s
daughter in “ Vice Versa,” and partly a duplicate
of Minnie Palmer, that fluffy charmer of old, in
“My Sweetheart.” Her fluffy appearance sug-
gests Minnie Palmer strongly, and she makes the
best, in a quaint and rather pretty fashion which
is mostly her own, of a somewhat weak part. The
part, in fact, consists mainly of a good deal of
precocious flirtation and a set of dazzling teeth,
and Miss Edith is good in both characters. Her
portrait appears in this issue.
** * *
“Siberia,” after an undeservedly brief
career at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), gives place, on
Saturday, to the “Two Orphans.” In the hands
of a strong company this drama almost always
pans out well; in the hands of a weak one it has
great possibilities in the way of bathos. It ap-
peared at its best some years ago, when Misses
Lucia Harwood and Maggie Knight were the
orphans, and whether it will ever again reach that
standard is open to question.
** * *
“Mdlle. Mbouche,” which disclaims all
pretension to opera, and admits indirectly that it
is a mere wicked French novelty with lots of
spice in it, had a warm reception at Melb.
Princess’ on Saturday. In fact, the fame of
“Nitouche,” and the Living Pictures to follow,
operating upon the circumstance that a racing
public needed a suitable place of refuge from its
thoughts, caused the “house ” to smash the record
at ordinary prices. People who couldn’t buy
standing room simply hung on to space, and
pounded the air with their feet when they wanted
encores. “Nitouche” now is the same three-act
riot that Sydney and The Bulletin approved of
a short while ago. Nellie Stewart and Lauri are
the sole supports of a plot which knows no com-
punction, and sticks at nothing. The artists
aforesaid begin and end in a convent school,
but the things they do outside of the school
would paralyse Miss May Pollard, if she only
knew. Miss P. is the Lady Superior, and her
demeanor in nun’s costume contributes a touch of
infinite calm to the ensemble. Wallace Brownlow
plays and sings his small part in the best of
manners; also, the four naughty dancers form a
beautifully straight line with their eight legs—
touching which, it may be remarked that there is
nothing exceptional about the posture of these
dear girls. Every member of a corps de ballet
must acquire the art of sitting down at full length,
so to speak, wherefore the charge of impropriety
levelled against dancers who happen to perform
this every-day movement in public is quite
absurd, and the occasional Johnnie who calls it
“horrible” is libelling the first rudiment of a
charming profession.
• * •
One of Nellie Stewart’s “Nitouche” cos-
tumes—a demi-semi toilette let in with large
glimpses of pink fleshing—paves the way for the
tableaux-vivants. Melbourne’s first experience of
the living pictures was very pleasing. The pro-
truding eyes of the male audience gleamed with a
strange light, and Mrs. Grundy, seeking revela-
tions through her lorgnette, felt bound to perceive
the purity of Art. A delicate compliment paid to
Venus and other statue pictures was the common
suspicion that they didn’t live at all. The still-
ness of sweet Psyche, gazing upon her reflection
in a painted stream, provoked a gurgle of admira-
tion. And the back-view lady, nestling against
the stuffed dog before she started resuming her
garments—nobody could believe her guilty of pul-
ling a string to make the animal’s tail wag, yet
she must have moved a muscle somewhere. The
saint who hopes to detect giddiness in these tab-
leaux must bore a gimlet-hole through the roof
and glue his eye to the aperture. Seen from the
stalls or circles they are deliciously decent, sug-
gesting nothing worse than an idea that one would
like to change places with the dawg aforesaid.
** * *
“ Jack Sheppard ” goes with an uncertain
amount of dash at the Royal. There was at least
one night last week when the old burlesque might
have travelled with more determination, but, as a
rule, the company make things lively. Maggie
Moore has introduced some new songs, and Blue-
skin improves as he goes along. Other people are
beginning to get a good polish on their reminis-
cences of extravaganza, Dick Stewart being a
particular case in point. Mr. Stewart’s dancing,
as Kneebone, is humorous as well as nimble, and
his legs express real pleasure at their respite from
the weary round of melodrama. A proof that
“Little Jack Sheppard” is suitably cast lies in
the management’s promise to keep it up for
another week or two, unless business drops with
unusual suddenness.
** * *
John Gourlay and his perennial ‘‘ Skipped ”
are back again at the Bijou. “Skipped” has a
habit of reappearing at one theatre or other when
least expected, and its company, apart from
Gourlay, is subject to violent changes. This time
Gourlay is assisted by some sound comedians and
a capital soubrette in Miss Isabel Stuart. The
show, moreover, has a strong musical attraction.
Miss Ulster, whose bonnie presence atones for
her amateurish acting, trills “Sing, Sweet Bird,”
and peals forth a waltz bravura in beautiful styleli
Meantime the little Oxford Theatre, under Cogill\
management, is still doing a great variety busi-
ness ; and Abell and Klaer’s circus flourishes like
the evergreen “ wheeze” of the clown who never
dieth.
** * *
On Wednesday and three following days
of the current week Mrs. Annie Besant delivers
farewell orations at Melb. Athenmum Hall. That
wonderful woman intends to deal with Theosophy
pure and simple on these last nights, and the
unbelievers will probably roll up to hear her
explain the difference between real spirits and the
spurious articles which collapse when the gas is
turned up.
** * *
Yice-regal Robinson’s opera, “ Preda-
toros,” was elegantly produced at Melb. Princess’
on Monday afternoon. These remarks are in-
spired by the first act only, for The Bulletin’s
rep, couldn’t wait to see the second. Remarks
upon both acts would doubtless have been stronger
—much, much stronger. The pervading charac-
teristic of Messrs. Robinson and Hart’s opera
is its want of originality. Unfortunately,
it is not a base imitation of any other
work. There is a thin story which ger-
minated in “The Mountebanks,” and drew a
little sustenance from “Pirates of Penzance.”
The story is told in thin dialogue and thin verses
set to thin music. The lyrics, though snapless,
pass muster, but the dialogue bristles with proofs
that Mr. Hart cannot discriminate between ab-
surdity humorous and absurdity stupid. No
stage business worth mentioning is to be seen.
At Poverty Point.
Dear Bulletin,— The Australian Actors’ Benevolent
Institutions seeming to be in such a bad way, 1 would
suggest that a plan which is in vogue here should be
adopted by your managers. I sent my card in to the
Garden Theatre and applied for a box, which I received
per return post. When I presented my ticket (I had six
people with me) we had to pay 10 cents (5d.) each. Natur-
ally, I asked what this was for, and was told it was a tax
upon deadheads and went to the Actors’ Benevolent
Fund. No exception whatever is made. Everyone who
has the privilege of entree has to pay this tax. I thought
it a splendid thing, and the meanest man in the world
could hardly refuse to pay sd. when he is getting, per-
haps, a 10s. seat for nothing. If this were done through-
out Australasia, no poor, old, or unfortunate actor or
actress need want the necessaries of life.—Yours, G. H.
Snazbllb, New York, 25th Sept., '9l. [Tub Bulletin
hopes to see this plan adopted.]
The just-announced marriage of a certain Anglo-Aus-
tralian official potentate with a veteran actress was a
mere formality to legalise a domestic arrangement of
very long standing.
R. S. Smythe has announced his intention of retiring
from management at 60, but he expects to rake in a few
more shekels before then.
A Melb. paper: “We can’t understand why George
Rig n old plays Marc Antony in ‘Julius C»sar,’ when both
Brutus and Cassius are better ‘acting’ characters."
“ Yon Cassius has a lean and hungry look," would fit
Rignold like a glove.
At a concert given in a Hobart (Congregational) church,
“secular” items were rendered, applause liberally
awarded with hands and feet, encores demanded and
given, and huge bouquets handed up to performers.
Edith Moore, of Hiscock’s Federal minstrels, now play-
ing in Sydney, is a daughter of the Fijian Surveyor-
General.
Titheradge has seven daughters. Mrs. Titheradge has
not appeared in Melb. for many years, since she took
Robinson Crusoe, in the Opera House pantomime.
That charming actress, Maggie Knight, was lately mar-
ried to Mr. Harry Anderson, a Sydney commercial man
who hails from Maoriland, and who years ago had the
reputation of being the stoutest oarsman in the Auckland
province.
On Wednesday week the pictures to be represented at
the Princess’ tableaux vivants were exhibited in a Col I ins-
street window, among them being the “ Will o’ the Wisp”
nude. The latter, however, only remained in evidence
about two hours, for at one o’clock its place was taken by
“The Doctor.” The man with the long upper-lip still
bosses Melbourne.
The expansive Maggie Moore has long been a confirmed
advocate of dress reform. At rehearsals she always dis-
cards the cumbrous skirt for the free-and-easy breeches.
She has not, however, advanced far enough to show the
outline of her ample limbs on the Block yet. The female
breeches brigade might petition Maggie to act as leader
of the forward movement.
LITTLE JACK SHEPPARD.
They greeted Maggie with a roar.
Their warm affection to confess,
And yet they don’t want Maggie more—
In fact, they want her rather less.
Melbourne Leader asks: “ Who is Miss RhodaHalkett,
who is being announced in England as an Australian
actress of striking beauty and talent? She is described
as an extremely beautiful woman, tall and fair, with big
grey eyes, has a rich, full voice, and possesses most
attractive manners.” Tim Bulletin isn’t dead certain
that Ehoda Halkett (Mrs. Parker) is Australian by birth,
and it isn’t sure that she is especially tall, and it wouldn’t
swear that her talent is very “striking.” But it backs
her straight out as the prettiest woman with the brightest
and most poetic eyes who has been on the Australian
stage for some years past, and the paper that professes
not even to have seen this dazzling vision is a hypocritical
affair which is mainly trying to propitiate its mother-in-
law.
Henry Herman, playright and story-writer, and joint-
author of “The Silver King,” who died the other day,
was an all-round man. Originally educated for the
German army, he became an M.D. of Paris and then prac-
tised medicine and edited a newspaper in South America.
He was a brave and distinguished Confederate colonel in
the American War of Secession. Then he drifted to
England and became a successful playwright. “ Silver
King” gave him £30,000, and “Olaudian” even more.
He entertained his friends as Lucullus would have enter-
tained them. Remonstrances on his extravagance had no
effect—his money poured forth like water until a lady un-
wittingly brought him to reason. He was to give a special
dinner in Mrs. Conover’s honor, and went himself toCovent
Garden and bought early peas at two guineas a quart.
After Mrs._ 0. had eaten, she turned to Herman and
thanked him for so great a treat. “Those peas, Mr.
Herman,” she said, “ are the best canned peas I ever ate
in my life.” Which so disgusted him that he never gave
another dinner. That one chance remark taught him
more than all his bitter experiences of sleeping in the
park, when first he reached London at the close of the
war without a penny in his pocket and wandered round
in search of a meal.
Dear Bulletin, —A late cable re the action of the
London County Council trying to close the pro-
menade bars of the “ Empire,” is possibly Greek to
hundreds of your readers. Hereunder lies the interpreta-
tion : The Empire promenade is the one public resort (at
about 10 p.m.) of the cr£rae de la crfime of the London
demi-monde. The upholstering of the place is on the
most superb scale, the promenade is furnished with
innumerable elegant lounges placed at different angles,
and mirrors so arranged that the size of the place appears
gigantic. A glass partition separates the bars from the
promenade, the entrance to which is through two glass
doors in the centre. If a visitor places himself opposite
these doors with his back to the stage and leans against
the plush-covered division which separates the gallery
from the promenade he will see a vision which cannot be
excelled in the world. He will quite forget that he is
taming his back on perhaps the best variety-show on
earth, and if he is an average Australian he will not turn
round again. He will see about 40 of the most beautiful
and well-bred-looking women that he has ever seen, some
sitting by themselves two or three on a lounge, others
promenading, here and there one or two talking in a sub-
dued tone to gentlemen, but all lady-like, and, of course,
dressed in the height of fashion. The nymphs visiting
this part of the premises are all known to the manage-
ment, and belong to a stratum never seen on the streets.
Five shillings is charged for admission to the promenade.
You will not be astonished to learn that the Empire Co.,
Limited, pays good divs., and is the property of a very
wealthy and aristocratic proprietary. A feature of the
place is the number of Australians you see there—with
their backs turned to the performance.—J.R.B.
Snazelle was to start his “rational Sunday evenings”
at the Garden Theatre, Madison-sq., N.Y., early in Oct.
Eleonora Duse, the Italian actress, threatens a visit to
Australia. From all The Bulletin hears, Duse is an
acquired taste, and by no means a howling financial
success in Europe.
Well-known and capable pros, (in some cases ex-stars)
are doing Melb. suburbs in standard dramas at no fixed
prices of admission, a silver coin only being expected.
Poor pros, in Melbourne, some of whom at one time
received their £8 to £lO weekly, jump at a chance of 6s.
a night, 7s. 6d. a performance being considered atop rate.
I thought Mrs. Langtry was a bad actress (says The
Bulletin’s London correspondent), but, after seeing
Anna Ruppert, the lovely complexionist, in ‘ Odette,’ 1
am inclined to think that the lovely Lily is a perfect
Bernhardt. Mrs. Ruppert apparently has a lot of money,
made out of face-washes at a guinea a bottle, for the
floral and other decorations of the Princess's at the pre-
miere of ‘Odette’ were most sumptuous. In addition
to owning the lease of the theatre, Anna is the proprietor
of a most inoffensive-looking husband, who seems devoted
to her. On the morning of the day * Odette ’ was pro-
duced Anna fancied she detected a smell of oats, so,
accompanied by her submissive spouse, who was laden
with bottles of scent, she went round the house spraying
perfume. Herbert Flemming, by the way, had a part in
‘ Odette.’ Salaries for two weeks were paid in advance.
One day last week Judge Windeyer made absolute 12
divorce-decrees nisi. The petitioners were mostly women.
An idea for them, from the recent handbill of a Yankee
dime-museum: “Twelve lonely, sorrowing, sympathetic,
grief-stricken ex-wives; twelve heartbroken, abused, mis-
led, unhappy divorcees. An object-lesson in establish-
ment of the fact that marriage is a lottery. Each one had
at her feet the man of all the world she loved best. Now
she will have at her feet a framed copy of the Divorce
decree which tells the story of a good man gone wrong.
They will be on view, beginning next Saturday, attired in
widows’ weeds. Their decrees are all genuine, each bear-
ing the seal of the Court and the signature of the Judge
granting it. 250 dollars in gold to the prettiest, the most
modest-looking, 4to. To be decided by the popular vote
of the visitors.”
C. N. Steen, the sleight-of-hand performer, says that
Anna Eva Fay, the medium on whose performances the
celebrated Crookes, the radiometer man, based much of
his spiritualassic reasoning, confessed to him when subse-
quently in his employ that she cheated the professor just
as easily as she could cheat anybody else. The Bulletin,
which used to see a good deal of the pallid, cigarette-
chewing professor, long ago concluded that he could be
easily humbugged by anything pretty in skirts.
Despite alleged bad times, the present theatrical season
is one of the most prosperous on record (says The Bulle-
tin's London correspondent). Money is being turned
away at Daly’s (“A Gaiety Girl”), the Prince of
Wales (“Claude Duval”), Adelphi(“The Fatal Card”), and
Comedy (“The New Woman”); whilst full houses are the
order of the night at the Globe (“ Charley’s Aunt”), Vaude-
ville(“The New Boy”), Lyric(“ Little Christopher Colum-
bus”), Terry’s (“The Foundling,” which gladdens the
heart of Plain Bill Holloway), and Toole’s (“ A Trip to
Chinatown"). At the Lyric, a few nights ago, Sedger told
me they had over £2OO in the house. All the while the
halls are doing enormous business.
The Sapio-Urso Co. had very ordinary success in Ade-
laide—mainly because they had no Smythe to do the
prelim, booming. At one or two concerts the hall was
not half filled.
The Brough-Boucioault partnership will, The Bulletin
hears, probably be dissolved on the latter’s return from
England. Boucicault will then run the Co., and Austra-
lia will lose Mr. and Mrs. Brough lor ever if they get a
footing in London.
The announcement of “living models ” at Melb. Bijou
sent a tingle down the spine of every Johnnie in Mel-
bourne, who fondly hoped that the beauteous Leslie
Norman, in tights, would be part, if not the whole, of the
show. The fair Leslie, who should be a model out of
skirts, was only a model in them. Some of the panto
people, though, are missing a chance.
Theodora, a circus rider, became Roman Empress, wife
of Justinian. May not Melba—but, no matter.
A peculiarity of Bernhardt is that distance does not
lend enchantment to the view. The closer one is the
better she looks.

playbill of the very first night of Melb. Royal, with the

A seedy mummer arrived recently in a W.A. town and
asked a local pastor to lend him a good coat in which to
make his first appearance. The astounded preacher
refused. Since then about 14 abusive letters have
appeared in the papers concerning his unchristian con-
duct, and the mummer has lived mainly upon the public
sympathy which he has aroused. Where the sympathy
comes in isn’t very clear. The man who lends his coat to
a seedy tragedian would probably have to preach next
Sunday in his shirt sleeves.
Tay Pay O’Connor speaks of Plain Biilolloway as a man
of the type that does credit to theatrical management.
Camilla Urso is at present “ resting " at Adelaide, and
meditates another Australian tour “on her own." Mme.
De Vere Sapio has gens ’ome to get £6O a week in London.

standing on her head is reflected upon the surface with
startling accuracy. The Bulletin, however, surmises
“ ®" a PP ie dom would prefer the real reversal of form
thstCMn® Swain used to exhibit to a mere shadow of a
shade, so to speak, where the lady’s skirts maintain their
normal position.

Madame Novarra (Mary Anderson) says she is happier
as a wife than she was as an actress. But all wives are
actresses.
Arthur Roberts, says The Bulletin’s correspondent,
has taken London by storm again with his “Claude
Duval. It is a farrago of absurdities, being, in fact,
Arthur and little more, except some pretty lyrics by
Bowyer. Of plot there is none, but Roberts, in a bewilder-
ing number of changes, keeps the ball rolling and the
audience in constant laughter. He is now a smart bar-
maid, with all her airs and graces; then a fashionable
lady palmist telling fortunes, and showing to the life the
vulgar ways of the parvenue ; anon he is a sharp up to all
the tricks of the ; now he is successfully playing
the confidence-trick upon a simple-minded old gentle-
man , now showing a “bit of life” to a young softy.
There is only one Arthur Roberts, and he was born, like
Penley, to make sedate people laugh. Yesterday people
were waiting in ths street at half-past 5 for the doors to
open at 7.
Dora Taylor, the “Little D’Argo ” of Coppin’s last
panto., though under ten years, saved a younger child’s
ife the other day at Healesville, Vic., by running l into a
creek and dragging the drowning playmate to land.
Better than losing diamonds.
The Music Hall, a London paper, awards a bottle of
strong drink as a prize to the sender of the following
funny story." A burnt cork comedian was lecturing
upon the characteristics ol “the bullock,” represented
by a red herring which he held in his paw. He asked
anyone in the audience to give him a watch and chain,
that he might tell the herring the time. A silver watch
and chain were handed up, whereupon the humorous
creature told the herring the time and walked away with
the goods. “He has got it (the watch and chain) and is
wearing »f. ' There’s the entire story. Think ol being
able to get a bottle of anything lor a narrative like that 1
