Richard Stewart, sen., will stage-manage Melb. Royal
panto.
Sophie Harris, the vivacious and shapely burlesque
artiste, now acting the name part in “Aladdin, Jun.," at
Chicago, appears to have traded off some wondrous yarns
on a Yankee interviewer. Sophie is a fine girl and the
memory of her legs will live in Australia when Parkes is a
vanished dream ; also, she once distinguished herself by
capturing a burglar single-handed in an Adelaide hotel.
But—she didn’t create all the contralto parts of Gilbert
and Sullivan’s operas in the Australasian capitals.
Melb. Lyric Club is palpably weakened in principals
since its first public performance, and sadly misses Ethel
Haydon since she turned professional and w-ent to
London. The Lyric, by the way, appears to have tired
very soon of its recently-announced intention of eschewing
opera in favor of sacred music.
If Mrs. Mellon’s words are true,
And she so shrunken grows,
When bringing spirits to the view,
That stockings leave her toes ;
If shapely ankles fade away
To merely skin and bone,
If calves to astral bodies pay
A Shylook kind ol loan:
Then let us pray that Violet V.,
A slave to tedium,
May never, never, seek to be
A ghostly medium.
For, what would Sydney’s feelings be I
How loud the wail would rise I
If we were never more to see
Those shapely legs of Vi.’s.
But, oh 1 the Joy of any spook
Become a form pro. tem.,
He’d feel a marquis or a dook,
Materialised in them.
Still, we prefer her on the stage
In shoes and stockings neat,
Than, victim to the latest rage,
Performing in a sheet.
It is recorded that during the Reign of
Terror the very children in the street were in the
habit of holding mock executions with mimic
guillotines. An enterprising Melb. toy-vendor
has recollected this fact and profited by it to the
extent of introducing a new and gruesome toy.
It takes the form of a small wooden gallows for
the hanging of dolls.

(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ I’ll do it, if I come off right.”
He kissed her roughly as he spoke ;
She held his shrinking fingers tight—
Poor victim of a “sporting bloke !”
Unmoved by sorrow for her plight.
He’ll marry if he “ comes off right.’’
She loved not wisely, but too well;
Who slowly fall repent in haste.
She took him for an honest “swell,”
And, dazzled by a “ bit of paste,”
Thought gaily of the wedding-bell—
A golden ring, and all were well.
Quite possibly he’d keep his word ;
He’s not a villain out-and-out,
He thought it looked “ the deadest bird ”
The Cup was over, bar the shout.
And, if he won, O thought absurd !
The surly dog might keep his word.
The days speed by, a cruel time !
Her fate depending on a race !
For passion is a spinster’s crime,
Maternity her black disgrace,
Says Mrs. Grundy’s creed sublime—
The “ creature ” has a cruel time.
Flash touts who sniff the early morn.
Write “Fizgig pulled up rather lame.”
An infant shortly to be born
Will bring one erring parent shame.
A girl’s last “ chance ” they view with scorn,
Those touts who tout the rosy dawn.
They’ll “bet their life that Fizgig goes,"
“ His leg can never bear the strain.”
Amid the wails of backers’ woes
We shall not hear one moan of pain.
Her bloke will curse, but Heaven knows
What Nell will do when Fizgig goes !
Melb.
E.F.
Traas. Ward has got a Bill through the
M.L. House of Representatives providing that,
in future, all local rates shall be levied on unim-
proved land-values only. Hitherto, municipal
rates have been levied on lands and buildings in-
discriminately, but if the latter are released
from liability, and taxation is levied on lands
alone, there will be a cold time for owners who
hold unimproved city property for a rise. When
the propiietor of a blank allotment has to pay as
much taxation as the man alongside who has
erected a five-storey stone building on his ground,
holding city lands for speculative purposes will
become eminently unprofitable. Also, the relief
to the owner of the big building will be so great
that it will serve as an inducement to erect fine
structures in place of weatherboard hovels, seeing
that the hovel will yield much less rent and yet
the tax will be the same. Probably, however, the
new idea will be decently interred in the Upper
House as usual.
Arthur Gallon, the Dibbs-appointed new
chum N.S.W. official historian, assures United
Service Institution that Churchill, first Duke of
Marlborough, was generous and of pure conver-
sation. Yet did Macaulay write :
Churchill was thrifty in his very vices, and levied ample
contributions on ladies enriched by the spoils of more
liberal lovers. On one occasion he was caught with the
Duchess of Cleveland, by the King, and was forced to
leap out of the window. She rewarded this hazardous
feat of gallantry with a present of £5OOO. With this sum
Hie prudent young hero instantly bought an annuity.
He loved lucre more than wine or women. At twenty he
made money of his beauty and his vigor ; at sixty lie
made money of his genius and his glory. He pocketed
pay in the names of men who had long been dead, &c., &c.
It is too late now to undeceive poor Mac.; but
Gallon should be the right man to embellish Aus-
tral’s Annals by whitewashing the progenitors of
Potts Point. The “best blood” and “the old
families ” of the senior province can safely trust
to the discretion of the gentleman who has the
bravery to praise for his morality, his generosity,
his loyalty and his high sense of honor, the soldier
who gained his first steps in promotion by
prostituting bis sister to the then Duke of York,
the Torn Jones of the Lady Bellastons of his time,
the skin-flint who had no God but money, and the
double-dyed traitor who played with Stuart and
Hanoverian until he had succeeded in getting the
highest price for his sword.
“ A Golden Shanty,” by Bulletin writers,
is one of the most striking products of nascent
Australian literature. Its contents are gems of
native lustre, glowing with no borrowed brilliancy.
Their breezy humour, their weird pathos, spring
alike from Southern soil, under a Southern sky,
and their promise is sui generis, unique and
unapproachable. The second edition now ready ;
post-free to any Australasian address, Is. 3d. ;
foreign, Is. fid.

FOUNTAINS IN PLAY.
Who doesn’t enjoy looking at a fountain in play ?
The bright water leaps into the air as though it
were a living, conscious thing. Then at the
summit of its arch it is shattered into spray by
the wind, or wafted to and fro like the skirts of a
dancing girl. And all the while the roar of its
rush and the tinkling melody of its fall fill the air.
This is water having a good time —water in high
spirits. What makes it jump, sing, and laugh in
that fashion? Wait a bit. If you please, we
will have the explanation later on. Perhaps you
know already ; perhaps not. It isn’t so easy to
know things from the bottom.
Under date of March 16th, 1892, a lady writes
that for twenty years she always felt low-spirited.
Now, what is meant by “ spirits ” in this sense is
a condition of the mind in respect of our being
cheerful, happy, enjoying our surroundings, taking
delight in all sorts of minor matters, and, in a word,
making the most of life ourselves and helping
others to make the most of it. Well then, it is
both a glorious and a profitable thing to be always
in good spirits ; I wish I could be myself.
But I can’t; anyhow, I’m not. Neither is
anybody. Yet why not ? What makes our hearts
sink down like a plummet, and then rise like a
cork ?
Before we answer let us read the rest of the
letter. The writer says; “For twenty years I
have suffered from liver complaint and indigestion.
I was constantly tired, weak, and languid. My
skin was a sallow colour, and I had great pain at
the back and shoulders.
“My appetite was poor, I had a bad taste in the
mouth, and much pain and weight at the chest
after eating. I was also frequently sick, throwing
up a sour, frothy fluid. I took different kinds of
medicine, but none of them gave me relief, and I
got weaker and weaker.
“In March, 1884, I became so bad that I called
a doctor, who attended me off and on for twelve
months. He gave me medicines, but I grew no
better, as nothing he prescribed seemed to reach
my complaint. About this time I heard of Mother
Seigel’s Syrup, and commenced taking it. In less
than a month all my pain and distress ceased, and
1 have since been in good health. I keep a bottle
of the Syrup in the house as a family medicine,
and by taking an occasional dose have needed no
doctor.
“I may mention that my aunt, who lives at
West Wycombe, Bucks, was afflicted with indiges-
tion and dyspepsia. She states that in her opinion
Mother Seigel’s Syrup has been the means of
saving her life. You are at liberty to use this
statement as you may see fit. Yours truly,
(Signed) Mrs. Harriett Cutler, Burton House,
33 Kenmure Road, Hackney, London.”
No philosopher has yet been able to say what
is the exact relation of the mind to the body.
This much, however, experience constantly assures
us of; that it is very intimate, and that the
condition of the mental faculties depends upon
that of the body. As birds sing when the sun
shines, and are dumb in the dark, so the spirits
and mental powers rise and express themselves
when we are in health, and are dull and torpid
when the body is under the weight of pain and
disease. And to know that is to know all we
require to act upon.
Everything, money, influence, and every form
of success depends so largely upon health that by
universal consent we don’t expect good service
from those who are ill.
And illness, nine times in ten, means precisely
what Mrs. Cutler suffered for such a long and
weary period—indigestion and dyspepsia—the
ailment cured (when the doctors are done with
their fruitless experiments) by Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup.
The fountains play only when the water is
forced powerfully through their pipes by gravity
or by machinery. So the spirits are free and
elastic only when the organs within us act without
hindrance or obstruction.

• “ If a man, when ho is in work, is without
the necessaries of life, what can he do when he is
out of work? His only course is to die of hunger.
In that case, a few words of pity will be uttered
over his corse. Let others be content with such a
fate. I could not be. I might have begged.
Deggary is cowardly and degrading. It is even
punished by law, which regards it as a crime. I
preferred to turn contrabandist, coiner of counter-
feit money, and murderer.”—Ravaohol, the
Anarchist.
Dear Bulletin, —Your par, rc the Sydney
man who planted his head in a hole, reminds one
of a distinguished ornament to Q. Civil Service,
who called himself “High Priest,” “Messiah,”
* n d “King of Israel,” “King of England,”
King of the Cannibal Islands,” and various
kings. His children were “Princes” and
‘Princesses,” and one day, finding the eldest
prince was not growing fast enough, he planted
him up to his neck in the garden, and was found
carefully watering the top of his head by a
morning visitor.—M. Rule.
Item on private railway ownership, from
the Tennessee (U.S. A.) Nation :
Railroad men going back to work at Little Rock are
asked to make oath that they have withdrawn from the
A.R.U. and that they will not join any labor organisations
NOR TAKE ANT PART IN POLITICS FOR FIVE TEARS ! So writes
one of the victims to rne. Laboring people, do you know
what this means ? This is a more damnable slavery than
chattel slavery. This is the result of private ownership
of railways. This is so much better than the “political
patronage "of Government ownership 1 No king of whom
history tells us wanted his subjects to take any part in
politics, either. And that is just why King George 111.
sent his Hessians to this country. Our forefathers
desired to take some part in politics, and he wanted to do
all that business himself. And now the railroads are com-
pelling American citizens to allow the monopolies only to
take part in politics 1 1 And this is free America 1
One of the passengers by the train
which was wrecked at Redfern Station had
a series of experiences that morning. When
he had crawled out of the ruins, and assured him-
self that he wasn’t dead or even injured, he got
into a cab, but before it had gone 30 yards the cab
collided with a tram-car, and the shock drove bis
head into the window-frame and nearly broke his
neck. Then he got along safely for half-a-mile or
so until his vehicle became mixed up with an
omnibus, and one of the ’bus horses got its legs
over the shaft of the cab, and it and the cab-horse
proceeded to kick the hansom to flinders. But
even then the passenger escaped, the shock land-
ing him in a sitting position in the road, and when
he had gathered himself up once more he decided
to walk the rest of the way. What happened to
him afterwards isn’t known. According to the
eternal fitness of things, he should have found his
office burnt down, and then gone home to find his
wife dead, and his dog stolen.

A recent London murder-case revealed a
terrible accumulation of preventible horrors.
Prisoner was a mad husband who slaughtered his
wife soon after the birth of her eighth child. His
madness showed itself first in the honeymoon,
when the poor wretch bit pieces out of his bride’s
face and attempted to remove her tongue by the
roots. He was sent to an asylum, and discharged
in due course as a “cured ” patient. Thereafter
he suffered periodical lapses into raging lunacy,
followed regularly by removals to other asylums,
and restorations to what the doctors called
“sanity.” The woman doesn’t seem to have
sought protection against the advances of her
lunatic spouse, and went on adding to the popula-
tion as though she considered her offspring worthy
hostages to fortune. But when the eighth little
stranger was drawing nigh to nativity she did
implore the authorities of Colney Hatch madhouse (
to refrain from setting her husband free. The !
man being “ cured ” as usual, however, they turned
him loose with his certificate, and the wife’s
murder was perpetrated under specially brutal
circumstances.

An American paper tells a story of Chinese
justice. A Yankee trader had roamed into the
interior; where, having quarrelled with a mandarin,
he was imprisoned. There were 15 other prisoners
in the gaol, and one day an official came along and
sentenced them all to death in a batch. After
that they lay around for a week or so waiting
events, till one day they were taken up to the top
of a very high tower, and told to walk across a
wooden bridge to a similar tower, 50ft. distant.
Below, at a depth of 150 ft., was a paved courtyard
studded with spikes, varying from 12in. to five
yards in length. As the procession of malefactors
marched across, a trap door opened and one man
fell through. Then it shut again, and as the rest
of the condemned tramped on they saw him turn
over and over in the air, with his arms and legs
and pigtail flying abroad, and finally land on a
tall spike which impaled him and almost tore
him asunder. The rest got over safely. Next day
they were marched across again; they all carefully
dodged the spot where the first victim fell through,
but another trap opened in another part of the
bridge and a second man dropped. After that
for three days the company did its daily march
without accident, but on the fourth day traps
opened in all directions and three men went down
together. So they did their promenade with
varying fortunes, till at last only the Yankee
trader was left. He walked across the bridge
alone daily for a week, but the traps remained
shut, and at the end of that time he was suddenly
scruffed out of the gaol and ordered to leave the
province instantly. Which he did. He is still
alive, and spends his spare time in trying to figure
out whether he was really sentenced to death and
reprieved, or whether the episode was merely a
humorousMongolianwayof impressing on him that
foreigners aren’t wanted in the Chinese interior.

TWENTY POUNDS
OB
Twenty Pence,
You’ve twenty pounds to spend—or twenty
pence, if you like. Your object is to get all you
require for the money you have, and you do not
desire even to contemplate the possibility of being
called upon for more. If you BUY OF US you
will get all you determined to secure, and have a
bit left for something else. Also, you will get the
best of everything—the goods of standard manu-
facturers. If you BUY ELSEWHERE your
TWENTY POUNDS, or your TWENTY
SHILLINGS, as the ease may be, will certainly
not go so far as you thought; you will either have
to SINK THE QUALITY, and be content with
inferior goods, or
Shell out more Cash .
Will that suit you ? We trow not. You’re no
fool. You’ve been wandering up and down this
blessed world for years and know your book.
One may say, “This is only assertion; where’s
your proof?” We reply that even as the quality
of that popular mixture of various compounds and
substances known as a pudding is proved by its
effect upon the constitution of the person who
assimilates it, so are we prepared with sound and
lucid demonstration. We say—Use your eyes,
exercise your judgment, criticise and compare.
Set your mental machinery to work, and convey
to your mind, through the medium of your organs
of visions, and with the aid of those glorious
reasoning powers with which you have been en-
dowed, the impressions that will be yours, and
you’ll find engraved upon your heart of hearts the
name of

To be had of all RETAIL DEALERS in
Australasia.
In a recent issue of the British Australasian
appear share quotations of 45 British loan, mortgage,
and finance companies doing business in Australasia, and
paying away the profits made there to English share-
holders. This only represents the fringe of the great
army of vultures, and takes no account of banks, private
investors, and syndicates and companies not officially
quoted. Truly the carcase is very lean these times. and
the birds of prey, who are called ‘‘foreign capital,” are
so many that there is hardly standing-room tor them
upon it. , ,
A Southern 600-percenter, the other day, ad-
vanced a large sum to one entitled to money on the
Continent, and took the usual power of attorney to collect
same and adjust accounts. However, the borrower went
to Europe per same steamer as the documents, and by
getting off at the first European port, got ahead of the
papers, which had to go round by way of London. He
collected all that was due, and left for other climes,
having thus supplemented his birthright by the Austra-
lian contribution.

A certain liberal money-lender, asked, the other
day what his claim was against moneys due to a certain
fast youth, replied £lOOO. When asked to give a state-
ment to the solicitors he made out a claim for £2300, and
submitted the necessary documents to cover it. On the
solicitors showing fight and issuing a writ, it was settled
for £5OO, the original advance being £llO.
* • * *
Mr. Yestikoff, ere his composition by arrange-
ment, did his old chum a good turn by giving him a lease
of a splendid suite of offices in the best part of the city on
a seven-years’ lease, and handing him a receipt for rent
for the full term. The mortgagees consented, thinking it
improved their security and rendered their interest more
secure to have such a lease. Of course the receipt was
not mentioned. When the composition by arrangement
had been completed some months, “ Old Chum ” was
asked for the rent and produced the receipt. As Mr.
Vestikoff was then on a continental tour, they had to fall
back on severe thoughts and bad language.
• * * •
A certain Northern importer a year or two ago
took a partner, who introduced the large amount of £l5O
capital; their London correspondent was advised that the
new partner had introduced £5OOO. Some time after, the
partners quarrelled, and the last-comer withdrew his £l5O
and left. The original then wrote -’Orae and got his bills
renewed on the understanding that he had paid out his
partner with cash. After a time he got too deep in the
mire, and one of the London people came out, made him
insolvent, and made a microscopic search for the mythical
£5OOO. The lesson cost the London house £13,000.

Melb. tea-dealers do not now advertise for can-
vassers. Hundreds used to reply to the “ad.,” receive
samples, varying from 21bs. to lOlbs. in weight, but
few orders did the dealers get. Half the people in the
suburbs used to get their tea on the “ never.”

At a recent meeting of directors of a certain
mil Ing co. it was pointed out to the chairman that he
was incapacitated from presiding as his calls were unpaid.
Apologising for the inadvertence, he called for a cheque,
filled it up for the amount, and presided at the meeting.
Cheque was paid in and returned marked “No Account.”
Mr. Stiffun pouched a guinea as his director’s fee lor
attending the meeting. Was it false pretences?

A big haul awaits the Treasurer who passes a
bill ordering all banks to pay to Govt, all balances not
operated on for six years. Also, all balances and un-
o dined divs. of estates in the hands of trustees after six
months from declaration of last div., or, if insufficient
for declaration of div., six months after adjustment of
accounts. Pawnbrokers are compelled by law after the
expiration of the agreed or legal term of the loan to sell
by auction all pledges, and if the borrower turns up he
can claim any balance after adjustment of accounts, if
the claim be made within 12 months. Now, instead of
allowing Moses to sell to his own friends by his own
auctioneer and retain the balances why not have the
goods sold by a State auctioneer and get the balances paid
into the consolidated revenue at once? “ Oh, Rebecca,
such a law 1 Mine profits all gone !”
• * * •
Mrs. Jessie Urquharb considered herself lucky
in leasing Mr, John Wesley Yowlraan’s semi-detached
cottage, as he had lived there himself, before moving next
door, and, being aretired plumber, had fitted it most com-
fortably with hot andoold baths, gas, wash-house, &c. When
she was there her gas bills were phenomenal ; the strictest
economy failed to reduce them. One evening she men-
t oned this to her nephew, a godless cyclist, who held
Mr. Yowlman in scorn. He turned the gas off at the
meter, and was not much surprised to observe the lights
go out in the holy Yowlman’s house. A plumber was
brought, and the mystery solved. St. Yowlman is to pay
two-thirds of all past gas-bills, has his little pipe cut off,
and defrays all expenses. Save the damage done to Mrs.
Urquhart’s faith in local-preachers full reparation has
been made.

Offices are advertised rent free, “best city posi-
tion ”in Melbourne. In scores of instances it has really
been a case of free rent during the last two years, for the
rent has been allowed (especially in connection with some
of the big buildings) to accumulate without the remotest
chance of its recovery.

A cattle-dealing client, the other day, turned
into a solicitor’s office, stated the case against him to the
clerk, and asked what would be a good defence. The
elerk told him. The client said, “ I can get that sworn
to,” did so—and the fiction won the case.

Dear Bulletin, —The law laid down in a par.
in your last issue as to the effect of bankruptcy on_ a dis-
tress for rent is clearly wrong, so far as N.S.W. is con-
cerned. You say: “ A landlord can levy on any time-
payment furniture, but if the debtor files his schedule the
assets pass to the assignee, the time-payment man
resumes possession under his letting and hiring agree-
ment, and the landlord stands a preferential creditor for
three months’ rent—to his sorrow, as a rule.” Now, I’ll
show you where you are wrong. If a landlord levies a
distress for furniture under a bill of sale or hiring agree-
ment and the tenant becomes bankrupt, the distress is not
suspended, because the Bankruptcy Act only suspends
distress “as against the goods of the bankrupt.” When
Ikey Mo goes into possession under his bill of sale or
hiring agreement, as he usually does direolly he knows
the landlord is “ on the job ” —the furniture ceases from
that moment, in the eye of the law, to be in the posses-
sion of the tenant, and, therefore, instead of escaping
from the distress, it may be sold by the landlord as “ the
furniture of a stranger.” There can be no question about
the law as I have stated it, as it is based on the Privy
Council judgment in Railtou v. Wood, ii. N.S.W. Law
Reports, p. 474.— Pa010 Macari. [This seems conclusive
as regards N.S.W. But is it good law in Victoria?—Ed. B.j
Apropos of the above, The Bulletin is told of
a very simple way by which anyone who borrows money
on a bill of sale may, under the law of N.S.W., make
things extremely awkward for the lender—namely, by
the borrower giving notice to the lender that he has
“ suspended payment.” This is “an act of bankruptcy,”
and if the lender takes possession after receipt of this
notice, and the borrower becomes bankrupt within six
months, the bill of sale will be upset and the lender com-
pelled to pay over the value of the goods seized to the
official assignee—with a good lump of costs added. Under
the N.S.W. Bankruptcy Act, the holder of a bill of sale is
always entirely at the mercy of his mortgagor, who can
commit an act of bankruptcy—which will render the bill
of sale void—at any moment. Registration of a bill of
sale is of no avail unless the mortgagee gets into posses-
sion before he receives notice of “ an act of bankruptcy.”

Sydney retail shopkeepers complain bitterly of
the competition they endure from the warehouses, who
are killing the goose that lays the golden egg. When
Simpkins, of the Civil service or the bank, wants a
macintosh, or a pipe, or a brooch for his best girl, he
knows a friend in some big establishment, who gets it for
him at a discount on wholesale price. The same with
tailoring ; cloth is bought in like manner, and given to
the tailor to make up. A well-known “snip” states that
quite half the suits he cuts now-a-days are made from
material supplied by customers, who deprive him of his
profit on the cloth.

In answer to a correspondent, J.L. (Kurnalpi) :
—The discovery (250 8.C.) over which Archimedes cried
“ Eureka ” is that which enables the gold contents of
quartz to be very closely estimated without dollying or
fracturing the specimen. It is a question of specific
gravities. The specific gravity of any substance is its
weight compared bulk for bulk with water. If native
gold were always of the same fineness, and quartz always
of the same composition, the problem would be simpler
than it is. Native gold, generally speaking, is 17J to 18i
times heavier than water, and quartz is generally figured
at 2'65 times heavier than water. In practice, of course,
the quality of the gold and quartz in a specimen could be
estimated by experiment with other specimens found in
the immediate vicinity. To obtain the specific gravity of
the specimen : weigh specimen in usual manner, then
weigh with specimen immersed in water. The “ support”
of the water will cause a certain loss in weight. This
“ loss ” is equal to the weight of the water displaced. The
“loss” divided into the weight in air gives the specific
gravity. It must be remembered that the “specific
gravity” of a substance is not so many ounces. _ The
assay fineness of a sovereign is not its weight, nor is the
“ water” of a diamond its weight. Specific gravity is a
measurement of comparative heaviness, just as the assay
is a measure of purity. The size of a man’s boot is 8.
8 what? “Just 8,” the man would say. NotB inches, or
8 ounces, but 8 dimensions according to a none too simple
standard. Similarly, *hen we speak of the specific gravity
of gold we mean that it has, say, 18 dimensions of heavi-
ness. A formula for working out the gold contents of a
quartz specimen is here given
Gold contents ... =* '
Weight of specimen = m
Specific gravity of do = n -
Specific gravity of gold = r
Specific gravity of quartz = I .
(n— »)r
Then * = tn x
(*•—») n
Let us break this jargon down by taking an imaginary
specimen. Its weight shall be 100 troy : its specific gravity
shall be considered as 10. For a rough calculation weoan
put down the specific gravity of gold and quart* as 18 and
23 respectively. Then our calculation wall read:—
(10—2§) xlB
Gold contents =■ 100 x
(18-23) x 10
100 x 7J x 18 3900
15J x 10 40
■m nearly 86oz. 2dwt.
The value of which, at £3 15s. per oz., would be £322 17s.
6d. Tub Bulletin wishes Kurnalpi many such.

One Melbourne pen-lady made .£4OOO in mining
during the boom, and lost the bigger part of it in trams

Civil servants have a big say in the ownership
of the Queen of the Ranges, Bright (N.S.W.), which
owns a hill (more or less) full of gold, two shoots, three
tram-lines, and a battery. Mostly surface-stone is picked
up on top of the hill and sent down to the battery at the
bottom; and, as shovelling isn't allowed, the miners
darting here and there after loose lumps of quartz look
just as if they were potato-racing. Thanks to cheap
working, the co. hopes to make 3dwts. pay.

Dear Bulletin, —What has become of the cele-
brated Southern Cross pearl, which, at the time of its
discovery at Roeburne, W.A., was valued at £10,000? It
was found in 1874 by the crew of “ Shiner ” Kelly’s
schooner, the Ethel, and when “Shiner” saw it, taking
it for a heaven-wrought miracle, he buried it, and did not
unearth it until the early eighties. It consisted of nine
pearls naturally grown together so as to form a perfect
cross, and was, perhaps, the most unique natural jewel
extant.—Fours, Robbcrnb.
» » * *
Unless Q. Government is careful, the pearl-
shelling industry will soon be a thing of the past. Pearl-
shellers are very properly forbidden to export shell of less
than six inches diameter. But private capitalistic com-
panies remove this very shell to their own private cultiva-
tion-grounds, thereby driving the small man to the wall.
The removal of all shell under the specific diameter should
be strictly regulated.
Ex-Premier Salisbury says that “ only
physical force will overthrow the House of Lords.”
Which is obviously mere Tory vapouring. The
House of Lords will vanish like a spook a,t day-
break when a bill is passed in the usual quiet way
through both Houses, and assented to in the usual
way by the monarch, declaring that the Upper
Chamber shall exist no longer. The Commons
will pass it when there is a big enough Democratic
majority to support a Democratic Government,
and both these will come. The monarch will
assent to it when the Democratic majority is
sufficiently large to show him or her that the only
safe proceeding is to assent. And the Lords will
concur peacefully when the Democratic Govern-
ment, backed by its overwhelming majority in the
Commons and its overawed monarch, creates five
or six hundred new peers, or as many more as may
be necessary, all pledged to vote for abolition.
Probably, however, there will be no need to
create any new peers. When the choice lies
between going quietly with their dignity unim-
paired, or going in any case and having the peer-
age so diluted that dukes would be two a penny
and marquises eight for sixpence, it is ten chances
to one that the Lords will go quietly.
Perhaps the most pertinent query of all
the host which have followed the recent Sydney
spook-eruption is “ What do spooks live on ?” If
they can eat chocolate-creams they must have a
stomach to put them in, and then what else do
they put in their stomachs when chocolate-creams
are “off?” This question, however, was answered
once and for all in the heyday of spiritualism 40
years ago—when spirits first, at Home’s command,
arose, from out the azure main. Spooks cannot
carry weight through the blue ethereal, and their
sole sustenance, apart from casual confectionery,
is that subtly invigorating compound, the essence
of good spirits, Marshall’s Tonic Nervine. Sold
only by Marshall Bros., Park-street, Sydney, and
Pitt and Regent streets, Redfern.
Russia: An Ode.
Out of hell a word comes hissing, dark as doom,
Fierce as fire, and foul as plague-polluted gloom ;
Out of hell wherein the sinless damned endure
More than ever sin conceived of pains impure,
More than ever ground men’s living souls to dust;
Worse than madness ever dreamed of murderous
Since the world’s wail first went up from lands
and seas
Ears have heard nob, tongues have told not things
Dante, led by"love’s and hate’s accordant spell
Down the deepest and the loathliest ways ot hell,
Where beyond the brook of blood the ram was
fire, . , ,
Where the scalps were masked with dung more
deep than mire, . ,
Saw nob, where the filth was foulest, and the night
Darkest, deaths whose fiends could match the
Muscovite. . . .
Set beside this truth, his deadliest vision seems
Pale and pure and painless as a virgin’s dreams.
Maidens dead beneath the clasping lash, and
wives
Rent with deadlier pangs than death —for shame
survives.
Naked, mad, starved, scourged, spurned, frozen,
fallen, deflowered,
Souls and bodies as by fangs of beast devoured,
Filth of raging crime and shame that crime
enjoys, . . . ...
Age made one with youth in torture, girls with
boys,
These, and worse, if aught be worse than these
things are,
Prove thee regent, Russia —praise thy mercy,
Czar.
Help is none in heaven: hope sees no gentler star:
Earth is hell, and hell bows down before the Czar.
All its monstrous, murderous, lecherous births
acclaim _
Him whose empire lives to match its fiery flame.
Nay, perchance at sight or sense of deeds here
done,
Here where men may lift up eyes to greet the sun,
Hell recoils heart-stricken ; horror worse than hell
Darkens earth and sickens heaven; life knows the
spell, . . .
Shudders, quails and sinks —or, filled with fierier
breath,
Rises red in arms devised of darkling death.
Pity mad with passion, anguish mad with shame,
Call aloud on justice by her darker name ;
Love grows hate for love’s sake; life takes death
for guide. .
Night hath none but one red star—Tyrannicide,
God or man, be swift; hope sickens with delay
Smite, and send him howling down his father’s
way I

of the Queensland Never Never is that the people

a low morality. Which recalls a certain famous
Scotch-minister story that nobody ever dares to
tell until the ladies have left the dinner-table.
Dear Bulletin, —It’s no news that the
goburra eats young birds. All the kingfishers
will eat anything they can master, and I’ve known
“ Jack ’’—the biggest of the tribe—get as far as a
duckling. It’s nob for harmlessness he’s “ pro-
tected,” but partly as a snake-slayer and partly as
a “representative Australian.” — Halcto.
Dear Bulletin, —“ L.” writes as one who
knows about the havoc committed by the kooka-
burra. I’ve often seen them demolish clutches of
chickens, and have, in defence of said clutches,
been compelled to break the law and slay the
laughing-jackass. Like all the kingfisher tribe,
they are remorselessly cruel and often kill for no
other reason than lust of blood.— Wurrt M.
Editor Bulletin, — “L” discounts the
popular virtues of our sedate countryman, the
laughing jackass. It is true the jackass preys nob
only on young, but also upon all small birds.
Perhaps not the sombre-plumed kukuburra, bub
the bright-winged bird I have often seen dine off
small full-grown birds in Central Queensland.
City men tell yarns of the laughing jackass killing
large snakes, bub bushmen say that he can only
kill very small ones, simply for food, as he eats
almost any small living thing. With the crow and
the cockatoo he shows creditable reasoning powers.
If one is surface gold-prospecting a few hours in
the ranges a passing kukuburra is sure to alight on
some convenient bough near the trench, and after
some jubilant hops he settles down to wait. A
few tense elevations of his tail, from time to time,
remind the prospector working below that the bird
is keeping a hold on his balance, ready for a dive
at the expected worm. The smallest moving worm
does nob escape his fixed gaze and aching void.
The jackass is as good as the birds he sometimes
destroys, so let him live and laugh.— Jaokbird.

Par away from Australia is the Thames, a part of which
is called the Isis, while Childers is not far away nor

the two young Bells (Sir Joshua’s sons) are to compete.
The writer receives his mails, amongst which are The
Bulletin, Maryborough Chronicle, Gympie Miner, and
Times, and the Bundaberg Mail, all sent on by friends.
A very old Church of England clergyman asks to see the
papers and retires. Interested in the smaller papers he
asks the writer about “gold finding ” at Gympie and
sugar at Bundaberg. Then he takes his little skiff and
rowingusto the nearest bank avoidingthe necessary lasher,
he produces The Bulletin and says that he has read it
through “My boy,” says the old man, “is that a lead-
ing paper in your new country ?” Our reply is, “ Yes, it
is the leading paper. “Then,” replied the old man,
“ God save Australia, for it has the hand of Satan upon
It." We rowed to Henley, and saw the Bells and their
crew win. Advance Australia 1 in spite of Satan.
But then that very old Church of England clergy-
man did read The Bulletin “ through ” after all-
The “I Was Dreaming” waltz, by Leon
Caron, was played by the Australian Military
Band at Flemington on Derby and Cup days—
still another proof of its wide popularity. It is
the ambition of every composer to have his little
piece played at Flemington during the racing
carnival, bub very few indeed arrive at that
blessed consummation. It is easier to get through
the eye of a needle than to satisfy the require-
ments of Zelman, who regards his band as the
hub of the universe.

THE KING OF GAMES.
Life is like a game of cricket—
Keen, uncertain, quickly o’er;
Some gain glory at life’s wicket—
Some have ne’er a chance to score.
Fortune speedily may bowl you,
Joying all your hopes to foil;
What is left, then, to console you?
Nothing—save St. Jacobs Oil.
Man is like a cricket-ball —
When he’s young he’s round and red ;
When he’s old, no use at all—
All his form and color fled.
Man is like a cricket-pad—
Tightly clasp’d but soon untied ;
So a friend, when times are bad,
Oft is lightly thrown aside.
Man is like a cricket-bat.
Like as brother unto brother ;
Men, like bats, need oil—and that
Is St. Jacobs, and no other.
Man is like a cricket-pitch—
’Neath the sun he lasts not long,
Though when young he’s verdant—which
Marks the ending of my song.

Dawn in the Bush.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The Sun had opened his gates, and cast
A wealth of light on the night-bound world.
The Night fled onward to where at last
She might reign with the black of her flags
unfurled.
Red lights to eastward in grey lights lay,
And a thousand song-birds sang in the day.
The Night fled onward, but swifter feet
Than Night’s were fleet to o’ercome her flight.
The Dawn had caught her with one wide beat
Of the wings that scattered her shades with light.
With reddened footsteps, and eyes of fire,
Came the Primal Light of the world’s desire.
The wattles trembled, and, trembling, sighed
Their sharp, sweet breaths on a wind of morn,
And swayed their heads, that the sun might hide
In their gleaming bodies the gold of dawn.
The tender sadness of early day
Lit up with the sheen of that wattled way.
The little gums, where the paddocks lean
To the withered creeks, made a line of red,
As light stole on through the tall trees green.
Till it burnt the tops of each lithe young head.
The tall gums, glimmering grey and brown,
Were a shadowy band in a silver crown.
Away to east, on the creek’s far side,
Beyond the shade of the short shea-oaks,
Great flannel-daisies broke white and wild
Through the grey-green grass of the paddock
slopes,
And caught the sun on their velvet heads,
And carried its gleams to their sandy beds.
The sarsaparilla’s slim clinging vine
Crept warm and red, over earth and shale.
An early snake shed a moving line
Of reflected light on its sunlit trail.
The tiny dews of the overnight
Set a trembling mirror to catch all light.
A wild clematis made rank and rich,
With starry blossoms a myrtle grim,
Gone black long since in a lightning’s stitch,
But revived in the white of this wild-flower’s
whim,
And sweet once more with the perfect breath
Of the pallid petals that graced its death.
For one brief breath-time of cool delight
The blue-gums murmured their wind-won tune;
Too soon, too soon would the sun-rays slight
With the glare of the tierce Australian noon,
And quell their crooning, and quench the dew
From their leaves, and wither with fire their blue.
The Sun went on up his old-world groove.
And lazy lizards began to stir
Prom their stone-retreats, and the birds to move,
With a twitter and sweep, through the early air.
Red lights to eastward from grey lights grew,
And the Bush was glad that the day was new.
Sydney.
M. LOUISE MACK.
The gaols of N.S.W., especially of Sydney,
are full—a sure sign of the times.
Dear Bulletin, —Ross Cox’s “course of
nature” was merely an adroit combination of two
previously-existing accounts of such an experi-
ment. One—a hoax, itself—represents the inves-
tigation as conducted for James L, with result
that the primordial language was (of course!) found
to be Hebrew. Of the other experiment—actually
made by Ptolemy Psammetichos —the resulting
first word was the Lydian “bek-bek” —“bread,”
shrewdly suspected, however, by Ptolemy himself
to be no more than imitation of the voice of the
goat that suckled the youngsters.—A.M.
Dear Bulletin, —The dogmatic “Sugar
Doodle” is shaky in facts and hasty in deduc-
tions. “ S.D.” is a ped., and has flat feet—there-
fore all peds. have flat feet, and the flat foot is the
normal foot! Some famous runners have had
notably high insteps, and that feet differ normally
as much as noses is a fact amply recognised in the
classic sculptures cited by “S.D.” The “pitch ”
of the metatarsal arch varies both individually
and racially, and, while the boot-modified foot
often combines flat sole with constricted toes, the
never-booted extremity frequently joins arched
instep with well-spread toes. The Papuan peoples,
for instance, are much flatter-footed than the
Malaysian, and the highest in.-teps I have ever
seen were among the Dyak women—shoeless
descendants of shoeless generations.— Macassar.

Dear Bulletin, —Piloting Chinese over
the N.S.W. border has become a regular industry
here (Corowa). Not fewer than 10 or 12 a week
have crossed from Victoria for the last 12 months,
and never a word of poll-tax. A local police-
officer states that he could fill the gaol with
evaders and their allies every week if required.
Much of the clearing and other work throughout
Riverina and elsewhere in N.S.W. is done by
Chinese —even to shearing and wool-scouring.
Chinese now monopolise the Vic. cabinet-making
industry; they have a good grip of the wine
industry ; and in a few years N.S.W. bush labor
will be their province exclusively if they oome in
unchecked—for there are always men greedy
enough to employ them. There is a large Chinese
camp at Wahgunyah with six stores, and most of
the storekeepers take contracts for scrubbing and
clearing in N.S. Wales. They are allowed to
bring Chinese over the border, under promise to
return them; but the promise is never kept. —
White Worker.

Mr. B. R. Minns, the Sydney artist, will
probably continue his studies of Australian
aborigines, which studies will remain as admirable
records of a rapidly-declining race. One of the
most interesting collections of pictures in the
world is the Gatlin collection, bought by the U.S.
Government. Gatlin was a lawyer who forsook
hi, profession and went on. beyond the fringe of
civilisation among the Indian tribes, painting
portraits of 3000 chiefs and people. However,
Gatlin had the advantage of starting to work
before the blessings of civilisation had begun to
wipe the American aboriginal out.

Editor Bulletin,— “ Unreforrned Man ’’indulges in
much argumentum adhonunem reasoning and many
wild predictions. His differentiation of the types that
advocate woman-enfranchisement may be just (at least as
to the persistent public advocates). Nevertheless, the
physical defects or sinister notions of those who affect to
be the mouthpieces of intelligent feminity are as potent
an argument against woman-suffrage as the gyrations of a
Gaiety girl are against the Copernican system. Whenever
it requires no prophetic vision to foresee that any political
movement is destined to succeed, under the banner of
that movement will be found knavish notoriety-hunters
and wary political-axe-grinders; just as urchins, under
the pretence of giving a helping shove to the fire-engine
of a volunteer brigade, get a lift to the scene of the
pyrotechny and enjoy simultaneously the satisfaction
of having assisted in a good cause. Now, if it be
just to exclude from the franchise those “she-
males ” who have demonstrated their incapacity
to bear and also those who never had an oppor-
tunity of doing so, why not also the impotent male
who has delayed getting “reformed” till too late?
(for scientifically he is as great an exception to Nature’s
grand dichotomy as the aforesaid shemale). “ Unre-
formed" puts it: “No representation unless taxed.’’
All right! When the incidence of taxation is shifted to
land, income and shares—as it assuredly will be—then the
only voters will be “proputty” (and the incidence will
then rapidly revert). This prophecy of the destruction of
the marriage-tie is precisely analogous to Fatmau’s pre-
diction of blood-deluged streets and bomb-shattered piles
that ware to ensue on manhood suffrage. No! trust to
